THE RED PAGE
J. M. Synge
The Celtic Renaissance, the most interest-
ing literary development of recent years, is
responsible for some notable additions to
English poetry, and for the beginnings of a
dramatic literature which promised to be
even more notable. W. B. Yeats would, no
doubt, have written poetry in any case ; but
it is very likely that we would not have had
the Plays of J. M. Synge but for the great
revival of the national spirit of Ireland,
which has manifested itself in literature.
The revival began with the unlocking of the
storehouses of ancient Celtic poetry, legend,
and history, which found fit and enthusi-
astic interpreters in Yeats and others. But
Yeats saw that the poetry he and his com-
patriots were writing was too poetical to help
much in keeping the national spirit alive,
and in order to get nearer to the people, he
established an Irish theatre, wrote plays for
it, conducted a propagandist magazine,
and stimulated all with whom he came in
contact.
In 1899 Yeats met John Millington Synge
in Paris, and found him reading Racine and
writing literary verse full of “ images re-
flected from mirror to mirror.” Synge had been
educated at Trinity College, Dublin, and
travelled the continent for some years ; and
at that time he was aimless and despondent.
Yeats prescribed, as medicine for body and
soul, a visit to the lonely, wind-swept Aran
Islands, on the west coast of Ireland.
Synge paid annual visits thenceforward, and
lived for some time amongst the simple
folk there. He also became intimately ac-
quainted with the West of Ireland peasantry
and fishermen. The result of Yeats’ stimulus
was that Synge studied these primitive
people and their picturesque language. He
wrote a book on the Aran Islands, and a
nujnber of plays for the Irish Theatre which
are singularly unlike anything else in modern
dramatic literature.
The Shadow of the Glen was produced in
1903, and Riders to the Sea in 1904. In the
first, an odd old farmer man pretends to be
dead. While his wife is preparing for the
wake a tramp drops in for shelter, and later
on a young neighbor calls who explains to
the “ widow ” the advantages of joining her
lot to his. She talks freely about the
corpse ” lying in the same room, until
himself ” jumps up and orders the wife out
of the house. The tramp gallantly accom-
panies her ; the thrifty neighbor remains to
dnnk whisky with the “corpse.” The
situation would be farcical but for the in-
tense air of reality about it, and the simple
beauty of the talk. The Tinker’s Wedding ,
written about the same time, is pure farce ;
but realistic in its portrayal of the wild,
harum-scarum characters, and their auda-
cious phraseology.
Riders to the Sea stands apart, an ex-
tremely moving one-act tragedy, treated
with great restraint. The dialogue is not so
picturesque as in the other plays, but has a
severe beauty of its own, and every touch of
commonplace only serves to heighten the
tragedy. The pathos of the old mother’s
entreaty to her last surviving son, Bartley,
that he should not go to the horse-fair by
sea is far removed from the theatrical-
pathetic :
If it was a hundred horses, or a thousand horses,
you had itself, what is the price of a thousand
horses against a son where there is one son only ?
Her daughter tells her that:
It’s the life of a young man to be going on the
sea, and who would listen to an old woman with
one thing and she saying it over ?
The mother knew what the end would be :
Bartley will be lost now, and let you call lin
itamon and make me a good coffin out of the white
boards, for I won’t live after them. I’ve had a
husband and a husband’s father and six sons in
this house—six fine men, though it was a hard
birth I had with every one of them, and they
coming to the world—and some of them were
found and some of them were not found, but
they’re gone now the lot of them. . . .
Bartley meets the expected end, and the
mother consoles herself with the thought
that he will have a fine coffin and a deep
grave. “ What more can we want than that ?
No man at all can be living for ever, and we
must be satisfied.”
In The Well of the Saints (1905), Synge
returned to the humorous treatment of the
peasantry, the fun depending upon the dis-
illusion ment of an old wizened and blind couple
when their sight is miraculously restored.
It is not, however, dramatic enough for three
acts. The saint is convincing ; but to save
trouble, the scene is conveniently placed in
“ some lonely mountainous district on east of
Ireland one or more centuries ago.”
The Playboy of the Western World (1907)
brought Synge more notoriety than all the
rest of his work, as its characters are open
admirers of the law-breaker, and the satire
hurt a good many. There was a fine row in
the Abbey Theatre on the first night, and a
number of Dublin papers condemned the play
as a wild calumny. Its slight plot resembles
that of The Shadow of the Glen ; but it is
more dramatic and more humorous than its
predecessors. The imaginative quality of
the dialogue may be seen in these quota-
tions :
Amn’t I after seeing the love light of the star
of knowledge shining from her brow, and hearing
words would put jou thinking on the holy Brigid
speaking to the infant saints, and now she’ll be
turning again, and speaking hard words to me,
like an old woman with a spavindy ass she’d
have, urging on a hill.
There’s torment in the splendor of her like, and
she’s a girl any moon of midnight would take
pride to meet, facing southwards on the heaths of
Keel. But what did I want crawling forward to
scorch my understanding at her darning brow ?
If the mitred bishops seen you that time, they’d
be the like of the holy prophets, I’m thinking, do
be straining the bars of Paradise, to lay eyes on
the Lady Helen of Troy, and she abroad, pacing
back and forward, with a nosegay in her golden
shawl.
It’s little you’ll think if my love’s a poacher’s,
or an earl’s itself, when you’ll feel my two hands
stretched around you, and I squeezing kisses on
your puckered lips, till I’d feel a kind of pity for
the Lord God is all ages sitting lonesome in his
golden chair.
When Synge published The Playboy he
prefaced it with a note on his own principle
and methods of dramatic writing :
When I was writing The Shadow of the Glen
I got more aid than any learning could have given
me from a chink in the floor of the old Wicklow
house where I was staying that let me hear what
was being said by the servant girls in the kitchen.
... On the stage one must have reality, and
one must have joy. ... In a good play every
speech should be as fully flavored as a nut or
apple, and such speeches cannot be written by
anyone who works among people who have shut
their lips on poetry.
Synge had shown himself capable of carrying
out his own principle, and his death in
Dublin, last March, at the early age of 38,
cut short the career of one who might have
done great things for the drama. It is
stated that he left behind him a book of
poems and an unfinished play— The Sorrows
of Deirdre.
Another Irishman, Herbert Trench, who
has written the best modern version
of Deirdre’s story so far published, has
arranged, with good financial backing, to
start a repertory theatre in London.
Trench’s theatre will be experimental, and
not a commercial speculation. But at the
same time an enterprising American manager,
Charles Frohman, intends to start a similar
repertory theatre in London and make it
pay. The practical Frohman has told the
young dramatists that they should quit
“ theatrical ” dramas, learn the conventions
of the stage only to disregard them, and
write about the life they know. So far, the
Stage Society has been the only avenue by
which the advanced dramatist could approach
the public. The revolutionary Frohman
theatre, which is to open next February,
has already secured four of the pioneer play-
wrights : Bernard Shaw, J. M. Barrie,
Granville Barker, and Galsworthy. Shaw
and Barrie we know ; but of Barker all we
know is that he is the fortunate husband of
Lillah McCarthy and the author of a play
called Waste, which shares the distinction of
being censored with Shaw’s Showing up of
Blanco Posnet. Barker’s plays are to be pub-
lished shortly; but three of Galsworthy’s
plays— The Silver Box, Joy, and Strife —have
just reached us, published by Duckworth and
Co., in one volume ( per Sydney Book Club).
John Galsworthy
In two of his novels, A Man of Property
and A Country House, Galsworthy exhibited
typical hidebound British families for in-
spection, and in Fraternity he delicately
contrasted a middle-class city family with a
group in the slums. A Commentary, pub-
lished last year, consisted of a number of
sociological studies by a literary artist. He
had. seen life with clear, steady eyes, and
depicted it as he saw it. The force of
the contrast between the subjects made
verbal comment unnecessary. The' same
method has been adopted in his Plays, but
more eflectively. Like Synge,. Galsworthy
has followed the advice of Hamlet, and
held a mirror,up to nature, “to show.... the
very age and body of the time his form and
pressure.” The device of getting your audi-
ence to “look on this picture and on this”
could not have been more skilfully utilised.
The effect is far greater than that of a cart-
load of lectures ; and, no doubt, for this
reason, that acute critic, J. M. Robertson,
included The Silver Box in his Courses of
Beading on sociology before the play
was published. The classes in The Silver
Box are represented by a wealthy Liberal
M.P., John Barthwick, his wife and son,
and their charwoman (Mrs. Jones) and
her husband, a hollow-cheeked member of
the unemployed. Barthwick, jun., arrives
home late, disguised in liquor. He had been
“ on the tiles,” where he met an unknown
lady who had chaffed him, and to score off
her he had decamped with her purse. While
feeling on the wrong side of the door for the
keyhole, Jones comes to his assistance.
Jones had had a few, and was hanging about
to see his wife in order to get more. Young
Barthwick invites him in, and asks him to
take a drink. “ Take a good long drink-
take a cigarette—take any tiling you like,”
and then subsides into sleep on the sofa.
Jones, very full; sees his own reflection in a
mirror, looks at the well-fed calf asleep, and
then makes off with the purse and the silver
cigarette-box. Next day the Unknown Lady
comes after her purse, and meets the M.P.,
who squares her to avoid a fuss—not the
first fusS he had avoided in the same way.
“The whole thing was very much against
his principles,” for, as his wife tells him
afterwards, his principles' are nothing in the
world ■ but—fright. He lets off some steam
on young Jack :
You don’t seem to have any principles. You—
you’re one of those who are a nuisance to society.
You—you’re dangerous. Your conduct, as far as
I can see, is absolutely unjustifiable. It’s—it’s
criminal.. .. You and your sort are a nuisance to
the community
W
The charwoman is suspected of stealing the
silver box, and is arrested in her squalid
room nearby. Jones, who asserts that he
took it and resists the removal of his wife,
is also arrested. Gn the trial, Barth wick’s
solicitor manages to skilfully slide over
Jack’s part in the affair, and to suppress the
Unknown Lady ; the charwoman is let off and
Jones sentenced. The Magistrate tells him :
Your conduct here has been most improper.
You give the excuse that you were drunk when
you stole the box.' I tell you that is no excuse.. .
And let me tell you that men like you who get
drunk and give way to your spite, or whatever it
is that’s in you, are—are —a nuisance to the
community.
This bald outline of the play will only give
a faint idea of the artistic treatment of an
every-day story. Both sides of the case are
presented impartially ; the Barthwicks are
just as human as the Joneses.
In Strife there is the same judicial state-
ment of the case for the company and for the
strikers. The principal interest centres in
the opposing wills of the strike leader,
Roberts, and the Chairman of Directors ; and
these two characters are finely drawn.
One scene between two minor characters,
George Rous and his sweetheart, will illus-
trate the dramatic quality of the play :
Rous (who has a fierce, distracted look): Madge!
I’m going bo the meeting. (Madge, without
moving, smiles contemptuously.) D’ye hear me?
(They speak in quick, low voices.)
Madge : I hear. Go, and kill your own mother,
if you must.
(Rous seizes her by both arms. She stands
rigid, ivith her head bent back. He re-
leases her, and he, too, stands motionless.)
Rous : I swore to stand by Roberts.- I swore
that! Ye want me to go back on what I’ve sworn.
Madge (with slow , soft mockery): You are a
pretty lover !
Rous: Madge!
Madge (smiling): I’ve heard that lovers do
what their girls ask them—but that’s not true, it
seems !
Rous : You’d make a blackleg of me !
Madge (with her eyes half-closed): Do it for me 1
Rous (dashing his hand across his brow):
Damn ! I can’t !
Madge (swiftly): Do it for me !
Rous (through his teeth); Don’t play the wanton
with me !
Madge (quick and low): I would be that for
the children’s sake.
Rous (in a fierce whisper): Madge 1 Oh,
Madge !
Madge (with soft mockery): But you can’t
break your word for me !
Rous (with a choke): Then, Begod, I can !
Strife is a better play than The Silver Box ;
the interest is wider and deeper, and the
characters are built with more subtle art.
Joy is slighter in theme, and has not the
same grip as the other two. The sub-title—
“A play on the Letter I” accurately
JOHN GALSWORTHY.
describes it. Each of the ten characters is
occupied with his or her own interest, but
they all work to the destined end :
Jox {in a whisper): Dick, is love always like
this?
Dick {putting his arm round her, with convic-
tion): It’s never been like this before. It’s you
and the ! {He kisses her on the lips.)
Excepting the (to us) unknown quantity,
Granville Barker ; we think that there is no
English dramatist to-day with such pro-
mise of a great future as John Galsworthy.

“Melba”': Who says Australian poetry
isn’t read ? Writer recently borrowed
Marion Miller’s “Songs from the Hills”
from the 'Victorian Public Lending Library,
and was amazed to find from fhe date records
on the book that !)4 other borrowers had
been before him. This aroused curiosity as
to the relative popularity of the younger
Australian poets, and the records were
checked off, with the results set forth below.
“ Banjo ” Paterson (“ The Man from Snowy
River”) is an easy winner, but the full
record has been lost in his case, so the num-
bers can’t be.posted. Brunton Stephens,
Will Ogilvie, and E. J. Brady have Only re-
cently been added to the library ; whilst
Roderic Quinn and John Farrell are “ non
est inventus.” Of the others, Marion Miller
surprisingly holds pride of place, prob-
ably because the library members are mostly
of the same sex. The same reason may
account for the popularity of “society”
versifiers like John Sandes. Here are the
names, with the number of times each book
has been taken from the library
Marion Miller (“ Songs from the Hills ”) ... 94
John Sandes (“ Rhymes of the Times ”) ... 88
A. B. Paterson (“Rio Grande’s Last Race”)... 87
Henry Lawson (“ When the World was
Wide”) 84
Barcroft Boake (“ Where the Dead Men Lie”) 81
J. B). O’Hara (“Songs of the South”) ... 79
Louise Mack, Quinn,! “A (
H e b-b let h waits, j- Southern -j 77
Church and O’Dowd J Garland” (
A. H. Adams (“Maoriland and Other
Verses”) 65
E. Dyson (“Rhymes from the Mines ”) ... 65
V. J. Daley (“At Dawn and Dusk ”)... ... 60
“ Anthology of Australian Verse” 55
“Old Bush Songs” (edited A. B. Paterson).h 55
T. E. Spencer (“How McDougallTopped the
Score”) ... ... 46
Guy Eden (“ Bush Ballads ”) 36
G. E. Evans (“ The Secret Key ”) 35
Rena Wallace (“ A Bush Girl’s Songs ” ... 35
The older Australian poets, such as Gordon,
Kendall, and Harpur, are seldom taken
down from the shelves ; but all find readers,
even down to Henry Parkes (“ Murmurs of
the Stream”), whose record shows two
devoted admirers.
GEORGE MEREDITH.
Forty years back, when much had place
That sin.ce has perished out of mind,
I heard that voice, and saw that face.
He spoke as one afoot will wind
A morning horn ere men awake ;
His note was trenchant, smart, but kind.
He was of those whose words can shake
And riddle to the very core
The falsities that Time will break. ...
Of late, when we two met once more,
The luminous countenance and rare
Shone just as forty years before.
So that, when now all tongues declare
He is unseen by his green hill,
I scarce believe he sits not there.
No matter. Further and further still
Through the world’s vaporous vitiate air
His words wing on —as strong words will.
THOMAS HARDY
(in The Times.)

DINNEFORDS
MAGNESIA.
NERVOUS
PROSTRATION.
A Marvellous Cure.
Thirty-one years have elapsed since Mrs. Ann
Griffiths, of No. 2 Garden Avenue, Glebe, came
to > this country from Shropshire, and five years
since the experiences contained in this report were
encountered. “I could hardly crawl along,” Mrs.
Griffiths went on to explain to our representative.
“I was so terribly weak, and the whole of my
physical misfortunes arose through bad digestion,
so it is a more important matter than most people
think. For months previously, I had been right
off my food, and I was kept back by the fact that
I was unable to get my rest. Then I discovered
what a dreadful thing it is to have nerves that are
unhinged, for mine certainly were. Why, if a
person spoke to me at all unexpectedly, I would
almost jump out of my skin, and if I happened to
be walking along the street I would be in con-
tinual fear of being run oyer. Good gracious ! how
I used to start if anything dropped on the floor;
but this was not the worst feature of my nervous
breakdown. I was for ever dreading something
that I knew not what. Somebody was going to
die. or be injured, or a fire was going to take place,
and I couldn’t get these ideas out of my head, no
matter how I tried. In bed at night I would find
myself fairly shaking, with perspiration breaking
out upon me, and I would be afraid to move. I
was full of trouble and felt as if I were gradually
sinking into my grave. A suffocating sensation
in my chest, acute aches down my back, a vile
taste in my mouth, and a. tongue white with fiir—
those were some of the symptoms that showed I
was not in good health, but the falling-off in my
weight was more el-oquent testimony still.”
“ What weight were you prior to your illness?”
“ Fourteen stone —a big weight for a woman—
but I got down considerably. Indeed you wouldn’t
have known me, I was so pale and thin, and it
wasn’t because I didn’t try to get well, Heaven
knows ; for I tried all manner of things without
any good resulting. I suffered.most unmercifully
from headaches and giddy feelings, and wasavv-
fully despondent at not getting relief from the
physios I had taken. I used to wonder what to try
next, and presently X struck the idea of baying some
Clements Tonic. Well, I could not have expected
so much as I realised from that medicine, though
from what I knew of that remedy beforehand, it
is a wonder I did not use it earlier, only the pre-
vious experience was in an entirely different case,
about which LIT tell you. Tn mv case, • though,
Clements Tonic was wonderful. It strengthened
me throughout and cured my indigestion, even
when all else fhiled. But its effect on my son
may reasonably be regarded'as one-of the wonders
of the age. When a child, he received a great
shock, which gave him St. Vitus’ dance. He was
treated at the hospital, and took no end of medi-
cine. Poor chap ! he was almost demented, and
hardly able to walk. It was pitiful to see him
quivering in every nerve, hardly able to drag one
leg after the other, with fearfully; hideous nights
in front of him always—no sleep very often, and
when there was, dreams that made him call out —
a poor appetite and weak digestion; really no-
thing but a shattered wreck that would bring
tears to one’s eyes almost to look at. And he was
like that for years, with never a hope of being
cured, as the doctors could do nothing for him, yet
Clements Tonic set him up and made him so
active, and as sound as any man in Sydney.
People marvelled to see him cured, and anyone
about here will corroborate what I have said.”
“They can see your statements in print?”
“ The whole world should know avhat Clements
Tonic can do for suffering humanity, and I am
only delighted to be able to tell you what that
remedy has done for me and mine. And you can
form an idea of what a mother's gratitude should
be, and is, when she has had her son transformed
from a tottering, quivering cripple to a lithe-limb-
ed, sound and robust man. Yes, publish this
news throughout the land in any manner you
desire.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, MARY ANN GRIFFITHS, of 2 Garden Avenue, Glebe,
in the State of New South Wales, Common wealth of
Australia, do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have
carefully read the annexed document, and that it contains
and is a true and faithful account of my illness and cure
by Clements Tonic, and also contains my full permission
to publish in any way my statements—which I give
voluntarily, without receiving any payment; and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the same
to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths
Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Glebe, this eleventh day
of March, one thousand nine hundred and eight, before
me.
12] HENRY W. WILLGOSS, J.P

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Killed by the Chemist.
Swinburne has ceased to sing-. There will never he
another great poet. Science has killed the imagination,
because its realities transcend all imaginative efforts, etc.
— P. E. Quinn in Lone Hand of June, 1909.
Swinburne sleeps ’neath his pall,
Toll, toll the bell !
Poets both great and small,
Hear ye,your knell !
Science has murdered Song !
Haste ye away !
Farewell ye singing throng !
Hail, Formulas !
Good-bye to fairy tales,
Science has come !
Poetry’s dead as nails !
Muffle the drum !
Midsummer sprites and imps,
Fairies and elves—
(Mythical things like shrimps !)
Bury yourselves !
Dear, unsuspicious bird,
Lover and maid,
Science says you’re absurd,
Go ! get a spade !
Make a deep cavity,
Cast yourselves in
With all due gravity
(Sexton, P. Quinn !).
Speaking religiously,
E’en this we know :
“ Odor of sanctity
Is H. 2 O.”
Curates protesting faint,
Archbishops snort ;
Science has felled the saint
With its retort.
There’s not a thing Above —
Likewise, Below—
(Sausage—Religion—Love),
Science don’t know !
Chemists imperious,
Puddle and curse
In the mysterious
Old Universe.
Hid with bad vapors, they
Savagely plod,
[ Gnawing like mites away
Cheeses of God.
Little flames leap and fade,
Crucibles hiss,
( What fact could e'er evade
Analysis ?)
“ Science has murdered Song!"
(How those words chimed !
Someone just sang them ! . . . WRONG !
SOMEONE JUST RHYMED ! !)
YICTOSIA.
THE PORPOISE.
The Pub in the Promised Land.
There are tales enough of the Golden Shore,
But, as far as meself has learned,
They haven’t got much to commend themselvi s.
So far as meself’s concerned.
They mostly go nap on a shiny crown,
Wings, and a golden lyre,
And a rickety yarn that life’s reward
Is a place in the angel choir.
\Vell, a crown and a harp’s no good to me,
Nor a place in the angel band—
I want to be havin’ me heart’s desire
When I get to the Promised Land.
And me heart’s desire ain't set on a hyinn,
Nor the gut-strings’ tumpty-tump ;
I want to cheer up on a goodlier tune —
The tune of the nectar pump.
If a fellow goes straight in this world below,
I reckon that when he dies
They’ll draft him along to the Things He Wants
In the corners of the skies.
There’ll be uniformed coves before the Gates
To yell as the souls drive up :
“ This way for the harps and the music halls ! ”
“ In here for the foaming cup ! ”
“Swing off to the right for the banquet hall ! ’’
Or “ The girls are four blocks west ! ”
And so it will go till the souls sort out
The places they all love best.
As far as me and meself’s concerned,
There’ll be nothing to do but tell
A porter that’s wearin’. a guide-room badge
That I’m after the Grand Hotel.
I can see him show me a great old pub—
The pub of the pubs I knew.
I’ll hear the voices I used to love
Of the long-dead pals—the few
Who were men you could count as hearts of gold
I can hear their surprised : “ It’s Jim !
.Now, how in the name of all that’s blue
Did they hand out a pass to him?”
And Madge, and Ethel, and everyone
Of the girls we used to know.
Who manned the pumps and loosened the corks
In the days of long ago,
Will set the glasses along the bar.
And, raising ’em high in hand,
We’ll drink the toast of our Heart’s Desire—
The Pub of the Promised Land.
N.s.w.

A GENUINE EXERCISE IN
COMPOSITION, BY A BOY
13 YEARS OLD.
“Sir, —You ask me how
1 spent King’s Birthday.
Well, my father called me
to get the cows at 4 a.m.
“Then 1 pumped water for the cows,
and gave them some feed, while my
brother got the horses ready to deliver
the milk in round the
town.
“We drove in and
delivered the milk, and
got back at half-past 7.
I cut the feed for the
cows,
“ an< J pumped some more water.
Then had breakfast.
“ I got up and drove them in.
I milked nine.
“After breakfast I turned the separ-
ator, and then fed the pigs. We
pumped more water for the cows, and
gave them more feed.
•* After dinner we cut some
lucerne got the cows in
to milk.” Then, having repeated the]
morning’s programme—with more pump*
ing, of course—the essay winds up
with: “Then I had tea, and went to
bed”—having first, let us hope, sang “God Save the King.”

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to Tiik Bulletin would oblige the paver
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is . not a pood thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” in' the proper
superscription for “ copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
ojlicials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, JULY 15, 1909.
HOW AUSTRALIA TO A GREAT EXTENT
MADE ENGLAND.
I’he Bulletin has received from England this
interesting communication, written in a neat
ilerkly hand on the back of a postcard :
38 Esher-road, Liverpool,
27th May, 1909.
Sir,—The kangaroo on an Australian postage stamp is
out of date, and I would humbly suggest an alternative,
namely, the “ Dog in the Manger.” For a people who
object to the Yellow man, and likewise their own kin,
to help in bringing the arid desert under cultivation, the
“ dog ” would be most appropriate. At the same time,
when the “ dog ” vvanjfcs a bone in the shape of a loan, he
can, as usual, have it for the asking.
To return good for evil is splendid Christianity, but
poor business. Shut out Britain’s goods, shut out her
honest poor, but pray accept her money and the pro-
tection of her navy. Yours, etc.
(Signed) John Siikehan.
That tale might be either English—the Podsnap
variety of English—or it might be Anglo-Aus-
tralian—the Decayed Fish variety of Anglo-
Australian. It would be characteristic in either
case. From the Podsnap variety of Englishman,
who really fancies that John Bull is God, many
things are possible. For the other alternative, as
The Bulletin has had occasion to remark before,
it is the ill-luck of Australia to be cursed with a
certain number of old Tories, mostly imported
from England, who consider any lie, slander
or dirty aspersion justifiable so long as it is
directed at their own country and its Demo-
cratic policy. These people hold that decency,
gentlemanly feeling, the Ten Commandments,
and what they are fond of calling “British Fair
Play ” are suspended when a chance comes
to demonstrate their “loyalty to the Empire”
by maligning the land which finds them
in food and raiment, when it ought rather to
find them in a good strong gallows.
* * * *
The story of The Bulletin’s British corres-
pondent is amazingly inaccurate, though that sort
of thing is only to be expected. Australia has
not yet shut out even one of its own kin, unless he
was so hopelessly diseased as to be a source of
positive danger. It certainly has an educational
test for immigrants, which test may be applied to
any and every arrival, but Australia didn’t want
to make it apply to white British subjects or to
any other white men. It was the very remark-
able British Government which declared that
there must be no appearance of drawing distinc-
tions between Englishmen, Zulus, Chinese, and
the like—the Englishman being strangely fond of
ranking himself with such people—and that the
education test, if there was to be one, must profess
to apply all round, otherwise the Act would be
vetoed. So, nominally, the education test applies
all round ; but no Englishman, Scotsman, Irish-
man, Frenchman, Belgian, German, Dane, Swede,
or any other white man whatsoever has yet been
asked to pass it, nor is there the least chance that
any of them will be asked. Instead of shutting
out the poor but deserving British person, Aus-
tralia is actually paying the unfortunate indivi-
dual’s passage money to help him get here, and
is hunting up well-paid jobs for him when
he arrives—very much better-paid jobs than
his own country offers him. It has been
doing this sort of thing for a very long while.
Within the last fifty-eight years Australia has
paid or mostly paid the passages of about 600,000
British people to its shores, which is a fair num-
ber of folk to drag half way round the globe on a
charitable errand. The number brought out in
1907 was nearly 5000, and the thing was done on
an even larger scale in 1908. As regards the
loans which Australia (described by The Bulle-
tin’s correspondent as “ the dog ”) gets from kind
old Bull ‘‘for the asking,” that is a familiar old
lie of the Tory press and easily recognisable. In
fact, it is as conspicuous as the absence of a bath-
room in the great majority of British houses. Aus-
tralia doesn’t get any loans “for the asking” ;
it gets them because it pays John Bull as good
interest as he can get anywhere else for the same
security; much more than he can get if he buys
his own consols; more than his own banks will
give him if he puts his money on fixed deposit;
more than he can make by investing in his own
agricultural lands since his absurd policy of Free-
trade ruined British agriculture, and quite as
much as his worst enemies, the Germans, are
willing to pay him. If Bull wants grovel, grati-
tude and abasement thrown in with a plain busi-
ness transaction, he had better knock half per
cent, off the interest. A half percent, for grovel
is not excessive. Of course, the idea, that the
borrower should be profoundly grateful to the
lender is very beautiful, and it is always acted
upon in England. In that country, where every-
thing is as it should be, the manager of the Bank
of England always goes down fiat on his stomach
when he sees a depositor—-even a very small one
—for the depositor has lent him money. And
everybody takes off his hat and steps lightly and
reverently while passing the pawnshop where his
watch and best clothes are, for the Moses within
has lent him money, and he feels, therefore, that
he should regard Moses as a father. Even the
British Chancellor of the Exchequer, when he is
doing the dog act, and is out after “a bone in the
shape of a loan,” manifests due humility, and
always hits his head on the floor three times. It
is one of the rules of the game.
Again, the lie about Australia shutting
out British goods is familiar. In the matter
of goods (as apart from coin and bullion)
Australia—tariff or no tariff—buys more from
Britain than Britain does from Australia;
and Australia gives Britain a preference over
other countries, yet it receives no preference.
In 1907 (the last year for which complete figures
are available) Australia bought from Britain
£31,900,000 worth of goods, and in return Britain
took from Australia £28,000,000 worth of goods
and many millions in gold. Any old coun-
try is willing to take gold. The biggest item in
the way of goods which Australia has to sell is
wool, and of that item France is now the largest
buyer, Britain comes second, and Germany is
third. All the same, the tale about the debt of
gratitude which Australia owes to its mode-
rate customer, John Bull, is a weariness of
which there appears to be no end.
And that amazing narrative about our indebted-
ness to Britain for naval defence grows to be a
weariness also. There are few British men-o’-
war here—very few, and small, and weakly-
armed, and mostly rather old. The best of the
lot is a third-rate warship aged 14 years, with an
obsolete and condemned type of boiler. It isn’t
considered worth re-boilering. One armored
cruiser of modern build could knock out the
whole lot. Their only active duty lies in keeping
order among the Polynesian isles, with many of
which Australia has little to do, and their beat
covers most of the South Pacific, where they do a
lot of work protecting English traders and Eng-
lish missionaries. It is commonly understood
that they are under orders to leave at once and
rendezvous at Singapore if war breaks out, but on
this point definite information is scarce. We
have no guarantee that any serviceable vessels
will come out in their stead in war-time ; but
more than one British statesman has declared
that, in the case of a conflict with a country of
Germany’s size, the whole British naval force will
necessarily be required around Britain’s own
shores, and that remote dependencies will have to
shift for themselves, and argue their own case
with any third party which chooses to think the
time opportune for plunder. Australia, as a
country that has paid a good deal for British
naval defence and got a very dubious quantity of
the goods, grows a trifle weary of these demands
upon its gratitude for problematical favors re-
ceived. The favors may be all right, but the
removal of every serviceable British ship out of
the Pacific creates a feeling of doubt. As regards
military defence, the six Australian States at
all events have sent very much larger forces to
assist Britain than Britain ever sent to assist
Australia. And they got much less reward for
their services. The officers of the little British
force which once loafed in this peaceful land
helped themselves rather liberally in the way of
rich grants of land, to say nothing of that amaz-
ingly profitable monopoly of the trade in rum
which they seized upon—wherefore the early
British garrison is known in history as the Rum-
Selling Corps even unto this day.
* * * *
It might help many Australians to get a better
understanding of the situation if they considered
this country’s back history a little more than they
do. The extent to which Australasia has helped
to make Britain is not generally understood
simply through lack of study. Just before the
Australasian goldfields were unearthed, Britain
was a poor but enterprising country, terribly ham-
pered according to modern ideas (in fact, all the
world was hampered in those days) by wantof float-
ing capital. Then the new wealth was dis-
covered, and the British pauper and the British
beggar and the British struggler and adventurer
rushed out, or were carried out at Australia’s
expense, to these parts, and in a great multitude
of eases went home again rich. A remarkable
number of them were carried out by Australian
charity. Inside 20 years (1851-1870) the Aus-
tralian Governments paid the passages of 307,158
British poor, that they might come here and dig
up the alluvial gold, and take advantage of the
land grants which, in the early days, were handed
out pretty liberally, and thereby get suddenly
affluent. A whole lot of the wealthy people
(some of them with titles) who malign Australia,
either in Australia or in England, on account
of its democratic legislation, might, to-day,
be scavenging or stone-breaking in the Old
Land but for this country’s liberality in that far-
back period. They might even be sandwich-men
—and it is difficult to imagine anything much
lower than a sandwich-man, toddling along in the
gutter between two boards, and barked at by
homeless dogs. Australasia raised £572,000,000
worth of gold between the years 1851 and
1908, and in one way and another Britain got
most of it. Australasia provided employment for
British bankers, who got as much as 36 per cent,
dividends on their money. That was what the
Union Bank of Australia paid to its English
shareholders in 1854 ; it started with a miserable
little capital of £143,972, and in its first 50 years
Australia enriched its English shareholders with
£8,607,422 of profits. In like manner the
Bank of Australasia started with £200,000 capital,
and in 53 years this country enriched its English
shareholders with £6,949,169 of profits. And in
the 20 years which have elapsed since then (for
these figures are only up to 1889) they have very
largely improved on this record. Despite
the crisis of 1893, the English banks in
Australasia, on the money which they actually
invested here, as apart from capitalised profits
and reserves which they earned here, are probably
making an all-round 20 per cent, to this day.
(There were four English-owned banks in Aus-
tralia when the memorable ruction of 1893 took
place, and exactly half of them suspended, to the
great inconvenience of Australian depositors, so
this country had a right to consider that English
banks, despite the great amount of profits which
they took out of the country, are not much more
good to the country than Australian banks after
all.)
* * * *
Then again, Australasia turned a whole herd, of
petty English dealers into merchant princes, and
some of them still have their warehouses in these
parts, and spend their profits in England. It
turned needy English adventurers into wealthy
squires, and they repaid the kindness by demand-
ing that the entire coal trade of N.S.W. should be
in their hands, and that no coal should be raised
except on their lands. In fact, for a while, any
Australians who dug up coal were sent to penal
servitude. Australasia supplied much profitable
employment for British shipowners, and it has
employed at different times over 100 British
Governors (and thereby helped to keep more than
one of them—-notably a dead-broke Governor who
dealt in hens—out of the workhouse) at salaries
ranging as high as £lO,OOO a year. For about 40
years, until the South African gold discoveries
brought a new contributor into the field, it was
almost wholly Australasian gold which created that
proud coin, the British sovereign, and it was
mostly Australasian gold which enabled Britain
to embark on that stupendous later career
of money-lending at compound interest which
made it the great world-usurer of recent times.
* * » *
In 1848, three years before the commencement
of Australasian gold production on a large scale,
it was estimated, according to the eminent British
statistician, Mulhall, that the whole of the
world’s stock of gold consisted of £157,000,000 in
the shape of coin and £343,000,000 uncoined
(jewellery, plate, etc.) —total, £500,000,000. There
had been a scarcity of new gold discoveries for
some centuries, and the world’s stock of the
medium of exchange was pretty low. It was this
unfortunate scarcity which caused the Bank of
England to suspend payment for 24 years (1797 to
1821), and flooded Britain with a depreciated
paper currency. Since the date of Mulhall’s
figures Australasia has supplied, mostly to Britain,
more gold than all the world was estimated to
possess before this country came on the scene.
Then, having run short of ready cash through
allowing its resources to be exploited wholesale
by British adventurers, Australasia supplied
John Bull with an investment for about
£300,000,000 of his money—most of it origin-
ally Australasian money, for until Australasia
started producing gold Bull hadn’t very much
actual gold to lend—in Australasian public securi-
ties, on which, in the early days, this country
often paid him as much as 6 and 7 per cent,
interest. In all this gigantic series of trans-
actions not one penny of interest or prin-
cipal has ever been in arrear, wherefore
Britain’s luck in its dealings with Australasia
was very different from what it experienced
when it lent to the Governments of Turkey,
Greece, Spain, Portugal, Guatemala, and a dozen
other foreign States. It was better than its
luck in its dealings with Austria, which
made a memorable composition. It was better
than in the case of Britain’s dealings with
France or .Russia. It was also better than the
luck Britain had with itself, seeing that the first
British public debt was repudiated in the time of
Charles 11. Nowadays Australasia’s trade with
Britain is almost equal to Britain’s trade with the
whole world 20 years before the Australasian gold
discoveries gave John Bull his memorable boost.
In proportion to its population, this country buys
more from Britain than any other country on
earth, and it is (in proportion to its population)
the most notable support of the British manu-
facturer. All of which things make up a fairly
good record.
* * * *
Australasia, by reason of the tribute it has re-
mitted to Britain in rents (many of them rents on
lands which the British owner never paid for) in
banking and insurance profits, in the profits de-
rived from mining concessions which were handed
out gratis to the British person, in the profits of
British mercantile firms established in Austral-
asia—in short, by reason of the Australasian
policy of allowing the British absentee the free
run of all this country’s resources —has supplied
Britain with enough money to meet the bill for
the whole naval defence of the Empire during the
last half century, and has contributed largely to
the Gold Country’s support in other ways as well.
And it has overlooked the fact that a big British
life assurance company once repudiated its Aus-
tralian liabilities, and that exactly one-half of the
British-owned banks here burst in 1893, to the
great inconvenience of the trusting Australasian
depositor. The fact that Britain, by its silly
Freetrade policy, has allowed a large proportion
of the profits it derived from Australasia to slip
away from it, is a different question, and one with
which Australasia has nothing to do. Consider-
ing all that Australia and Maoriland have done
for Britain, these communities have a right to be
just a little weary of the Imperialist or Decayed
Fish party, with its constant yelp about the
stupendous debt which we owe to the 3VIa Land.
Now and then there is even a feeling that the
debt is on the other side of the ledger, and that
but for Australasia there wouldn’t have been
nearly so much Ma Land as there is. The story
of How Australasia Made Britain is at least
as convincing as Parson Fitchett’s wonder-
ful narrative about How England Saved Europe.
And —apropos of the correspondent quoted
at the beginning of this article, and his typical
remark about this land’s readiness to “accept”
Bull’s money—it may be mentioned that Bull
accepted all the money which came to him from
these parts, and refused nothing. He accepted it
thanklessly and off-handedly, as one who only took
what was his due. He has even been accused now
and then of being a trifle condescending towards
the country which gave him so stupendous a leg
up, and of assuming the attitude of the Superior
Person; but that was doubtless a misapprehension.
THE CITY OF LIES.
Last week, the Sydney Telegraph was moved to
sudden fury in the matter of the proposed Aus-
tralian capital, and published two articles, one
headed “The Attack on Yass-Canberra,” and the
other “ Victorian Allegations : the Water Supply
Scare.” A few extracts follow :
So far as the Melbourne press is concerned, therefore,
the demand for Dalgety as the site of the Federal capital
—and it has been frankly admitted that, if Dalgety were
chosen, the seat of government might remain in Mel-
bourne for 50 years—can easily be grasped and accurately
appreciated. The interests of newspapers, in whatever
State they may be published, have no more claim for
consideration, of course, than the interests of any of the
citizens of the Oommonwealch. But the grievance of the
Melbourne papers is that, if the capital should be estab-
lished at Canberra, the exceptional advantages which
they enjoy would go with it, and that they would be on a
level with the other newspapers of Australia.
The opposition to Yass-Canberra, being fanned in Mel-
bourne, is prompted by objection to any site that is not
either actually or in effect Victorian. As the seat of
government, Melbourne is ideal in that view ; while as a
capital site Dalgety is the next best thing, because it
would be nearer Melbourne than Sydney, and anyhow it
presents so many difficulties and grounds of objection
that its selection would mean hanging the whole question
up and leaving Melbourne as the capital for years to come,
if not for ever. That this pettily provincial attitude
is representatively Victorian is quite unlikely, but it
has to be combated all the same, and involves
the same necessity for emphasising the proved suitability
of Yass-Canberra, the same energy on the part of New
South Wales Federal politicians in pressing for a settle-
ment of the whole question on constitutional lines. Yass-
Canberra has triumphantly survived the test of expert
inquiry under which the other sites have gone down. It
has the requisite scenic beauties, it is handily situated,
it has a city site that can be easily sewered and drained,
while as for water, in addition to the Cotter River there
are the Molonglo and Queanbeyan to levy on if necessary.
These facts are all attested by the Advisory Board,
which places recommendation of the site on an
impartial as well as an expert basis. The sugges-
tion that Sydney’s interest is at the back of
the advocacy of Yass-Canberra is absurd. What
has Sydney to gain from the capital being there, when
even the little bit of trade and traffic it might bring is
intended to go by way of Jervis Bay, and when the
capital will be so connected with the railways as to
enable inter-State visitors and Federal members to go
direct from their States? Whatever may be said of the
State as a whole, the chief Sydney interest in the matter
is in having the Constitution observed and Parliament’s
declared choice given effect to. Not much time, politi-
cally speaking, remains for doing this, consequently the
State’s r 'presentatives will need to vigorously press for
the final steps to be early taken to place the capital
where it belongs by constitutional provision and Parlia-
mentary preference.
* * # *
Now, even the worst kind of gutter publication
doesn’t, as a rule, start a course of hard lying
without having some object to serve; therefore,
the really remarkable series of falsehoods in the
screeds quoted above calls for investigation. In
the first place, the statement that it is the Mel-
bourne press which has consistently advocated
Dalgety as against Canberra is a lie: it was The
Bulletin that did so, and The Bulletin did it
in what it believed to be the interests of Australia,
as apart from the local interests of either Sydney or
Melbourne. In the second place, the statement
that “it has been frankly admitted that if
Dalgety were chosen the seat of Government
might remain in Melbourne for 50 years ” is a lie,
except in the sense that something of that, sort
might be “ frankly admitted ” by the Telegraph,
or some such rag. In the third place, the
statement that if the Federal capital were
planted at Yass-Canberra the Melbourne papers
“ would be on a level with the other newspapers
of Australia ” is a lie. for the Sydney papers
would be only about 200 miles from the Federal
city ; the Brisbane papers would be about 800
miles away ; the Melbourne ones would be about
600 miles away ; the Launceston, Hobart, Adelaide
and Perth papers would be so far away that their
chance of competing for any Federal capital busi-
ness wouldn’t be worth alluding to. And in the
fourth place, the statement that Dalgety is nearer
to Melbourne than to Sydney by any possible
road or railway route is a conspicuous and glaring
and foolish lie. And the reason for all these lies
is worth looking into.
* * * *
There are no special and intrinsic virtues about
the Yassberra Federal capital site to recommend
it above all others. It was reported upon only
the other day by four specially-appointed officials,
all men from Sydney, and presumably not pre-
judiced against Yassberra because of its nearness
to Sydney. They said nothing at all in favor of
the beauty of the site or the fertility of the soil.
They said, distinctly, that the Cotter Creek, which
has been put forward as the great standby of
Yassberra ever since it was first heard of, can’t be
relied upon to supply the wants of a modern and
up-to-date city of more than 50,000 people ; and
when the little United Kingdom, which would be
lost in a corner of one of Australia’s big States,
has a capital with 6,000,000 people, this idea of
providing for the future of a mere 50,000 inhabit-
ants seems a terribly short-sighted business. They
added that, even for 50,000 people, the supply
would be very costly. About two other creeks,
the Queanbeyan and Molonglo, they said that
“ prolonged gauging and still further investiga-
tion ” would be required before anything would
be really known as to their value. Concerning the
proposed Federal port at Jervis Bay, they said that
considerable works would be necessary to make
it a safe and serviceable harbor. And since that
report was issued a N.S.W. State Parliamentary
committee has declared against the proposal to
extend the N.S.W. South Coast railway from
Bomaderry to Jervis Bay, on the ground that the
country down towards the proposed Federal port
is so barren that it isn’t worth the trouble and
expense of a line.
* * * *
Therefore the question keeps rising : Who is to
profit by this Canberra capital, and why should
the Sydney Telegraph lie so furiously and reck-
lessly and unscrupulously on its account? The
place has neither scenery, fertility, water supply
nor water power to recommend it. It is no more
within the limits of the Constitution than Dal-
gety is. It won’t be of any special advantage to
the Sydney traders; if it is a failure and a
mere petty country town its trade won’t help
Sydney much, and if it is a success and
becomes a big, noble city its competition will
be disastrous to Sydney. Apparently the only
profit will go to the Sydney morning papers.


With a capital at Canberra, these papers, aided
by the special trains kindly put at their service
by the N.S.W. State Government, could arrive in
the Federal city about 7 a.m., which is as early as
anybody wants a paper. Under these circum-
stances, the job of starting a rival paper in the
Federal city would he just as tough and difficult
as the job of starting a rival paper in Sydney
itself. But at Dalgety neither Sydney nor Mel-
bourne papers could arrive until much later in the
day. They would both be out of all serious con-
sideration. The Federal city would produce
great journals of its own. They might be Pro-
tectionist papers, which would be bad in the view
of the S. M. Herald and Telegraph. They
might be strongly Federal papers, as against the
State Frights attitude of the journals of
the State capital, which would be even
worse. They might even be Labor papers,
which would be horrible—ten different kinds of
horrible. The idea of a Federal city where the
Labor Morning Herald or the Workers’ Tele-
graph—a paper which advocated high wages,
Federal control of industrial legislation, Federal
ownership of railways, Unification and Recon-
struction, the abolition of State Governors and
State Upper Houses, the Federal progressive
land-tax, and the like—would be dropped on
everybody’s doorstep at 7 a.m., while the dis-
credited Tory and anti-Socialistie morning paper
from Sydney only arrived about 10, and the
lamentable Tory rag from Melbourne didn’t toil in
till noon, is the sort of idea that certainly doesn’t
appeal to the Telegraph and the S. M. Herald.
Of course, they might start papers of their own
at a capital like Dalgety, but these papers would
set out from scratch, in competition with the
workers’ daily local organ; whereas, in such a
pla.ee as Canberra, the Sydney anti-Socialist
papers would be great, strong, old-established
publications, and their existing organisations in
King-street and Hunter-street would answer as
well as they do in Sydney itself. If the capital
was placed where the Sydney-built paper could be
delivered in time, there would be no need for new
offices, new machinery, new staffs, and new
organisation. These are the only visible reasons
why the Sydney morning papers should lie in such
a wholesale manner in favor of Canberra —the
barren/ordinary place with the unreliable water
supply.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Elective Ministries—Some Further Aspects.
For days the morning papers have been wailing
over the stoppage of public business, and the dis-
organisation and scandal and chaos that have
resulted from the Labor party’s demonstration in
the Australian Parliament of the farce to which it
is possible to reduce the existing governmental
system. And the spectacle has made so strong an
impression outside the House that once more talk
is rife concerning Elective Ministries—the one sure
method of preventing the sort of futile time-
wasting to which the dailies take exception—and
actually a proposal is afoot to introduce the
Elective Ministry system in the N.S.W. Legisla-
ture. So the Sydney Telegraph, because it
is always against the adoption of any principle,
however obviously sane, that is not of English
extraction, squared its jaw (l/7/’O9) and waded in
to show that, however bad and - foolish, and
ruinous to progress, and preventive of the carry-
ing out of the business of the country, the present
obsolete system may be, yet the Elective Ministry
one —which has been working smoothly on the
Continent of Europe for years—must be infinitely
worse. Its arguments in support of this theory
are:
(1) The Elective Ministry arrangement might act as a
solvent of party ties.
(2) The official position of a Minister elected by a
majority of members would be such as to make him
entirely independent of his colleagues. It would be feas-
ible that Ministers of diametrically opposite political
views might be elected to sit together in Cabinet, and
harmony of policy would be impossible.
Why the Elective Ministry system should dissolve
“ party ties” —in the sense of driving people to go
back on their convictions and their leaders—
is not clear. That, as a fact, is the effect
that is being produced under the present
arrangement which puts so high a premium
on the abandonment of principle by parliamen-
tarians. The maddened Freetrade leaguers who
met in Sydney recently and repudiated various
prominent Federal Freetraders, and pledged them-
selves to work against the latter for the future,
would never have been impelled to do so had
those whom they assailed been members of a
House run on the Elective Ministry basis, and so
not been forced to follow a fiscal path of which
they disapproved. Possibly the Telegraph fears
that the Freetrader who was left out of a Ministry
by the votes of his fellows would be liable to turn
Protectionist in his annoyance at the slight. But
Freetraders haven’t apostatised to any great
extent as a protest against being left out of Minis-
tries by their leaders in the past. Equally absurd
is the contention that the presence in an elected
Cabinet of Ministers of conflicting opinions must
make for dissension. The reverse is the case. In
what Elective Ministry would it be possible to find
a greater number of conflicting elements than in
the present confused Cabinet, which holds Joe
Cook, Freetrade Tory ; Deakin, Democratic Pro-
tectionist; Forrest, Protectionist Tory, and a
heterogeneous mass of sectarians, anti-sectarians,
pro- and anti-defenceites, State-frig liters and
nationalists. It is impossible for these folks
to be honest and to agree for long under
the imported Cabinet system at present in vogue
—a system which postulates that a Minister
who holds views contrary to those of his colleagues
must either dissemble the same or go. The
Federal Cabinet, as at present constituted, exists
collectively and individually, if not by open
lying, at the least by chronic suppressio veri. The
expression by any member of it of his sincere
views on almost any topic of importance would
mean an instant burst-up of the entire concern.
And that is a state of affairs that must make,
men with any glimmerings of self-respect,
for perpetual humiliation and friction. TjhMm' the
Elective system a Minister would not need to be
dishonest to remain in office, and a prolific source
of jar and trouble would be removed. Instead, the
Ministry being elected irrevocably, for a set period,
regardless of the expressed views of its members,
or of the fates of the measures they supported, it
would be practicable for Deakin. if the mischance
of the ballot cast him into Cabinet association
with one so antithetic to him as Cook, to vote and
speak according to his convictions on a vitally
urgent issue such as the capital site one; and it
would be pbssible for Cook to remain Deakin’s
colleague and yet do no outrage to his (Cook’s)
fiscal conscience. Each could speak the thing he
thought without danger of losing his steady job as
Minister thereby. And, further, the House at
large could vote and talk on these and other
questions as it thought, so that it would be
possible for one who was anti-Australian on
the fiscal issue to vote pro-Australian on
(say) a defence issue without the ghastly
prospect of hurling a well-loved leader into
screwless obscurity ; and possible, in like fashion,
for a Victorian Protectionist to assault Dread-
nausea without injuring, or severing relations with
Alfred Deakin. Assuredly the effects that
would not be produced are those which the Tele-
graph fears—viz., the weaning of the politician
from his allegiance to the “party”—the crowd, to
wit, that thinks as he does on any given political
topic—and the causing of dissension among
Cabinet colleagues. Apart from which it is a
scheme that is worth introducing, if only for the
huge amount of lying (directly and by implication),
and dishonesty generally that it would exorcise
from our public life.
The Curious Case of the B. N. Z.
The affairs of Maoriland’s big bank —the B.N.Z.
—are a curious study these days. The institution
has £1,000,000 capital, of which £500,000 consists
of preference shares taken up by the State. The
dividends on the State's preference shares
are limited to 10 per cent. ; the dividends
on the £500,000 worth of ordinary shares
held by the public may go up to any figure,
and as a matter of fact the ordinary shareholders
have just received 12J percent. The Government
has also advanced to the bank £1,000,000 of its
own good loan money at 4 per cent., repayable in
1914. Now. last year the bank earned £408,151,
so if the £500,000 of ordinary capital, the £500,000
of the State’s preference capital, and the £1,000,000
State loan had all been regarded as capital rank-
ing equally, the return would have been over 20
per cent. And it would have been possible to
pay a 10 per cent, dividend all round and yet
add £208,151 to reserves. As the Government’s
£1,000,000 loan only carried a fixed 4 per cent., the
£368,151 of profit which remained after this item
of interest was paid represented £36 16s. 3d. per
cent, on the £500,000 ordinary capital and £500,000
preference capital. Either way the case is looked
at the profits are fairly gorgeous, and for all these
profits the bank is indebted to State interference.
It was once a purely Private Enterprise concern.
The private shareholders elected strange, weird
beings to manage their affairs, and the strange,
weird creatures steered the vessel on to the rocks.
They appear to have been so excited that they
couldn’t see a financial rock anywhere without
rushing straight at it. A time came when all the
capital and reserves were lost, and a lot of the
deposits as well. It was decided to close up for
good and all, unless the State would come to
the rescue. The State came. It took a risk
that no private capitalist would look at, and
advanced £2,000,000, of which £500,000 has
since been repaid. It took over the manage-
ment, gave the bank all the Government business,
bought up the Colonial Bank and added its con-
nection to that of the 8.N.Z., and saved the
situation. But for the State the shareholders
would never have seen another dividend, and
would have had, in addition, to pay up every
copper of uncalled capital that could be squeezed
out of them. Thanks to the State, the private
shareholders have just received a 12£ per cent,
dividend, and have the joy of knowing that the
reserves stand at £710,999. And now a remark-
able thing is foreshadowed. At present the
private shareholders own half the bank; that is
to say, they hold £500,000 out of the paid-up capi-
tal of £1,000,000. But it is understood that they
are to be allowed to pay up their uncalled capital,
which they will joyfully do if permitted—any-
body would joyfully put more money into
a safe-looking concern, where the profits, thanks
to State aid and to the fact that the State
only asks 4 per cent, on its advance of
£1,000,000, run to over 36 per cent, on capital; so
these private shareholders will presently own
two-thirds or perhaps three-fourths of the rich
institution which the State saved for them,
instead of a mere half as at present. If this is
done it can only be said that the wild generosity
of the State, in giving away to Private Enterprise
so- large a share in a bank which ought to be
wholly its bank, because, save for the State, there
would be no bank at all, is unaccountable. Also,
it seems a trifle mad. The State would be fully
justified if it insisted on turning its £1,000,000
loan at 4 per cent, into preference shares with a
right to dividends up to 10 per cent., and thus
made itself permanent owner of three-fourths of
the concern. Even then the fourth interest that
was left to Private Enterprise would be the State’s
free gift to the shareholders, seeing that, but for
the State, the B.N.Z. would have died alto-
gether. A State which made Private Enterprise
a present of shares with a face value of
£500,000 and a five-eighths interest in profits
while they keep at the present £400,000 a year,
could hardly be called stingy. Then, if the profits
kept up, the State would get a return of £150,000
a year on its investment of £1,500,000. Assum-
ing that it set aside £60,000 a year to pay interest
on the £l,soo,ooo—that sum being all loan money
—it would have a balance of £90,000; and if it
set that aside to accumulate at compound in-
terest in reduction of the truly awful public debt
it would at least be doing something for the public
good without putting any strain on the people’s
purse. The amount —£90,000—may not look
much, but in 50 years it would accumulate at
per cent, compound interest to nearly £12,000,000,
and in 100 years it would almost overtake the
existing public debt, large as that is. Maoriland
is so deeply attached to its public debt—it loves
it as if it were a brother—that it would probably
prefer to give away this chance as a free gift to
the private shareholders of the 8.N.Z.; still, in
case the Dominion should have a lucid interval
the matter is worth a mention.
* Food-grabbers.
A tattle news by cable, and a little more by mail,
came to the Australian daily press concerning the
“ Report of a Departmental Committee appointed
to inquire into combinations in the Meat Trade,”
as it affected the land of ,T. Bull. The food-
grabbers of Chicago have lately captured beef-and-
cattle companies in Argentina, and the baleful
glare of the vulture eye has been cast upon Aus-
tralia and Maoriland also. Here are some of the
facts established concerning this gorged meat-
eater, known as the United States Beef Trust: —•
(1) Four U.S. firms—Armour, Swift, Morris, and the
National Packing Co. (all owning subsidiary English
companies)—together with two of less importance, exer-
cise in combination a large control, rapidly growing, over
British markets.
(2) The Trust runs a Black List of persons who are not
to be supplied by any of its members.
(3) The Trust, by regulating supplies, can send prices
up or down in the enormous Smithfi Id Market, and does
actually and absolutely fix provincial prices at an agreed
advance upon that of Srnithfield.
(4) The Trust has control over practically all the ship-
ping space for carrying cattle from North America to
England ; and can smash any rival not possessed of a
capital like the Pyramids.
(5) The Trust holds many stalls in Srnithfield Market,
and can coerce stallholders who depend upon it for sup-
plies.
(6) The Trust has captured two huge beef companies in
Argentina; and is likely to capture more. When it does,
“ the situation may well become serious.” (Even J.
Butt recognises that.)
(7) The Trust controls absolutely the supply of chilled
beef from the U.S. A. ; and this was almost the whole of
Britain’s import till Australia and Argentina took a hand.
(8) The Trust has “made inquiries in Australia and
Maoriland.”
Now, it is easy to see how the depredations of
this cormorant affect J. Bull, and just as easy to
see how they affect Australia. It reaches out to
swallow the Argentine beef, because Argentine
beef jumped from about l,ooo,ooocwt. in 1904 to
3,600,000 cwt. in 1908. When the Argentine meat
goes down its maw, one of two things has got to
happen to the Australian beef-export trade. It
will be either captured or killed. If U.S.A. and
Argentine supplies are refused to dealers who
buy Australian beef, the Devil would not smell
worse to them than an Australian carcase ; and the
deadness of a frozen leg of bullock would be
nothing to the deadness of the industry. The
Wise People say, “Anyhow, mutton is safe”; but
a Polyphemus that had gulped all that beef would
soon raven for mutton also. Therefore, it is up
to J. Bull to deal harshly and bitterly with these
marauders, who otherwise will devour the Chil-
dren Who Give Him Dreadnoughts. It is also
up to J. Bull to consider his own hungry con-
dition. The Beef Trust, if it likes, can send his
national sirloin up to a huge price, so that the
Roast Beef of Old England will be a dish for
dukes only. It is a rare enough diet already for
Britain’s 12,000,000 on the verge of starvation ;
but it will become so rare that the 12,000,000 won’t,
know the difference between it and lobster.

Cash v. Corpse.
It seems almost impossible to suppose that Tait,
who wears the cigar in hisface, is wholly responsible
for it; but it is an amazing fact, nevertheless,
that more people have been killed on the Victorian
railways during the last three years than in any
previous half-dozen or so. Compare the last three
years with the three before that: —
1903 .. 28 1906 .. 60
1904 .. 25 1907 .. 55
1905 .. 25 1908 .. 90
Total 78 Total 205
Formerly the Victorian railways didu’tdoany thing
special in the way of butchery, though they had
a bad record in the boom days ; and for 14 years
from 1901 down to 1906 they averaged only 37
corpses a year. Now, however, they jump to an
average of over 68. Of course, as remarked just
now, it seems almost impossible to suppose that
one man is responsible for this ; yet it isn’t calcu-
lated to make Victorian railway travellers any
more comfortable when they remember that
Canada, where this one man got his railway
training, and learned method which he presumably
tries to reproduce, has the worst railway
statistics in the world so far as sudden
deaths among passengers are concerned. Tait’s
own Government printed the latest statistics on
the subject, the other day ; they showed Belgium
at the top of the list with three-quarters of a
corpse per 10,000,000 passengers, down through
Austria, France and Germany, all with less than
one full corpse per 10,000,000 passengers, and on
to these last disgraceful three at the bottom :
Spain .. .. .. .. .. 5 "54
Russia .. .. .. .. .. 9’Bo
Canada XO'OS
It may, of course, be that the Canadian has a
habit of lying on the steel rails for the sake of
warmth, and gets fatally hurt in consequence ; on
the other hand, it may be that the Canadian rail-
ways decide to take certain risks —practically to
do certain murders—for the sake of saving
money. It looks cold-blooded when put that
way ; but in industrial concerns—mines and such-
like —the cold-blooded proceeding is done every
day of the week. Practically every accident is
preventable; it is simply a matter of spending
enough money and spending it in the right way.
Adopting that principle, one of the commonest
of all State mining regulations is that the
occurrence of an accident shall be priiaa facie
evidence of neglect on the part of the owners or
managers. Therefore, when a railway system
shows an appalling number of accidents it is a
fair thing to assume that more than what others
reckon a fair amount of risk is being in-
curred ; and that is an item which ought to
be taken into account in comparing finan-
cial results. Statisticians haven’t yet agreed
what is a reasonable thing to charge up
for a corpse; but if £5OOO is about the
right figure, then Tait’s financial results for the
three years are in the aggregate about £450,000
worse than they look, seeing that the corpses have
numbered about 30 a year more than the average.
However, Victoria doesn’t want corpses, anyhow,
even if they are put in a balance-sheet after-
wards ; and what it therefore needs to do is to
very seriously consider whether it approves of a
system of railway management which, presumably
in order to save pence, risks life as it obviously
has been risked during the past three years.

Bull and Chow.
Hitherto our father 801 l has been hardly able
to understand Australia’s antipathy to yellow
immigration ; and has been wont to remark that,
as the Yellows are hard-working, sober, law-
abiding people, it is impossible to see why they
should be excluded, unless it is that their virtues
are objectionable. Now he has got a very small
Chinese problem of his own, and in a little while
he will probably be able to view the white man’s
exclusiveness with an altered mind. The first
Chinese settlement of any size was in Liverpool,
and the aliens seem to have been a picked lot.
They settled down quietly, and took unto them-
selves English wives. The colony was watched
closely by the police, who reported from
time to time that the Chinese were law-abiding,
industrious, sober, and altogether very free from
crime. The fact that a great crime was being
committed by the mixture of the races and the
creation of a half-caste population, didn’t appeal
to Bull ; mulatto and Eurasian and piebald
Eroblems are new to that complacent, top-
ooted, bulldog-followed person. Then a colony
settled in the Limehouse district of London, and
the West India Docks quarter was quickly and
quietly overrun. Again the police kept a close
supervision, and reported a striking absence
of drink or crime. They didn’t inquire
about opium. As the Chows paid their way
and never gave back talk to the shopkeepers, the
“ business people ” were loud in their praises, and
had no hesitation in declaring that the heathen
were much superior to their own folk. But when
the Chinese shopkeeper, boarding-house land-
lord, and entertainer arrived on the spot, the
Chinese youth cheerfully chucked the English girl
under the chin, but refrained from spending any
cash with the English. In fact, he began to com-
pete with the business people. Then the business
people turned with a snarl, and made complaints.
But the complaints were unheeded. Quite re-
cently, however, a batch of Chinese coolies, 1000
strong, whose time on the Rand had expired,
came to South Wales and were promptly put to
work in the coal mines at one-fourth the very
moderate wages received by the local Taffies. Had
this happened ten years ago, Bull would not
have been much impressed by the yells of his
working classes; but with the German menace
haunting his dreams, the patriotism of our father
Bull threatens to become aggressive, and he is
worried. The question that is worrying Bull is
—if the sons of Han displace the English
and Welsh workers, where will the country get
the fighters it will certainly one day require to
shove back the German attack ? So, notwith-
standing their thrift, industry and other domestic
virtues, our father Bull begins to regard the
Chinese immigrants with a cold, unfriendly eye ;
and if the influx continues it is certain that some
sort of measure of exclusion will yet be submitted
to Parliament. Bull, in fact, is threatening to
evolve a White England doctrine.
Peers as Pioneers.
About an African white elephant:—
There is a prospect of Rhodesia becoming part of
United South Africa, and the peers and their friends who
compose the Chartered Co. are jubilant. Australians, it
is said, are too prone to seek aid from Governments. If,
however, one desires to see cadging carried on in a
manner high and aristocratic, let him observe the ways of
the B.S.A. Co. That company is composed mainly of
members of the British nobility ; a duke is president, and
his co-directors include a baronet, a lord, and a marquis.
Smaller fry have a say in the business ; but, being small
fry, they are of little account. The charter was
granted 20 years ago, and the authorised share capi-
tal is £9,000,000, of which £6,000,000 has been issued.
There have been no dividends, and to-day the public debt
of Rhodesia is probably not less than £12,000,000. The
company possesses no railways or harbors, and the best
parts of the country have been sold, or granted, to com-
panies and to individuals. This is the great area that
Rhodes “added to the Empire.” Believing it to be
studded with future gold and diamond mines, Rhodes
first intrigued with its unsophisticated monarch, then
turned the Maxim guns on his men, and slew them as
Kitchener butchered the Soudanese. The British empire
is not colored blood-red for nothing. Once the old king
had been driven into the wilds, Rhodesia became part of
Britain’s possessions, but on a peculiar, and a strictly
financial,! basis. The British South African Company,
with its lords and dukes, Was formed, and shares were
sold to the common people. Lands were disposed of, tjje co.
receiving the purchase money. But the co. retained all
the belts supposed to be rich in gold. Customs and
Excise duties were levied by the co, which, at the same
time, administered some kind of law. Rhodes thought
he would get gold—gold galore. The gold he was to keep,
the land he was to sell; and what was left —nothing—he
would present patriotically to the Empire.
Mashonaland and Matabeleland turned out to be poor
in gold, and Rhodes realised that, as far as he was con-
cerned, Rhodesia was scarcely worth the trouble of
acquiring. Of the mines opened few proved payable,
even with labor which cost next to nothing. Rhodes
soon entered upon a game of superb bluff. While dupes
and myrmidons acclaimed him as the great Imperialist, the
man, it is now known, was engaged in a sordid attempt
to foist Rhodesia upon Cape Colony'. John X. Mkkri-
max, present Premier at the Cape, knew as much about
the ways and the dodges of Rhodes as any man living,
and he it was that prevented the millionaire schemer
getting rid of his bad bargain. Rhodes desired that
the Cape should take over the public debt of
Rhodesia—then some millions fewer than it is now
—and buy out the British South African Co.
at a tremendously enhanced figure. But John X.
let it be known that he was aware of the true state
of affairs, and the transfer of Rhodesia to the Cape
had to be postponed. The peers who direct the B.S.A.C.
are unique as barefaced cadgers. Twice they have
appealed to the British Government for aid, and twice
they have been refused. Cr.ewe, this year, wrote that
the British Government “ had never undertaken financial
responsibility in connection with the Chartered Co., and
was unable to depart from that policy.” Our common
Australian pioneers knew not how to go about their
business. A peer or two to do the cadging, in a style
befitting aristocrats, and a Rhodes to dispose of the spoil
may yet become the fashion. For brazen impudence in
the begging line, and in grandly passing along bad bar-
gains, the peers of Britain stand supreme. Their chance
seems to have come at last. Everything comes to him
who waits, even though the him is a peer.

enough to prevent prices falling. To ei*ible
it to do this the company is subsidised by the
pastoralists. During a few recent mouths supplies
had been’coming forward freely, and the co. had
Futile Toilers.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Wilks (N.S.W.) refuted the statement of Mr.
Thomas when he said that supporters of the Ministry only
represented the well-housed, the well-clothed and the
well-fed. He represented as hard workers as did those
sitting opposite to him.—Parliamentary report (8/7/’O9).
True for you, Wilks. Without the slightest
doubt
You represent an ultra-busy crowd ;
Unceasingly it works—day in, day out,
And machinates and plots—grave, anxious-
browed—
To put the Pope (and myrmidons) to rout,
And cast them forth hopelessly “broke” and
cowed.
Each street and lane of your electorate,
East, west, and likewise furthest north and
south,
Contains its quota of weird folks who rate
And scold aloud, like squatters in a drouth,
At Rome and its “appalling moral state,”
And slay the agbd town by word of mouth.
In sundry lodges scores and scores of them
Do gather, and with speech and song proceed
The Scarlet Woman’s march to try and stem.
In their endeavors to effect this deed
They spout like boreholes, pour forth gem on gem
Of thought—and yet somehow they don’t
succeed.
On all sides “ Prentice Boys of Derry” cheer
The late King William ; tear large gaping
rents
In John the Twenty-third’s good name, and jeer
At Pius and the rest —the which events
Are held to fill the Seven Hills with fear
Of those who, Wilks, are your constituents.
From Balmain in the north to Annandale,
From Mortlake unto Rozelle in the east,
The mere word “ Carthlick” makes electors wail,
Drop what they’re at and start to smite the
Beast,
(The Vatican to Wit) and wrench Its tail—
Withal It doesn’t worry in the least.
Your friends toil madly, Wilks, one must confess,
That is a point on which all will agree ;
The thing that’s hard to grasp or learn or guess
Is what the sense of all of it may be.
It seems so curiously profitless—
Such a wild waste of time and energy.
N.s.w.
J. B. D.


The Electrolytic Refining and
