Nothing seems to gratify party spirit so
much as party obstruction. And so the game
continues, in spite of D.O.L. Whisky being always
available to inspire wiser counsels. D.C.L., the
scholarly brand, as the spirit of progress subdues
any desire towards unruly, ebullitions, of party re-
sentment, and is the ally of all parties of wise
patriotic aims.*
to buy very hard in order to maintain the
famine price of steak and chops. As a re-
sult, the co. : s works had to run full time
preparing the carcases for export. (It is,
of course, necessary that the Sydney Meat Co.’s
purchases should be kept off the local market.)
The works ran until breaking point was reached,
and then the management gave notice that a halt
would have to be called in order to overhaul the
plant. At this intimation there was a slight
fall in values at the Flemington saleyards,
and consumers thought they saw cheap meat
on the horizon. But they didn’t. The
squatters simply kept their stock back till
the Meat Co. would be again ready to buy and
thus preserve the artificial famine and the arti-
ficial scarcity ; and the only difference was that
prices, with the artificial competitor out of the
way, were a shade higher than usual—and the
Lord knows they are usually high enough.

That rather interesting sheep-man, “ Wingen ”
Abbott, recently told N.S.W.' Pastoralists’
Union that he was in a puzzled state of mind :
He failed to understand why all our Australian Govern-
ments, while doing all that was possible to hamper,
restrict, and discourage the pastoralisc industry, should
make such strenuous efforts to create and subsidise
numerous small industries, which could not live without
ever-increasing assistance, as in the irrigation colonies of
Victoria and N.S.W., by heavy subsidies at the expense of
the non-subsidised staple industries, which grow up
naturally without assistance.
The grammar is not very splendid, but it is clear
enough to show that Mr. Abbott is still suffering
from an old and exploded delusion. The wool
person is firmly convinced that his industry is
“natural” because Providence didn’t put the
sheep here, and that, say, the iron industry is
artificial because Providence did put the neces-
sary iron and coal here. It looks rather an in-
verted yarn, but the pastoralist sticks to it man-
fully. And he has an intense admiration for his
own independence and the way he has built up
his; industry without State aid. The paltry
assistance he got from the community was mainly
this :
(1) The State provided the original sheep.
(2) It supplied the original squatters with land gratis.
(3) It supplied them with practically gratis (convict)
labor.
(4) In later years it leased out land to the squatter at a
mere fraction of the price which it would charge for
similar land to the agriculturist. It still does this.
(5) It covered N.S.W. with non-paying railways to bring
the squatter’s wool to market, and taxed the city artisan
to make good the loss on the squatter’s railways. No
railway through purely squatting country has ever paid.
(6) It exempted the squatter during many years from
the local rates and taxes which the city artisan had to
pay, and taxed the city artisan to provide the squatter
with roads, etc.
(7) It carries the squatter’s wool by rail at a far lower
rate than it charges the manufacturer on his goods. It
carries fodder for his stock in drought time at almost
nothing. It shifts his stock in drought time at just about
nothing. It makes Government tanks and bores to water
his travelling stock. Also it supplies agricultural
colleges, travelling instructors, stud bulls, and other
trifles, all on pauper-aid principles.
Barring these and a dozen or two other matters,
the squatter has always been a fine, upstanding
fellow, scorning State aid, refusing subsidies, and
declining to be pauperised in any way.
+
Sydney Telegraph's London correspondent,
writing on May 21, told how the unskilled
casual laborer is made, and the story has lessons
for Australia :
The trouble in London is that there are tens of
thousands of positions which a man might fill, but a boy
can fill equally well. A boy leaving school is a smarter
messenger than a grown man, and he is as good as a man
at delivering goods, at working an elevator, elementary
office work, and a dozen other things that give employ-
ment to tens of thousands in London. ... In the great
majority of cases he reaches 19 or 20, needs more money,
applies for it, and is promptly paid off and succeeded by
another schoolboy. ... He is driven without option to
the ranks of casual, unskilled work.
It all comes of sending children to earn their
living at work that can give them no future. It
suits the employer to pay better wages to an
errand boy, or lift boy, than he will to one whom
he is bound to teach something; and it suits the
parents to be relieved of the maintenance of their
children. But it does not suit the nation to have
its collective earning power reduced in that way.
We may not be ready for it yet; but if the manu-
facture of casual laborers continues at the present
appalling rate, the State will yet have to keep
hold of every boy on leaving school, and give its
consent before he accepts any particular employ-
ment until he is, say, 18. Quite half of the bovs
who leave school simply drift into work—any old
work that happens to offer at the moment. With
the help of the Education Departments, some
bold steps will have to be taken to stop so much
drifting.
“Lex”: Said C. Gr. Wade in N.S.W. Legis-
lature, apropos the elevation of C.J. Darley
to the N.S.W. Bench :
And it was common knowledge that he made a great
pecuniary sacrifice, out of regard for public duty, to go
on the Bench. It was a sacrifice that the public should
not b% unmindful of—etc.
As I read it, the tired feeling crept insistently all
over me, and refused to be exorcised. Whenever
a N.S.W. barrister takes to the lucrative and by
no means onerous job of judging, this hoary old
gag is trotted out. The said gag is an importation
from England, where it is no unusual thing for a
specialist in one line or another (divorce, patents
law, parliamentary work, or what not) to make
£15,000 a year. As applied to N.S.W. it has
no significance. I once heard C.J. Darley
say that in his best year he never made £7ooo—
i. 1 e 'y as n °k man to depreciate himself.
Ihe barrister’s income takes a lot of earning. It
means work by day and night, rigid attendance at
chambers, considerable outlay in travelling ex-
penses, solicitor-entertaining and so forth—and
w ' t h lean years, litigiously speaking,
are liable to happen along in which the
takings slump, and painfully little ink goes
into the fee-book. The Judge, even of the
puisne breed, banks a certain £52 per week
(if he is C.J. it is £7O), and also he commands
£254 per annum for an associate (who may be a
son, if the family is so disposed), and has all his
travelling and hotel exes., when he is on public
business, paid by the State. He works short
hours, gets a vacation of imposing dimen-
sions, and, finally, he hasn’t to lay up
any of his earnings against the dark hour of eld
—for the country supplies him with a generous
post-graft allowance till he dies. There are few,
dashed few, barristers who can pouch a regular
£5O a week the year round in N.S.W. The ones
that do amass the cash by the sweat of their in-
tellects, nor have they any hope in life of carry-
ing on the profitable work for more than a limited
number of years. There is none of them but
would leap madly at a judgeship if it were
dangled in his neighborhood. Also, there is none
—big-fee earner or otherwise—of whom his fellow-
barristers wouldn’t say, WADE-like, after he had
leapt and made good that he did so “at great
pecuniary loss,” and solely out of a desire to boost
up the fortunes of his suffering State.
.
The cry of the Tory press about what it calls Mr.
Fisher’s “policy of obstruction’’—that was the
Sydney Telegraph’s phrase—is rather curious
when read alongside its language of eight or nine
years ago. Then Kingston was shoving the tariff
through, and the Telegraph remarked proudly
that he didn’t know what real obstruction meant
until McMillan and Pumpkin Smith and Cook
showed how the thing was done in N.S.W. One
memorable evening McMillan wanted to adjourn
after a single item of the tariff had been passed.
Kingston refused, so Pumpkin Smith and his
party arranged a stonewall to last all night. The
generalship of Pumpkin, according to the Tele-
graph, was marvellous; and it went on to add, in
a tone of reverence, that his own contribution to
the blockade and the stoppage of public business
was a speech of five hours, and that he
finished up quite fresh. There were many
months of this silly, wooden-headed stone-
walling, and through it all the Telegraph’s
reverence for the staying power of Pumpkin
Smith was an almost holy thing. But the scene
has changed, and now, when the Labor party
talks for two days over a vote of censure the
Telegraph is shocked at these “ obstructive
tactics.” “Legislation is to be prevented and
the Parliamentary machine wrecked” it wails,
“ because a minority is incensed at majority rule.”
What a difference in the point of view, according
to whether the minority is Pumpkin Smith or
isn’t Pumpkin Smith.

About an episode in the career of certain sturdy
beggars :
The full strength of the disgraceful act of the North-
cote (Vic.) municipal Bumbles in cadging £3OOO from mil-
lionaire Carnegie, with which to build a library, has now
become apparent. A while back it dawned on these
Northcote worms that a library would be of advantage to
the youth of the town ; but, instead of setting about
raising the money locally for books, and utilising a
room at the Town Hall till they could build a special
apartment, they crawled to Premier Murray and begged
£350 wherewith to erect an edifice. Murray, apparently
without making any stipulation as to what was to go into
the building, consented. Just then, ’Cote heard that
Mildura had been dragging Australia’s name in the
mud with success to itself, and decided to get on
its waistcoat and do a crawl to Carnegie likewise.
When the millionaire sent along his £3OOO, North-
cote’s library committee reckoned the £350 Mur-
ray had granted for building purposes would do
nicely to buy books—apparently it hadn’t up to then
thought it necessary to have reading matter in its library.
Again Murray was complaisant and told the committee’it
could go ahead. The Murray of older days would have
grinned sardonically at the notion of a ten-shilling book-
case to contain a bob novel, but the Murray of to-day is
different. The matter would seem to have been settled
by then ; but at that point the council as a whole chipped
in and decided that the library committee had no busi-
ness to splash the £350 on books, but that the coin
should be spent on the purchase of land, so that Car-
negie’s £3OOO might be all available for bricks and mortar.
This sort of thing, it seems, is calculated to increase the
value of property in the neighborhood—the coun-
cillors being, of course, property-holders. So the North-
cote library is to consist of these items :
(1) Building cadged from Carnegie .. £3OOO
(2) Land cadged from the State .. .. 350
(3) Local contributions Nil
(4) Books .. .. .. •• ..Notone
£3350
But, presumably, even the most dreadful suburban coun-
cillor will see the discrepancy of these things, so the
Solons of Northcote will bail up Premier Weary for more
public money, and when they get it they will rush forth
and buy all the works of Nat Gould, Guy Boothby, and
“ OurDA.”

After interviewing Victoria’s Agent-General,
Taverner, and getting that great man’s seal of
approval put upon him, a London linen-draper a
while ago paid £54 as passage money for himself,
his wife and four children. On arrival in Mel-
bourne, expecting to find dairying land ready for
him to take up, he was sent first to the small
holdings near Mordialloc, and then to Gippsland.
Under the conditions, the Cockney linen-draper
naturally failed to pull through, and now he wants
Murray to send him back again. He was one
result, and one victim, of the provincial method
of doing things. He was one of the immigrants
that the six State offices in London compete for
—and then leave in the mud. At the very moment
that Cotter, M.L.A., was telling the Assembly
about his London linen-draper, Palmer, M.P.
for Echuca, was telling the Fused Government
that it must certainly do something to provide
lands for immigrants." The House gave Palmer
five minutes’ unaccustomed attention. Having
begun like this, it seemed possible Palmer was
going to have an attack of wisdom. But no !
Palmer, having fervently assured Deakin and
Cook that immigrants must be attracted, went on
to recommend Deakin to “apply to the State
Governments to throw open their lands.” It is a
pity Palmer and other State Frighters couldn’t
be brought into contact with the London linen-
draper after somebody had informed him all about
the Six Hives of Cantankerous Insects and their
buzzing habits, about the Six Provincial Parlia-
ments and the Six Houses of Obstruction, about
the locked-uplands and the unpatriotic landowners,
and about the blastiferous affability of successive
Federal Governments and the abounding capacity
of the Federal Parliament for letting things slide.

Yesterday (Wednesday) night at Her
Majesty’s, Sydney, Miss Nellie Stewart will
appear, unless something prevented her, in “ As
You Like It,” provided the roof didn’t fall in, or
the Wade Government doesn’t lodge an objection
to Rosalind’s buskins, or the lady’s voice doesn’t
fail her, or something. It is necessary to hedge
thus cautiously in discussing a theatrical event
which happens to-morrow when viewed from the
hour of writing, and last night when considered
from the morning of publication, and the double
point of view causes the tenses to become mixed.
For six nights, dating from the occasion which is
partly to-morrow and partly last night, Rosalind
will hold the forest; and then, amid a flood of
limelight and much feminine applause and brolly-
hammering, J. Superhuman Knight will transpire,
superbly, on a rock as Boney I.
** * *
Nothing has occurred to block or hamper
“ The Great Rescue ” which eventuates nightly at
Sydney Royal. It is the same effective ceremony
as of yore. There is no bungling on the part of
either salvaged or salvagers. All perform their
parts in the slick, sure fashion of experts. With
the summer approaching—the perilous season
when people take to falling off ferry-boats in a
state of inebriety, and go bathing in the undertow,
and travel by Johnson’s trains, or go down to the
sea in the Bega and behold the marvels of the
great deep, and otherwise take their lives in their
hands—it is desirable that all should roll up and
learn from the Blandolt co. the way to snatch a
fellow-creature from the jaws of Dissolution.
** * *
Even as The Bulletin proceeds to press
(which feat it performs weekly on Tuesday night),
Walter Bentley, tragedian and Scot, is donning
the sombre robes and make-up of Hamlet, pre-
paratory to an evening’s gloom at
Sydney Criterion. Walter proposes
to represent the anguished foreign-
er from Denmark till Saturday
next, after which lie will under-
go certain changes as to clothes,
proboscis and so forth, and
appear in “The Bells ” as
an anguished foreigner with
a dead Jew from Poland on
his mind, the said unpleasant
object having been resting
on the same substance for
many years. The portrayal
of anguish is this player’s
strong suit, and he doesn’t
mind in the least whether
it is the woe of a Danish
prince or of a mere foreign publican, who made
his first rise by butchering an Israelite in the
dead of night.
** * *
Sydney Tivoli has nothing new to say this
week. The first part still opens with the remark-
able Scotch farce, and the second contains no one
who was not there last week. The most radiant
patches of the bill are still provided by R. H.
Douglas, Florence and Lillian, Cissie Curlette,
the Three Laurels, and Rosina Casselli’s dogs.
The Tivoli weather department is now announcing
the last nights of Florence and Lillian, Graham
and Dent, and the Casselli dog troupe, so this
paper assumes that a change of programme is
approaching from the sou’-east.
** * *
With the Royal closed, and the Tivoli
given over to the jigging film, Adelaide Empire
has had a week to itself; and Lennon, Hyman
and Lennon are doing well in it at popular prices.
The Bernards (and dog) have been getting most
of the applause; Yokama and the quaint Bruce
Drysdale have just come in. The Royal has re-
opened with “ Struck Oil,” and a younger genera-
tion rejects incredulously the statement that the
same lady appeared here in the same piece 34
years ago ! Adelaide has not seen much of
Maggie Moore lately ; but it sees a good deal
now. The old play still has a certain drawing
power; but, all the same, theatrical affairs in
Adelaide cannot be said to be very exciting this
week.

“ 0.K.” throws more gloom on the Peer-
age of England
Still harping on the question whether the imported
Andersonian melodramas introduce mis-titled persons of
good old British rank into their midst. lam practically
challenged by “ 10-2-4” to quote an instance of an “in-
correctly styled Debrett resident” in a British-made
bellowdrama. Well, I have turned up the programme of
a 10-year-old shocker, “The Power and the Glory,” by
the author of “ When London Sleeps”; and what do I
find ? Ha ! ha ! The aristocratic matron of the piece is
styled “ Lady Gwendoline (of Dyce Morriss)”; and the
inane young man who loved a plebeian damsel is “Johnnie
Dyce Morriss (Lady Gwendoline’s son).” By what
rule of Debrett’s Peerage or Baronetage, I would
like to know, is Johnnie Dyce Morriss’s noble
parent entitled Lady Gwendoline (of Dyce Morriss)?
But I will also quote a more notable instance of im-
ported incorrectness. The programme of “The White
Heather” gives us Lady Janet Maclintock—the character
that Mrs. Bland Holt has always played—widowed
mother of Captain Alec Maclintock. This strikes me as
being not quite according to Debrett, but it may be all
right. I am, however, certain that the programme of
“ Sporting Life ” —another of Bland Holt’s importations—
is wrong in naming one of the characters “John, Earl of
Woodstock.” Our Governor-General is an earl, but I
don’t remember to have seen his Christian name on his
visiting card when he pushed it under the front door.
And now, by way of a crusher, I quote from the pro-
gramme of “London Day by Day,”a drama by George R.
Sims and Henry Pettitt One of the characters is
Lord, Kempton (a sporting baronet). Finally, I quote
three lines from the bill of the Andersonian production—
Plea ! ure -’’ Here are the three lines, and
if 10-2-4 can make any Debrett sense out of them let
nim declare it:—
In the Prologue.
Harold (Earl of Carlingforth) .. Mr. Robert Inman.
In the Drama.
Lord Carlingforth (of the Towers) Mr. Robert Inman
Lord Arthur Selwood (Lord Car-
lingforth’s son and heir).. .. Little Dorothy Swift.
Why Harold should have his title enclosed within
brackets, and how the earl of the prologue was succeeded
by a lord, and how that lord, even if he were an earl
could have a Lord Arthur for his eldest son—these are
mysteries to me.
** * *
“Sweet County Kerry,” introduced with
profuse advt. at Melbourne Bijou last Saturday,
is said to be a Yankee perversion of “ The Kerry
Cow,” which was accepted in former days as a
poor relation of the Boueicault family of Irish
drama. The emerald green shell of an old-
fashioned play remains in evidence. The Irish
squire bemoans a mortgage on his estate ; the
process server, with bent back and bandy legs,
washes his hands with invisible soap whilst doing
the villain’s dirtily work ; and an incidental major
figures as one of the “ould sort.” But the
substance of the original play—if it had any
must have been scooped out to make space
for a lot of insipid soft stuff and saccharine
matter, commonly known as “piffle” The
period of the story is 1798 (on the pro-
gramme), and the mild young blacksmith
who pervades every scene is arrested in the
first act on a false charge of making pike-lieads
for the invisible patriots. Also, he expresses in
song a hope that all the emigrated sons of Erin
will eventually return with one accord—like Jews
to Jerusalem—“ and set ould Ireland free.” But
the beginning of the story has no body and tail to
it. Though the blacksmith is duly placed in a
rebel’s cell, he escapes from prison and goes
straight to his smithy, where he blows the bellows
of a blameless life as before. Just here a sus-
picion of racing melodrama falls upon the pro-
ceedings. The villain has laid a scheme to
nobble the favorite, or otherwise soil the
purity of a noble sport. A fat racehorse,
impersonated by Murmer. the Caulfield Cup
winner (by kind permission of John Wren, Esq.),
appears at the smithy, bestridden by a she-jockey
in green silk jacket and cap, and wearing a num-
bered saddlecloth of the kind that first came into
Australian racing vogue 100 years later than 1798.
The she-jockey performs a harmless, song and -
dance, and the adjacent population breaks out
into Irish jigs. The villain, however, fails to cap-
ture the Kerry Cup or marry the heroine, so the
story ends happily.
If “Sweet County Kerry” has been the
means of putting 4,000,000 dollars ;to the bank

it follows, naturally, that The Bulletin is sorry

make anybody’s fortune at Melbourne Bijou. As

weak term of contempt, but it is strong enough to
meet the case.
*■*■*■*
Allen Doone, who, seemingly, has come from
America to do this thing, is an unassuming dis-
ciple of Mack as regards his mumming methods.
Lacking force and individuality, he is perfectly
easy on the stage, and his husky, melodious voice
is always acceptable in an Irish ballad. Miss
Edna Keeley (who, presumably, is Mrs. Doone) is
just as pleasingly unaffected. For the rest, there
is a capable walking villain in Percy Walsh, and
a few other adult characters capably or crudely
performed by local actors. Five children come
on and caper occasionally, and a very limited
orchestra provides a running accompaniment.

The latest novelty is the noiseless clock that
does not tick ; neither does it require winding ; but goes
in any position in a hut, castle, or motorcarforlOOO days
and nights without attention, at the cost of one shilling.
The explanation, of course, is that it is an electric clock,
driven by a battery of cells. The balance wheel is rotated
by the current, and a lever connects the balance wheel
with the hands. Ball-bearings are used to ensure noise-
lessness ; and King Ned is much impressed. The inventor
is Sigismund Kutnow, A noiseless clock will certainly be
a boon to light sleepers, and a cheerful novelty to happy
mortals who can sleep like hogs.*
afflicted young woman in a very pleasing
way. The prologue promises more than
the drama fulfils; nevertheless, the develop-
ments from the prologue are ingenious in design,
if faulty in execution. Jo Smith shows the ex-
perience of an old actor in selecting the old
materials for his situations, rather than a pro-
nounced literary talent for shaping them and
joining them together. The impossibilities are
adroitly kept from obtruding themselves, but
they are not shrouded with a veil of probability
as they might have been. One sees the faults too
clearly, whilst recognising the touches of origin-
ality in the author’s work, and looking forward
to better things from him. Another play by Jo
Smith will be produced anon (at the King’s), and
in the meantime “A Miner’s Trust” ranks as
perhaps the first full-sized play built in Aus-
tralia which has scored a considerable success on
the strength of its human interest. And
this in spite of a colorless performance of the
romantic-hero character by Herbert Bentley, who
merely talks rather like Julius Knight, and looks
rather like Harcourt Beatty. Atholwood,
Hilliard Vox, and Harry Halley are well placed,
the people in the prologue meet all requirements,
the nice-girl parts are neatly filled, Miss Holloway
being especially effective. The co. will be gone
from Melbourne by the time The Bulletin
arrives there. To-night (Thursday) the amateurs
try “ The Case of Rebellious Susan” at the Royal,
and to-morrow —well, to-morrow will be the day
before Saturday.

“The Taming of the Shrew” is due in
Melbourne on Saturday. Oscar Asche and Miss
Brayton, and the co. from the Cold Country,
make their first appearance here in Shakspeare’s
famous anti-suffragette comedy. It will be played
with the Induction, and in the dazed character of
Christopher Sly, tinker, Oscar Asche will give the
b ill of fortune a vigorous kick-off.

J. and N. Tait, whilst retaining their grip
of Melb. Town Hall on Saturdays, have set up
their ’graph at the Athenaeum Hall. The present
menu comprises the usual fact, farce and imagina-
tion. Houdini, the Handcuff King, is seen
releasing himself from substantial bangles, and
contemptuously proves the mere irony of iron.
An averted felo de se, and the astounding remorse
of a rapacious Cohen, are beheld in “ The Money
Lender,” and a pallid orgy of biscuits and
lemonadeis the thrilling theme of “ The Midnight
Supper.” Some big gun shooting is exceptionally
displayed in “ Battleships in Action.” The
picture, having been taken from the vantage of a
fighting top, gives some interesting view's of
moving guns and cleared battle decks.
Hr H£ Hf H£
At Melbourne Olympia, the pictorial West
is unfolding the drama of “ The Poisoned Flower,”
which ends in a climax of poetic justice. A
miscreant is lured to his doom by the widow of the
person that he slaughtered, and, in his death
agony, he is shown an artistic tableau com-
prising a roomy cofßn and a herd of monks.
Cupidity, love, and burnt-cork Hindus are the
strongest features of “The Brahma Diamond,”
and a trip in the breath of the W'orld is shown in
“ The Dirigible Airship.” But the gem of the
present collection is a series of glimpses of Buda
Pesth. One gets views of the royal palace in
Buda; of the Franz-Joseph quay; of sundry
streets; and, lastly, of the magnificent new Houses
of Parliament. When there are pictures like that,
people can go into the Olympia feeling quite
suburban, and come away feeling wholly cosmo-
politan.
** * *
Edward Reeves, the recitalist from Ade-
laide, is holding the stage at the Masonic and
Athenaeum Halls a few nights this week. Last
Saturday (at the Masonic) he started his second
Melbourne season with an interesting render-
ing of “The Sky Pilot,” which is to be
repeated this (Thursday) afternoon at the
Athenaeum. On Saturday he reverts to the
Masonic for the purpose of ’citing “ Enoch
Arden,” and on Wednesday next week he goes
back to the Athenteum with “The Christmas
Carol ”on the tip of his eloquent tongue. Mel-
bourne seems to have an appreciative audience for
Reeves every time.
* -* * *
The first meeting of the Repertory
Theatre promoters happened at Melbourne Town
Hall the other week, with John Madden in the
chair, but the work of elevating the taste of Aus-
tralian playgoers seems to be as far from a practi-
cal beginning as ever. Madden’s introductory
remarks were suitably severe on the things that
be. Public taste inclines to performances that
charm the eye rather than the mind, and as
regards comic opera, or musical comedy, the enter-
tainment is only pleasing to “ nice ” persons when
they fail to understand it. For example, Madden
instanced “ The Merry Widow.” He regretted to
he was compelled to take his daughters
to “ The Merry Widow.” They enjoyed it very
much, whilst he was merely thankful to know that
they didn’t understand the points which shocked
their intellectual parent. For him there was no
enjoyment to be gained from frivolitiesof that sort,
therefore he was in favor of a Repertory Theatre,
where plays pleasing to himself (and compre-
hensible to his daughters, no doubt) would be pro-
duced. The rest of the meeting being likewise
eager for intellectual entertainment, the question
arose as to what would become of the £4OOO which
the Repertorians propose to raise if it were given
to J. 0. Williamson to play with. The idea of
J.C.W. losing other people’s money, instead of
“doing in” £4OOO on his own account, was
evidently distasteful to the intellects present.
All the credit would go to the piper, the meeting
seemed to think, whilst all the loss fell upon the
shareholders who called the tune. Finally, the
meeting arrived at a suspicion that it would rather
get all the credit for losing £4OOO very rapidly
than let the Williamsonian management gain any
credit by losing the money less rapidly.

AT POVERTY POINT.
“One Who Pays”: When Oscar Asche was afc the
Grammar School in his native Geelong', he wanted a
humble part in an amateur performance there, but the
stage manager, an usher known as Peggy, said “No,
Asche, you will never be able to act.” Has not that been
said to every aspirant who burst the bars of circumstance,
and grappled with his evil star ! No, it was not said to
Brooke. He bounded on the Dublin stage, as leading actor,
in “ William Tell,” at the age of 15, and was known as the
Boy Tragedian, led about,, in a short jacket and frills, by
his fond mother. Turning up the Melbourne Grammar
School records, I find there was a performance of scenes
from “ Richard the Second,” and the Play Scene from
“ A Midsummer Night’s Dream,” just about the time
when Asche would be there. The star was one Lawry,
and I think he, too, came from Geelong. It was the poet
Stitt Jenkins, Parlces’s rival, and the greatest man before
Juddv ,who was credited with writing these beautiful
lines :
Hail Geelongia ! Hail thou favored spot!
What’s good is found in thee, what’s not is not.
So Geelong has made history.
Fred Clutsum, well-known Melbourne musiciation, seems
to have convinced them in Germany with his semi-
circular piano keyboard, and big makers talk of revolu-
tionising the instrument with the Australian invention,
which enables a player to manipulate the keys in the
distances of the board with “astonishing ease.” Every
note can be struck with equal power, and there is no
clawing out after far-off keys to exhaust the energies of
the storm-tossed professional. Clutsum tried his key-
board on the dingo, and it sniffed disdainfully. Then he
tried it on the British bulldog, and that conservative
animal was equally contemptuous. Now the enterprising
German piano-founders have got it, and think they’ve
secured a good thing.
“ Silas ” : “ 0.K.” is interesting on Jones, and right in
the main, but strains his point, I think, in trying to
make “The Liars” support his charge of Pharisaism;
neither do I think the fact of Henry Arthur having been
a bagman is pertinent to the point, unless he represented
the British Bible Society. In fact, Henry Arthur Jones
has not made good his claim to the title of Grundy. He
is not sure of himself. For instance, in “ The Crusaders”
he laughs with great heartiness at the moral reformers,
and if “ Michael and His Lost Angel ” is not a strong in-
dictment of the fatuous man who deliberately sets out to
be an archangel on earth, I don’t know what it means,
neither does H. A. Jones. Michael sacrifices himself, the
woman he loves, and two other characters to his besetting
holiness, only to find in the end that he has lost happiness
and sown discord and death. In this play the woman
with whom Michael has “ sinned,” meeting him when he
is working out his soul’s salvation, says : “ I have been
skipping and chattering from Hyde Park corner to the
Inferno by a new short cut. What have you been doing? ”
Michael replies : “ Trying to repent and forget.” “Ah,
well,” says she, “ I haven’t been wasting my time quite so
foolishly as you, after all.” “ Michael and His Lost
Angel” was a partial failure in London. That is a good
reason for our not getting it here.
“ Parisine ”: The Melbourne Bijou gives a splendid batch
of living pictures, called “ The Kiss of Judas.” Christ is
absolutely embodied in form and outward dignity by
Mounet-Sully, of the Theatre Fransais. Lambert’s Judas
is highly instructive. All the Old Masters make him a
villainous lago. Lambert does not show him as half-
witted, but rather tending that way, a cunning knave—
also fool. The Last Supper is convincingly presented,
according to Leonardo da Vinci. Peter, John and the
others are as real as device can make them. I heard an
orthodox clergyman and theological professor explain
from the pulpit that Christ did not absolutely invent the
Last Supper. It was adapted from the principal of the
Antique Mysteries, in honor of Ceres, when, from time
immemorial, cultured men, something like Freemasons,
met thus to partake of bread and wine.
“ J.G.” : Melbourne Age of July 3 had a par about play-
wright and critic G. B. Shaw, who has now had two of his
plays turned down by the British censor. The first one
biffed was “ Mrs. Warren’s Profession.” It went out on
its listener because it was irreligious, and likely to offend
the British Chapzanders and Alexjudkinses. The second
play of Shaw’s to be outed was “The Showing Up of
Blanco Posnet.” This time the play was knocked out
because it was religious. Yet Heaven knows there are
scores of plays going the rounds that are religious, or
meant to be, yet should have been passed out. The Age,
however, says “ that Shaw is an incorrigible Fabian, and
still believes in a flannel collar and shirt, and never wears
anything else, except on those rare occasions when he
goes to the opera, and has to obey the opera panjandrums,
when they say that evening dress is indispensable.”
Wonder if Shaw’s costume had anything to do with the
banning of his plays by the Lord Snooks, or the Earl of
Mount Horrible, or whoever acts as the official censor. It
is bound to be a hard tussle for a beastly Fabian and
Socialist to get anything past the Lord Snooks, but when
the Fabian turns up with an armful of MSS., and no
other clothes save a flannel collar and shirt, his cake is
bound to be dough.
“Vic.”: The glad hand to Randolph Bedford for a
daring and original play on White Australia. The dia-
logue needs something of what the playwrights call “ lift-
ing.” George Gordon put “lift” into the play of “A
Royal Divorce” by inventing the tableaux of Waterloo,
etc. Bedford, like the Owe’Sullivan in his drama of
“The Eureka Stockade,” and Duggan, in his on the same
theme, is apt to substitute politics for passion. Vivid
writing does not quite come off. Sardou’s is the
whipcord dialogue, amazingly effective even when
Englished. Pinero is another master. Likewise was
Boucicault, sometimes diffuse, but always telling.
The finale in the White Australia play, destruction of the
Jap fleet in Port Jackson by the airship, is very far
beyond my expectation. The most actual scene is the
telegraph station at Daly Waters, with the poles stretch-
ing away over the desert. This takes you to the very
place. How the monotony is relieved by listening to
the whole news of the world as it ticks out.
Bailey’s aboriginal is the best I have seen. There is the
proper rapid guttural, different from the staccato of the
Ute Indian in “ The Squaw Man.” Aboriginal talk is like
the frizzling sausage sound in the gramaphone. Diver’s
Jap has some of those splendid actualities which oozed
from every pore of Howard Vernon’s Ko-Ko, studied in
Tokyo. Of course, Vernon and Diver cannot help being
tall. By the way, it is pleasing to see Vernon’s son doing
so well. Reverting to “ White Australia,” you may say it
is rather more of a panorama, like “ Round the World in
Eighty Days.” A word for Temple Harrison’s Old
Rouseabout. He is immeasurably better than the
Lone Fisherman, in “ Evangeline,” which tickled
America; but Australia experienced nary a tickle. Lastly,
it was a grand idea to have that drop curtain of
Australia. Our beloved continent there lay All White, on
the blue ocean. In the midst of the continent was the
blue Southern Gross flag of Eureka, without any of the
irritating Jingo concomitants. This drop being hoisted
right up in one piece leads one to ask why we cannot
have picture drops again between the acts. Advertising
curtains are enough to drive people to drink. What
lovely drop curtains Melbourne used to have 1 The
Theatre Royal had five in succession: “Shakspeare’s
House,” “ Bacchus and Ariadne,” “ Lake Maggiore,”
“ Thespians Leaving Deserted Greece,” and “ Stratford on
Avon.”
“Snell”: Clarke, Meynell and Gunn are not the first
to invite the Church to co-operate in luring the people
into a playhouse. I remember when Alfred Dampier
threw wide the Melbourne Alexandra doors to the clergy,
and kept open house for pastors and spiritual masters for
a fortnight. The pilots seized the opportunity by the
wool, too ; but in the press of pulpiteers the doorkeeper
presently identified one suit. He knew it by a white
patch on the left shoulder, as if powdered faces had rested
there too numerously, and a circular disfigurement at the
back, hinting that the wearer had inadvertently resumed
his seat on top of a tart at a Metholean picnic. The
wearer was different every night, but the suit and the
powder-marks and the Sign of the Tart were always the
same. The doorkeeper drew the management’s atten-
tion, and the suit was watched. When the manager had
identified it on three different wearers, he cornered the
last inhabitant, and talked blood and raw meat to him.
It then transpired that the parsonical rig was a mere
fancy dress that had figured at 100 balls, and which was
passing round a large circle of acquaintances as an easy
“ stiff ” to Dampier’s show.
“ H.” : Some Melba worshippers had a bad time at
Rockhampton (Q.) last Friday. They wanted to hear the
cantatrice (as the S. M. Herald chastely hath it) on the
cheap or dead-head system. So they got them a ladder
and stormed the roof of the Town Hall wherein she was
carolling, applied attentive ears to various skylights and
things, and drank in the sounds that percolated through
with the greater zest in that they cost nothing.
The concert over they prepared to descend. Alack ! the
village humorist had spirited away their ladder.
Two alternatives faced them : they" might either
yell to the crowd below for help (thereby disclosing
the unworthy game they had been at), or keep quiet and
stay the night where they were. They decided on the
second course. Not till the next morning did hunger and
weariness impel them to jettison dignity and attract the
attention of a passing John Dunn. As dreary a band of
economists as the district had seen streamed home shortly
afterwards. They had been “on the tiles,” and they
looked it.
“ Casuarina ” : Next to Melba and Crossley, Oscar
Asche is the most important person who may be called an
Australian who has returned to his fatherland with a
show reputation. He went to the Melbourne Church of
England Grammar School somewhere about the time
when Deakin left. Doubtless he will explain the awful
yarns credited to him by English interviewers. He was
a good deal identified with Geelong, like the gentle and
priestly J. F. Hogan, M.P. But it is not about the Pivot
that Oscar is alleged to have romanced so wildly, but
about his boyhood in the wild Dandenongs. There he
was reported to have seen the Kellys, who were never
within a hundred miles of the place. In like manner
Sarah Bernhardt shot a tiger not very far off, at Melba’s
Lilydale. It must have been the Tantanoola one, that
overland bunyip. Genevieve Ward, back from S’Afriea,
was asked : “Haven’t you a lion story ? ” “Oh, dear me,
yes. I forgot. The ferocious animile sprang at me. I
opened my umbrella, and” — “I understand—fled.”
“No —dead.” iStrange to add, it is a positive fact, re-
ported in most of the Victorian papers, that, a few days
ago, a bull charged a man, who put up his umbrella, and
the bull fell dead. Fatty degeneration of the bull, no
doubt.
“Flinderino” from the Far North: —Towards the end
of the year the Yarrabah Aboriginal Band and Concert
Party will most likely descend upon Brisbane and Sydney
and, perhaps, Melbourne and Adelaide. These niggers
belong to the Yarrabah Anglican Mission, which has its
camp away out from Cairns; and when they turn up,
Harry Rickards will have to put on something extra if he
wants to keep ahead of them. They got as far south as
Townsville and Charters Towers recently, and their con-
certs took wonderfully, especially the corroboree dances,
though that part of the show is of Samoan origin. Their
full evening dress consists of a red sulu (waist cloth), a
white singlet, and a broad, flat nose ; and owing to the
fewness of their clothes the southern tour will have to be
rushed through in the summer. They are barefooted and
near no headgear except cockatoo-feathers. When
arrangements are completed for the tour, the heathens
will leave Yarrabah in their own b@che-de-mer schooner,
the Matubu, and sail as far as Rockhampton or Brisbane,
and thence go by train. >
“ Harrison 0.”: An article in London Times records
some interesting facts regarding “The School for
Scandal.” The writer shows how what was originally a
crude melodrama was developed by Sheridan into the
brilliant comedy as we know it. In its original form there
was no Lady Teazle. A Mr. Sol Teazle and his young wife
are to be found in another sketch by Sheridan. Maria
was the original heroine, and Crabtree the original
villain —though present writer has grave doubts about
any villain being original. Snake was at first a woman—
Miss Virginia Verjuice. Somehow, I think the character
would have been more convincing if it had been allowed
to remain so.
“ F.C.” about a house-breaker :
It is a pitiful fact that some men really don’t know
how to work for themselves. There was, for instance,
the Melbourne man who, the other day, set out on a
career of burgling. Without a published “ Guide to
Burglars” to help him, this new-chum started his pro-
fessional life among the seaside residences on Port Phillip
Bay, which are merely trifling abodes where week-enders
dwell in summer. In one he discovered a scrap of
bedding, in another an aged blanket, and in a third a
frying-pan. As the afternoon wore on, he became-
a disgusted house-breaker, hut his spirits rose as.
he came to a recherchi Queen Anne villa among
the ti-tree. First he wrenched off the shutters-
of a window, and then broke the glass, but.
found the orifice boarded up inside. That, he argued,,
was plain proof that there were treasures within ; so he-
violently drove his foot through a door-panel, damaging;
his toe in the progress, and got inside—only to find the*
loot amounted to one pair of old pants. It was heart-
breaking ; but the worst disaster struck him when he’
crawled through the hole again and fell into the arms of ai
constable, who had heard the uproar. Finally the um-
feeling Law marched him two long miles on his injuredl
foot, and wouldn’t even let him have the stolen bedding-
in his cell. As he observed, his luck was “ dead crook..’”
Financial disorders have caused a flutter
in Melbourne banking circles. First a Vic.
country manager of the Bank of N.S.W., owing
to a four-figure deficit in his accounts, was sen-
tenced to three years. Next an employee of the
Colonial Bank in Melbourne was discovered to
have departed and left no address, nor any ex-
planation of a chasm in his books. Then a mere
babe of 23, a teller in the Commercial Bank at
Prahran, finished up sensationally with a bullet
in the cranium. This last youth, like the gentleman
from the Colonial Bank, was said to be a student
of loitering quadrupeds, and was the third in the
same branch to disturb the serenity of his bosses.
Two previous employees in that grimy-looking
edifice gave their manager so much nourishment
for worry that he was declared to be mentally dis-
abled. In one way and another the banks are
having a good deal of trouble with the ill-paid
clerk, Adolphus.
The Old Soldier is disrespectful about ,
a business that gets a lot of trumpeting :
The Bulletin says (24/6/09) that the Australian army-
does not take itself seriouslj'. Is it to be wondered at
when the army reads this sort of thing in the S. M..
Herald: “Staff rides are an excellent medium forth
training of officers. They supply all the situations;
necessary for practice in strategical or tactical work;
without the presence of actual armies. A few soldiers
represent a battalion of infantry: a couple of horsemen
do duty for a regiment of light horse. Everything is
carried out as if there were large bodies of troops.”.
Everything? Well, not quite! Everything, perhaps,
except the sense of the actual presence of the troops.
In the staff-ride in question one of the contending forces
was supposed to consist of “ four brigades,” or 24,000
infantry, which, with proportions of cavalry and artillery,
would make up an army corps 30,000 strong-; and, in
order of battle, in camp or bivouac, this lot would occupy
a front six miles long, or on the march would form a
column ten miles long. You can’t learn how to handle
a body of that size by pretending that a dozen or so men
represent the lot.
Staff rides are a German invention to give generals-
commanding divisions and army corps a chance to
traverse the districts where their forces are quartered,
ami decide on the operations for the next manoeuvres,
when they will go t hrough them with the men actually
present. When the British War Office was smitten with
the idea of the staff-ride, it remembered the trouble it
often had to collect men enough for an effective trial of
some new tactical idea, and it also remembered that, in
the old drill books of 60 or 70 years ago, there were rules
for what was called “skeleton, or rope drill,” in which
two men with a rope stretched between them would rep-
resent the front of a company, or squadron, or any other
sort of “ unit.” It was a good way of making a few meni
go a long way, and so it was grafted on to the British
staff-ride as a new means of teaching tactics, which is the
fine name for drill.
One trouble about carrying out everything in a staff-
ride “ as if there were large bodies of troops present ” is
that, with so very few men, it is almost impossible to
make the huge and complicated mistakes that are often
made in war. Once during the operations on the Loire
in ’7O-1 a French army corps had to move from one posi-
tion to another, and the two divisions marched by
different roads. The right division, when about half way,
was suddenly stopped by finding the left marching across
its road. The latter had taken a wrong turning, and, as
a result, when the army corps reached its new position, it
was inverted, the right where the left ought to have been,
and vice versa. Such a mistake as that would be impos-
sible in a staff-ride, because, where there are only a hun-
dred men or so in question, taking a wrong road is really
of no consequence. But a real army-corps marching in
one column would take three and a half hours to pass a
point on the roadside ; and mistakes under those circum-
stances are of very great consequence.
So, though you get some of the frills and the fancy
work in the staff ride, you don’t get the real business of
soldiering : you don’t get the experience in handling big
bodies of men, and moving them quickly and accurately.
And without that experience anything else the staff ride
may teach isn’t of any great consequence.
THE WAIL OF THE RED-NOSED ONE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The variety business is gradually besoming the circus.
—Bulletin (June ’O9).
I used to be the turn of the night—the top of the bloomin’
bill ;
But times are changed and the public taste (excuse my
French) is nil;
Oh, the managers must be “ dotty 1” they’re too pros-
perous by far,
And who mifde their fortunes, I’d like to know ? Why
the comic-singing star.
Yet now they’re gone on animal acts. Each blessed pro-
gramme’s filled
With performing beasts and birds and fish ; in big type
they’ll be billed,
While a gifted human like myself, a man of world-wide
fame,
Has to look at the bill with a microscope if he wants to
find his name.
I used to make my entrance once in a lime-lit scene,
“full-set,”
And when I took the centre—O, the receptions I used to
get 1
But now my scene is a dark front-cloth, very much the
worse for wear,
And when I crawl on from the bloomin’ wings no one
seems to know I’m there 1
And the “full-sets” now have gone to the dogs—or the
lions or bears or cats,
(Do you think that’s what the public wants? Ask me,
and I answer “ Rats ! ”)
And I’ve got to yell my bloomin’ songs till my throat is
fit to crack,
For when I’m on in that d —d front scene—oh, the row
that goes on at the back !
If you think that your songs are going to go, or that your
gags will score,
You’ll find you’ve made a bloomer when the lions and
elephants roar ;
So what’s the use of a funny voice, or funny togs or face ?
And yvhat’s the use of a funny dance in an inch or two of
space ?
So I’m going to chuck the reddened nose—there’s no
money in that biz,
And an animal act take on instead, for that’s where the
money is :
The comic-singing is heavy work, so here’s for a life of
ease—
Will anyone buy my props ? I’m going in for Performing
Fleas!
S.A.
EARDLEY TURNER.
DISILLUSIONED JANE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
For years Jane played in drama fine
The persecuted heroine,
The comely wife who strove, indeed,
’Gainst pain, and want, and spite, and greed,
To thwart the wicked heavy lead ;
And ever she exposed the crime—
She beat the villain every time.
At length she married (not for pelf),
Went into business for herself;
And, as a comely little wife,
Jane faced the weariness and strife
Of “ wedded bliss ” in real life.
Not twenty months had gone before
She voted it an awful bore.
Jane waited now for each dull day
To start the old familiar play,
To bring the villain, dead to shame,
To work his wiles and play the game.
Alas ! the villain never came.
So in the pit, poor wife, these times
She cheers the villain for his crimes.
VICTORIA.

THE UNBELIEVER.
The cry of “Boh,” a Melbourne suburb
girl:—
It has been raining for a hundred weeks. Some people
say six, but I’m sure it’s a hundred ! ! The gentleman
who brings the clean clothes home wears a hunted-devil
look. And we wear No matter—we wear them !
Mother roots in the old wardrobe on extra-wet days, and
brings them forth triumphantly. We have all got colds
and sore fingers. Mother cuts up old sheets and makes
what she calls “ morning handkerchiefs.” And, by reason
of our chilblained fingers, we take it in turns to do the
washing-up with a stick. We have read everything in
the house, most of it twice. And the man that keeps the
local library bolts when he sees us coming. He can’t
cater for a family that reads six books a day. Our mar-
ried sister plays all the old music she used to practise in
the schoolroom years ago ; and we sit round the fire, and
get striped blue devils, talking of all the things that
ought to be and are not, till mother tells me to go and
“call on” people—they will be glad to see anyone this
weather, she says. If the weather doesn’t “ let up ” soon
I will buy a packet of wax matches, and so—no more !
The Hornsby (Sydney) Municipal Council
spent a recent evening in wrestling with the
unpaid bill for the rent of the local School
of Arts, which was hired for a meeting in
aid of the Dreadnought. The secretary of the
movement had hawked the bill about in vain, and
finally he dumped it before the local council.
After looking at the horrible object from every
point of view, the councillors declined to risk the
wrath of the ratepayers by charging the sum (155.)
to the rates, and booted the document on farther.
A law-clerk, brought up the other day in
a Sydney court, for maintenance, begged to be
let down at 10s. a week, owing to the beggarly
payment he received for his services. The Court
fixed the levy at 155., and the poor wretch will
probably have to pinch himself in order to pay it.
Yet that Court knows, and thousands of others
know too, that clients pay big fees for law-clerks’
work. But the clerks don’t get the money—
the solicitors pocket most of it. The law-
clerk is paid 30s. or 40s. a week. But the
client pays ss. for every letter the clerk writes—
which pans out 6d. for the clerk who does the
work and 4s. fid. for the boss who doesn’t. A
Torrens Act transfer is wholly clerk’s work. A
client pays £2 for that—which means Is. fid. for
the clerk and £llBs. 6d. for the boss. Of course,
the boss has rent to pay ; but, giving all that in,
the law-clerk lives a sweated life, and will prob-
ably go on living it until he is permitted to prac-
tise as a law-agent on his own account. Numbers
of them are ready to submit to the strictest
examinations or tests of competency ; but the
lawyers hold the gate of the paddock where the
fat fees grow, and won’t let the clerks in.

The great hoax of last week was a cable-
gram to the effect that a German had devised a
stupendous magnet, which is to be moored close
to the beach in order to deflect the enemy’s iron-
clads out of their course and drag them ashore.
It is a very beautiful idea, but—
(l) The ironclad would haul on the magnet just as hard
as the magnet did on the ironclad, and if the enemy had
20 ships on the coast, each of 12,000 horse-power, the
magnet, unless it was tied to a hundred forests, would
almost certainly break loose and rush out and make a
present of itself to the enemy. If it was tightly tied it
would probably take the coast-line with it.
(2) It would haul upon German ships just as hard as
upon foreign ones. If the German fleet was chaspd into
that part of the sea during an engagement, the magnet
might destroy Kaiser Wilhem’s navy while the enemy’s
fleet got away.
(B) It would make its part of the coast quite useless,
for, if it was big enough to influence ironclads at a ser-
viceable distance, it would grab iron passenger-steamers
and sailing-vessels if they even got on its horizon, and
drag them ashore.
(4) As a magnet works both ways, it would drag the
nails out of houses, take off iron roofs, seize on fireirons
and ironmongery stocks, tear up railways and drag them
into the sea, and otherwise devastate the adjacent ter-
ritory. And, if Kaiser IV. went with his bright steel
sword and his cast-iron cheek to inspect the new device,
it would probably seize on him and cast him headlong
into the sea.
(5) Also the magnet, on account of the trouble it makes
with iron, would have to be got into position by wooden
or tin or brass or paper machinery, which is a serious
difficulty to begin with.
Consequently a theory arises that the magnet is
really an advance puff for a new play, in which
the military villain will be drowned through his
own infernal invention attracting his helmet from
a distance of three miles, while the poor but noble
hero, wearing an old suit and a cloth cap, will
survive and dwell happily for ever after.

“ Victorian ” writes, in anger :
Tait of the Oigar is the Victorian Minister for Mistakes.
Ever since he took hold there has been a sound of things
breaking. This is mostly because Tait aspires to be the
Chief Cheese-Parer of the Universe, and the Most Expert
Camel-Loader on Earth. But his cheese is always cut so
thin that it gives satisfaction to no one, and his camels
invariably have their backs broken. So the net result is
a dead waste of camels, straw and cheese—to say
nothing of Tait’s salary and cigar. Tait’s Sun-
shine wreck gouged one huge hole, and now Tait’s
railway station is discovered to be chewing an
extra £45,000 or £50,000 beyond the amount of
estimated cost. Tait, when attacked on the subject, has
nothing to offer but smoke—talk smoke and cigar smoke.
The oldest engine on the premises could do it almost as
well. Melbourne thought in the beginning that all its
trains, both country and suburban, would be brought to
the new edifice in Flinders-street. It was a holy thought,
and as the Railway Department—which is supposed to
know—had the same idea, the general public went about
stuffed with faith and pride, and began to look on
Spencer-street and Princes’ Bridge as relics that were
already crumbling. Later on, it was broken to the world
that Spencer-street would have to be retained; and
the world stood it with the help of a little bad language.
Still later, the news transpired like an escaped cat that
Princes’ Bridge could not be done without after all ; and
once more the populace bore up against the news, and
refrained from making inquiries about the capability of
Tait or the efficiency of his Department. The position
then was that Melbourne was saddled with the cost of an
enormous new station without having received any com-
pensating traffic advantages. The only,improvement was
that you didn’t get rained on so much while you fled for
your train—if it happened to start from the new station.
The people whose trains departed from Spencer-street and
the Bridge proceeded in haste to the old ramshackle
spots, arid got as wet as ever. It would be bad enough
and more than sufficiently foolish if the Flinders-street
muddle had simply eaten up its allotted £440,000 and
gone on doing the work that the job lot of smoke-soiled
sheds formerly did. But it has improved on that;
it has become the jumping-off platform of a
badly-wanted £40,000 or £50,000, and, at time of
writing, no one seems to know what train it left by
or which precise brick is its tombstone. Tait sheds very
little illumination on the financial accident. He waves
his cigar and says that a lot of things cost more than it
was thought they would ; that traffic has increased in an
utterly unreasonable manner ; also that there were delays
and omissions and other wild beasts that eat money.
Speaking generally, he cuts and spreads the blame out
nice and thin like the Departmental cheese. Tait talka-
tive seems hardly as satisfactory as Tait silent, and he
might have done better had he remained perfectly still
and smoked—like an unemploj’ed locomotive on a siding.
A LAUDABLE AMBITION.
WIFEY: “ There are times when I wish I were a man.”
HUBBY : “ When ?”
WIFEY : “ When I pass a milliner's window and think how happy I could make my wife by
giving her a new bonnet .”
The Sydney fluff-and-tape merchant !n the
retail way of business got a bad bit of news the
other morning, when he read that the disrupted
“Warehousemen’s Association of N. S. Wales”
had been re-formed. The Sydney wholesale fluff-
and-tape monarch is mostly a person who believes
in freedom of contract—for the shearer and the
clerk, and every other sort of wages man. But*
after trying it for a few months on himself, he
reckons it isn’t the thing for a fluff-and-tape
monarch. While he was supposed to be enjoying
the great blessings of freedom of contract,
what he found was that it was the retailer
who had the real enjoyment—the retailer
who, setting one fluff monarch against the
other, squeezed out of all of them a 5 per
cent, discount, among other things. The fluff
monarchs stood it for a while ; then the “inesti-
mable advantages,” etc., were taken out and killed;
the fluff monarchs’ union was re-formed ; and the
5 per cent, discount was abolished, and at the very
most now per cent, is allowed, while for
ordinary purposes it is 3 per cent. So the retailer
of the fluff and the tape isn’t feeling quite so well
pleased with things as he did a month ago ; but
the wholesaler thanks his gods that the bad night-
mare of the last year or so is over.
* * * *
A quaint case of deceit was disclosed in a
Sydney police court last week. About nine
months ago, a recently-arrived English widow,
being in a tramcar with her sister, asked a man
for information as to where to get out. Not only
did he tell her, but he got out also, and escorted
the pair home. He called next day, and many
other days, and finally he proposed marriage to
the widow, and was accepted. He gave his name
as Detective Walker, and during their acquaint-
anceship, brandished a pair of handcuffs at every
opportunity, in order to lend verisimilitude to the
narrative. The fiancee sold her business, and was
nearly ready for the wedding, when her sister in-
terupted the pair as they conversed on a sofa by
bringing in the “ detective’s ” wife. The man, it
appeared, was a perfectly mild and ordinary and
everyday paperhanger, living in domestic happi-
ness in a near-by suburb. He was middle-aged, and
had a perfectly ordinary paperhanging family.
For trifling with the widow’s affections and pawn-
ing some £lO worth of her jewellery he was fined
£2O. For being a bogus detective he was fined
£10; and his wife, who testified that up to the
time of his sudden and inexplicable straying he
had been a model father and husband, paid the
money down. So the one bright romance in a
brown existence came to an end, and again there
is only a long vista of paperhanging ahead.
“Red Tape Junior”: Holliman, of the
Treasury, and a couple of other highly-salaried
friends of mine have just been awarded a per-
manent 15s. a week for sitting on the N.S.W.
Public Service Tender Board ; and Neitenstein,
of the Gaols, is to gee 30s. a week for presiding at
the meetings. I don’t mind ; but what, I wonder,
has happened to the N.S.W. Public Service
Regulations? The boss at my place gets £4OO a
year, and, judging by results, earns about £BOO.
He has in his possession some beautifully typed
letters pointing out that, in the opinion of the
Board, the higher-paid officers of the Service
should work gratis after hours when required ;
and for the purpose of this regulation a highly-
paid officer is one at £3OO or more. These letters
are signed “ J. W. Holliman,” and J. W. Holli-
man is the very man who has just got 15 bob a
week all the year round added on to his £lOOO
per annum rather than work gratis 'after
hours. Now how can the Board expect us'
to respect its regulations (which it is a punishable
offence not to respect) when the fat pigs can
grease themselves, and hang the regulations !
* * * *
Melbourne’s most eminent Pak-a-pu-Bah
fell under the feet of the police lately, and some
Chinese bad characters which were found chalked on
the wall of the raided “shop” have been translated
to mean: “In the midst of life we are in gaol.”
The trouble of Soon seems to have been that he
was fairly Soon, but not-Soon enough. The dodge
that laid him by the slippers would never have
outed a very Soon. A theatrically-minded police-
man brewed the successful plot. He dressed him-
self in old clothes, and arranged a stage fight
between himself and another plotter in the lane
next to the gambling kennel. The fellow-plotter
pretended to be in an advanced state of beer, > and
desirous of wrecking Chowdom and giving
no quarter. The disguised policeman, as the
Champion of All Chows, fell upon the'
spurious drunk, knocked him out according to'
arrangement, and thereby won the esteem and
friendship of the furtive Pak-a-pooher. The
downfall of the kennel, and the dramatic arrest
of two shuffling heathen and several Europeans,
followed a few nights later. It reads very amus-
ingly in the daily paper until you remember that,
while the police are having private theatricals in
back lanes, murderers and safe-robbers are calmly
doing their murders and robberies in big buildings
fronting the main streets of the city. If O’Cal-
laghan’s Dramatic Co. would let poor, excited Ah
Soon alone, and rush a few of the more obnoxious
criminals into gaol, the public would applaud.
The ordinary citizen doesn’t care which China-
man wins the other Chinaman’s money, but he
hates to be frequently murdered with impunity.
CALLED FROM THE BAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
About 400 South Australian barmaids have secured
registration under the new Act. When they disappear,
the species will be extinct so far as that State is con-
cerned. —Mournful fact.
These are the last! Amid the gleaming bottles
Oh, nevermore will you a new one find
With golden teeth and hair ! Emotion throttles
What little speech we have ! Fate is unkind !
The fading barmaid at the counter lingers,
Smiling at anything that drinks and pays,
Whilst Time, the Artist, with avenging fingers
Works at her face through noisy nights and days.
A wrinkle here —a crow’s-foot dimly lurking
Beneath an eye that twinkles bravely still,
Betrays the hand of Him who, slowly working,
Shall, later, banish her from shelf and till.
Now Love’s the theme for idle conversation,
And they that oft frequent the drink.v hall
Are served with promptness for loud admiration,
Whilst sulky strangers simply wait and call.
When she has gone—when Death or Matrimony
Has called her from the bar where now she reigns,
The argument about a stiffened pony
Shall breed confusion in the sportsman’s brains.
The drink that now o’ertops Youth’s brimming sleever
In sweet return for yearning glances cast
In her direction, bv that green deceiver,
Will cease to flow when she for aye has passed.
Five years—or ten? Perhaps some old campaigner
Who’s wed “ th’ Boss” will stoutly hold the fort —
In speech and features both a good deal plainer—
When we, old man, have reached the oldest Port.
The rest by Time shall slowly be abolished,
And, one by one, like broken bits of delf,
Amid the bottles that they often polished
Be placed with care upon the dusty shelf.
N.S.W.

IMPORTANT DISCOVERY.
How Eat Mat be Removed Rapidly by a
Simple Home Remedy, Without Causing
Wrinkles, Disturbing the Diet, or
Undergoing Violent Exercise.
“Eat is nothing but unused energy,” says an
eminent physician, and the man or woman who is
over-burdened with it can easily get rid of the
excess if they wish. All they need is pne half-
ounce of Marmola, one ounce of fluid extract of
Glveyrrhiza 8.P., one ounce of pure Glycerine
8.P., and Peppermint Water to make six ounces ;
all of which they can get at any chemist’s at a very
reasonable cost. Then let them take two tea-
spoonfuls after each meal and at bedtime until
they are reduced to the desired weight.
This simple home receipt is far better than any
of the secret preparations, for it reduces the fat
safely and harmlessly. The ingredients are
wholly beneficial to the system, having both tonic
and purifying qualities, and so help rather than
distress the stomach. The remedy does not cause
wrinkles, for it reduces one gradually and
naturally, preserving a good outline, and, best of
all, needs no assistance from physical exercise or
any change in diet—one can get the best results
and still take things easy ; eating just what, when
and how one pleases.*

309=311 GEORGE STREET, SYDNEY.
Last week a Sydney Wages Board learned
a few things about how the fiery gingerbeer is
got ready for the rampageous Cold Tea brigade.
It isn’t a soft, dreamy, tame-curate sort of busi-
ness, by any means. The bottler works under
conditions which one witness mildly described as
“ not very pleasant.” His face is covered by a
network mask and his hands by gloves, while he
usually wears an oilskin. The two former are for
protection from bursting bottles, and the latter to
keep him reasonably dry. Bottles are filled at
such a rate (up to 150 dozen an hour with the
automatic machine) that there is no time to
get out of the way before they burst. Cuts
are frequent; a jagged piece of glass flying off
like a bullet may make a wound requiring
a dozen stitches. Barrister Milner Stephen,
the chairman of the Aerated Water Board, got an
idea of what the pale stuff can do : he was poking
round one factory, when a bottle of soda water, on
being dropped a couple of inches into a basket,
exploded and enveloped him in fizz and frag-
ments. Both bottler and bottle-washer (latter
keeps up a speed of CO dozen an hour on his
machine) work under damp conditions. The
washer has water constantly slopping over him,
and in the more primitive establishments
he mostly stands in a puddle. For this and
similar pleasant jobs the wage paid in a few cases
reaches the dizzy heights of £2 or £2 ss. ; but
more often it is nearer 30s. And there are a large
number of casual hands taken on during the
summer, and liable to be put off at a moment’s
notice, who get 275. 6d. or less, and work for any-
thing up to 60 hours a week. One permanent hand,
starting at the age of 11, had. after 19 years’ toil,
reached 405., and was generally allowed to work a
bit on holidays as a reward for long and faithful
service.
THE TEST QUESTION.
VISITOR : “ Can you tell me the
future?”
FORTUNE-TELLER : “ Oh, yes.”
VISITOR : “ Then how long shall I be able to keep
my new servant.”
The long aiid costly law cases over the
Stan well Park land resumptions have been finished
at last. Stan well Park is a beauty spot in the Illa-
warra district of N.S.W.. and promised to be a
national playground. But, instead of being
reserved for that purpose, the usual syndicate
bought the land, and the usual subdivision at so
much per foot was auctioned; and as one of the
attractions of the sale it was announced that the
Government would reserve some of the beaches
and land adjoining for a national recreation
reserve. This the Government did—and was im-
mediately met with a bill for £lO,OOO, partly for
"damages ! ” The Crown offered about £1650,
and after spending many weary days over it, the
jury said £3200, or thereabouts. So the Govern-
ment saved about £6OOO and virtually won its
case ; yet it is saddled with the whole of the taxed
Costs. The State is the best milking cow in the
world for the shindykit and the shark to get hold
of. And in N. S. Wales the authorities, as a rule,
give them a hand in running the patient animal
into the bail.
The Sydney Board of Health
has begun to put a moderately
heavy boot down on a practice that
badly wants crushing. In 1900 the
population of the suburbs was
367.890; then Federation arrived,
and factories went up, and the
importer went down, and by 1908
the suburban population was
466,265. Thus in eigh v . years it
increased by nearly one-third, and
that crowd took some housing.
And that’s where the owner of
the jerry building-allotment
came in. He owned what
used to be, and in some
cases still is, a swamp; but
during the past four years-
wetness was remarkably scarce
in Sydney, wherefore the jerry
allotment hasn’t been looking
quite such a swamp as usual.
In other cases the natural bog
or waterhole has been filled in
with empty jam tins, dead cats, old
boots, and other sundries; and
over the lot has been cast a few
loads of clay. After that there
mostly sprang up a signboard
notifying that these desirable
residential blocks were to be sold
at a great sacrifice by somebody
who would help the buyer to
build; and “Why pay rent?”
was shrieked in the ear of a public
that mostly hates the rent-
collector. Then the jerry allot-
ment began to be sprinkled with
jerry cottages ; and as the missis
tickled the surface to put in a
pepper tree or a geranium, the un-
derlying dead cat and other fold
things started to make remarks,
and the doctor had to be called in
to little Hethel or Hemma or
Hedgar, and after the doctor
pretty often the undertaker and
the tombstone-maker. It is an
awful and ghastly thing that last
year at St. Peter’s, where the swamp
allotment flourishes, there were 132
infant deaths per 1000 births, and
at North Botany 139 per 1000, while in
the northern suburbs, where there are prac-
tically no' jerry allotments and no swamps, the
average was only 52 infant deaths per 1000
births. For some time past the Board of (
Health has had power to prohibit the build-
ing of premises on land of this deadly sort;
but hitherto it has put down only a carpet slipper
on the practice. Now, however, it declares that
it is going to change its footgear, and action has
already been taken regarding one “ desirable sub-
division ” at St. Peter’s.

On July 6 some elaborate humorist put
into the S. M. Herald an “in memoriam ” poem
re a presumably imaginary girl, said to have died
six years ago. A careful consideration of the
initial letters revealed the violent statement that
the editor of the paper was an idiot. It is a
simple and primitive form of jest which still lags
superfluous on the stage of literature.
* * * ■*
Ever since the smash-up between the tug
Hero and the Fiji liner Suva, last year, there has
been a row over the carrying of mates by the
small vessels that tumble up and down the
N.S.W. coast. The Admiralty Judge cast
the whole responsibility on the Hero, because
it did not have a mate aboard. So last
session the N.S.W. Legislature passed an
Act commanding that every vessel over 15
tons should carry a mate, and about 50
small sailing craft, running out of Sydney
and Newcastle, have been swearing ever
since. For there are not enough able-
bodied mates to go round, and the
steamers have grabbed all there are.
Further, the newly-certificated young
men won’t sign on the dug-outs which
carry on the timber trade of N.S.W.
The sailing ships are therefore driven
to various expedients to nominally
comply with the law. The chief of
these expedients is to call in an ancient
mariner, who, for a trifle, consents
to smoke his pipe in a corner of the
deck and flap his ticket in the in-
spector's face when that official comes
to see if the law is complied with.
The chief duty of a “mate” in the
coastal trade is to wrestle with huge
pieces of timber ; and as Methuselah,
the retired mariner, is unable to bear a
hand at this sort of work, the skippers
have to engage an extra man, much to their
owners’ wrath.
* * * *
The Victorian railways have got their
“economies” down pretty close to bedrock.
They used to employ a boy porter at 2s. 6d. a day
to work the station and the adjacent gates at Glen
Iris. But the other day the shamefulness of
wasting money like this was borne in like a flood
upon the Department, so it replaced the boy with a
girl at 2s. per day. If the girl could be persuaded
to take in a bit of sewing or something, Mister
Tait, from Canada, might possibly get her for
lljd. per day. Whereupon, of course, Parlia-
ment, recognising his great value as an economist,
should put up Tait’s salary another £5OO a year.
BRAINS WILL
TELL.
“ Oh, I was engaged
before I married you —and
to a much smarter man.”
“He must have been, or he wouldn't have got
out of it.”
“ Astron ” : Halley’s comet is expected
shortly, and astronomers the globe over are-
polishing up their lenses, and preparing to bog in.
This hurried messenger is due about every 75
years. Scientists would not have been
able to trace its ancient history but for
the plodding Chinese, who regularly
marked it down without trying to find
out why it transpired or when it would
transpire again. It would come with the
regularity of the rent collector, but for
the disturbing influence of the great bodies
which it passes in its hurry. The planets
of our own system act thus, and nobody
knows what myriads of other suns and
planets the comet whizzes past in its
ellipse. So Halley’s express train is apt
to be a year or two early or a year or two
late, but it has got to come, unless the
celestial Tait who runs the show gets the
time table mixed up beyond repair, and
there is the father of a smash at some
skyey junction, and a ditched comet
with a broken back is out of business for
good.

The Syrian, who with his bundles up looks
as though butter wouldn’t melt in his mouth, can
still come down like a wolf on the fold, provided
there’s nothing in the fold besides lambs and
shorn, shivering ewes. To illustrate this, a yarn
was told in the Fitzroy (Vic.) police court, the
other day, when one of the breed, calling himself
Henry J. Simeonson, was charged with smashing
furniture and assaulting a woman. It was, it
seems, the Syrian’s own fold that he came down
on at 11'30 p.m. He had rented it to one Edward
Bull and his wife, and Bull, according to every-
body’s account, was behind with his rent. The
woman’s story is graphic enough to stand by
itself:—
When she was in bed at 11.30 p.m. with her little child,
defendant broke open the door and stood over her with a
chisel in his hand. He said, “If you don’t get out of the
house I will rip you open.” She was dragged out of her
bed and chased out of the house down the right-of-way
by defendant. A gentleman lent her his overcoat, as she
had only her nightdress on. Defendant bundled every-
thing out of the house. Some of the broken articles were
her wedding presents.
There was corroboration to this extent, that when a
constable arrived the Syrian cohort was still brand-
ishing the chisel; also, it (thecohort) “bore traces
of having been roughly handled, evidently by men
who sought to protect the woman.” Fortunately
there were people around who had some doubt
about the propriety of chasing women out of bed
with chisels at 11 o’clock on cold nights;
but the Law didn’t seem to think that small
matters like this are worth troubling about.
Anyway, the Bench, which fined the man £3 for
breaking the crockery, assessed the assault at 10s.
The Fitzroy Bench has its own ideas about the
relative value of women and crockery.
On June 30a “mass” meeting was held
in Invercargill (M.L.) to celebrate the third
anniversary of the night on which Bung was first
compelled to bang his door. The proceedings
were “hearty and unanimous,” and the meeting
passed resolutions about certain deplorable
happenings in the King Country, about 000 miles
away. Apparently it didn’t know what was
taking place under its own nose, so to speak ; but
next morning, along with the report of its own
meeting—including the remarks about the de-
plorable King Country—it had a chance to read
that for the June quarter the Excise duty on beer
manufactured in Invercargill was £435, an increase
of 20 per cent, on the figures for the June quarter
of 1908. This meant that about 5500 gallons more
beer were brewed—and presumably drunk. Still
Invercargill goes on being very sorry for the King
Country. And, meantime, another hundred pubs
have been closed in Maoriland, and the habit of
having a keg in the house grows wider and wider.
In 1896, before the local prohibition victories
started, the Maoriland drink bill was £2 19s.
per head per annum ; and it has gone on rising
steadily till last year it was £3 15s. 6d.

“Sr”: Melbourne Age, of July 1, told
a simple tale of a boy who was taken to the hos-
pital because be had swallowed a half-sovereign.
I. know the parties and the details, and it was
a really tragic yarn. Even Mercia going to the
lions in her nightdress wasn’t more pathetic. The
trouble arose in a job printery. Ichabod, the
compositor, held up half-a-sovereign and said,
gaily, that it was going on the pony Ahasuerus
that afternoon, and Ahasuerus was a good thing,
because Menahem, the bookmaker, had told him
so. Nadab, the other compositor, said he was a
goat, and knocked his arm up, and the half-
sovereign hit the roof. Pekahiah, the boy,
snatched it as it came down, said scornfully
that it was a gilt sixpence, and bit it to test
its genuineness. Ichabod hit him hard on
the back to make him give it up, and
the shock caused him to swallow it.
Stomach-pumping and other violent measures
failed to recover the coin from the innards of
Pekahiah, the boy; and as Ichabod hadn’t
another half-sov., he buried his sorrow by putting
in an afternoon’s overtime at the case. About
5 p.m. the news came that the tip of book-
maker Menahem had proved correct; Ahasuerus,
the pony, had started at 15 to one and come in
easily first. Then, in distraction, Ichabod rushed
out to demand that Pekahiah’s father should
make good the loss of £B. Being cast out with
scorn, he went to the favorite pub of Nadab, the
other printer, who had started the whole trouble ;
and taking careful aim through the doorway
which separated the bar from the parlor, he threw
a beer-pot at Nadab and smashed a large
mirror. That is all up to now, save that Ichabod
has a dark suspicion that the half-sovereign has
been extracted from the innards of Pekahiah, the
office boy, and that Jehoahaz, the youth’s father,
is keeping it to buy furniture with.
