Barrister Levy has been telling the N.S. W.
Assembly some solemn truths about the expensive-
ness of law. To quote one instance : If a man
loses a case before one of the inferior departments
of horsehair and wants to appeal to the Supreme
Court, he first requires a copy of the notes made
by the Judge who threw him out. This is sup-
plied by the Judge’s associate, who charges ss. for
every 288 words. If the Judge was a voluminous
person the bill may run to £25. Then the litigant
has to get these notes printed in leaded pica type
on “cream-wove white foolscap folio paper, with
an inner margin lin. wide and an outer margin
2gin. wide.” Having accomplished these great
works, the plaintiff is in a position to hire a costly
solicitor and three ruinous barristers —it is best to
have three in case two fail to turn up after taking
their fees—and get to business. But if it proves,
after hiring the three ruinous barristers, that the
margin was only 2Jin. wide the applicant is Lost.
THE LIMIT!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In all the sad, bad city,
Just we two—
Within a cab ! (“ A pity
’Tis, ’tis true!”)
The wheels were rattling badly.
Did we care ?
We loved each other madly.
We—the Pare ! '
So intimate and shocking !
It was fine !
(But Nemesis was knocking,
Friend o’ mine !)
The cab pulled up; romantic?
It was more.
But then he drove me frantic.
For he swore!
I stood there, wet and draggled.
While (what say you?)
The awful creature haggled
With Jehu!!
N.S.W.
HELEN JEROME.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Sydney Telegraph had a bit of bad luck on
Monday. It happened this way. Some years ago, when
the 1.0. F. insurance fake invaded this country, the S. M.
Herald had the sense to do as The Bulletin did, and
warn the public against the Red Indian concern that
wasn’t exactly a deliberate swindle, but was the worst
possible sort of a trap. But in spite of the fact that the
jaws of the trap could plainly be seen if a critic did a
little scratching, the Sydney Telegraph poured out
quite a lot of sympathy on the concern, and quoted ap-
proving remarks, and, if it didn’t actually' advise the
public to rush in, it at any rate was careful not to warn
the public to keep out. Consequently a few thousands
of Australians were snared into the Red Indian order. And
then, just when the Sydney Telegraph was once more
posing as a financial authority that had summed up
things in a calm, judicial way, and on the very morning
when it was assuring the Commonwealth Government on
its word as an expert that it saw no danger in a policy of
borrowing, the S. M. Herald reprinted the last English
Board of Trade returns covering the operations of the
I.O.F.—the other institution in which the D.T. could see
no danger. And these returns showed conclusively what
a wretched hand the D.T. is at seeing danger even
when it is twice as big as a mountain. The valuation
balance-sheet is quite a simple document; it merely
sets out that the 1.0. F thing the D.T. couldn’t
see any danger in—has a net liability of £13,402,298, and
has £2,293,088 to meet that liability with ; consequently
its deficiency as at December 31, 1907, was £11,109,210.
The 1.0. F. is, however, worked on the assessment dodge:
that is to say, while it doesn’t give any surrender value
in case the policy-holder can’t or won’t continue to pay
up his premiums, the office can increase the premiums in-
definitely. In other words, the office can make the pre-
mium what it pleases, and if the policy-holder doesn’t pay
up he forfeits everything. As a matter of fact, some of
the policy-holders have lately been called on to pay £2
10s. where formerly they had been paying only £1 ; and it
looks as though either a whole lot more will have to
forfeit everything, or the assessments will have to go
still higher. These were the things that the D.T. ought to
have foreseen, but didn’t; in fact, it wouldn’t even see
them though The Bulletin repeatedly drew attention to
them. Wherefore when the D.T. declines to see any
danger in the borrowing policy, and consequently advises
the Commonwealth Government to bog in and borrow, it
will be just as well for the Government to remember
that the paper’s inability to see danger is an old-standing
and apparently incurable disease.
» * * •
Tasmania’s State revenue climbed up beyond
the million for the year 1907-8; but the effort was too
much for it, and it fell back exhausted. For the past 12
months the receipts totalled only £934,326. The Com-
monwealth refunds were £61,418 less ; but, apart from
railways, there was a small shrinkage nearly all round.
Expenditure figures still remain a dark mystery ; but it is
very well known that there is going to be another sub-
stantial deficit. In many respects Tasmania is the Awful
Example among Australian States. Out of its public debt
it has paid for more unproductive side-shows than any
other State has done. In 1908 the gross receipts from ail
its public works and services didn’t amount to as much
as the interest bill;—
Interest £366,855
Works and services .. 323,034
Deficit .. £43,821
But the working expenses amounted to £232;257 ; so that
the net revenue from works and services was £276,078
less than the total interest. From that amount the only
fair deductions are less than £25,000 on account of interest
on loans to public bodies and for telegraph construction.
The balance has to be met out of taxation ; and, as year
after year a deficit is shown, the position grows worse.
For of course a deficit simply means that, instead of rais-
ing enough money, things are squared by borrowing a
trifle at interest, and that trifle isn’t represented by
anything productive—it has gone to pay some of last
year’s salaries. There is only one possible end
for a State or any other concern that works on those
lines. Already over 40 per cent, of the total expendi-
ture goes in interest; and, apart from the Commonwealth
refunds, the State has not £75,000 left after paying
interest and working expenses of its railways, etc. The
figures for 1908 were—
Total revenue apart from Commonwealth re-
funds £711,015
Interest and charges £405,746
Working expenses, railway and other
public works 232,257
638,003
Available for all other purposes (Education,
Justice, Police, Public Health, etc., etc.).. £73,012
And as the States can’t rely upon what they have hitherto
received as Commonwealth refunds, simply because
the Common wealth’s own demands are yearly increas-
ing, and must go on increasing if we are to have
adequate defence, Tasmania’s outlook is shockingly bad.
But it persistently refuses to realise the fact, and goes on
drifting further and further into trouble. To put itself
on a reasonably sound footing it ought to tax itself, and
practise hard economy, until it has wiped out at least
£5,000,000 of its total debt of £9,754,000; for its works
can’t show a respectable return on any more than 4|
millions. Instead, however, of attempting to do any-
thing of that sort it deceives itself with weird sophistries,
exactly the same as the bank directors of Melbourne did
just before the awful smash that happened 17 years ago.

A.J.S. Bank shares, £1 paid, but carrying a
liability of £2 10s., have not been sold in the open market
for many a year, and there has been no regular quotation
for them. During the last four years they have been
given away with as little as 10s., and as much as £2. The
man who received £5O with 100 shares will now get 250
shares, which will thus cost him £2OO net. If they pay 3
per cent, he will get 3f per cent. on his investment.
The man who got £2OO with 100 shares will get 250 shares
for £5O, and under the same circumstances will get a
return of 15 per cent. Before the bank can pay 3 per
cent, on ordinary shares,it will have to earn—

The past half-year’s accounts really look a little better
than is shown in the last line above ; but the co. writes
£lO3 off furniture, etc. (about one-third of the book
value), and also pays £289 as a bonus to the staff. The
capital is £lO,OOO ; this and the reserves are represented
by premises, £12,000 ; investments (chiefly mortgages),
£6885; and book debts and cash. The premises return
about 8£ per cent, gross. This includes a charge of £3OO
a year for the co.’s own accommodation—which, by
the way, is not charged up among the expenses;
it is not shown, therefore, in the comparison printed
hereunder; and, on the other hand, the amount is, of
course, not included among the receipts from interest,

April, April, Oct., April,

Commissions .. £1,540 £2,788 £2,635 £2,634
Interest, rent, etc... 535 403 510 814

Profits p.c. of funds 10/12/7 12/7/4 10/17/4 10/5/3
Trust balances .. 103,234 538,644 1,073,392 1,122,838
The business is increasing pretty rapidly, and it con-
tinues highly profitable. Westralia is a State on which
the absentee has a considerable grip, and though that may
be had for the State it is good for this co. There is a
liability of £2 on the shares, which are 10s. paid ; but the
reserves now exceed the paid capital. Still neither one
nor the other is in a very liquid form, which might be
awkward if the co. ran against a serious snag. Snags,
however, don’t seem numerous in the track of Australian
trustee cos.
Still, few things are more certain than that
trustee cos. won’t always escape trouble. They know the
old snags, certainly, and may be able to dodge some of
them ; but the interpretation of wills is a ticklish busi-
ness—the tremendous number of equity suits based on
disputed interpretations is proof enough of that. While
property values are on the up-grade the trustee co. hasn’t
got much to worry about on that score ; but when a
slump comes, and beneficiaries only get £5OO where they
expected £lOOO, they not unnaturally look round for
somebody to cast a stone at. And since there are quite a
number of gents one who have no particular affection for
trustee cos., because those cos. have laid a large and grow-
ing hand on one of the ancient fat pickings of the
profession, there are always plenty to suggest that
the trustee co. should be the target ; and thus legal
questions are raised to shift the loss from the estate to
the trustee. So it happened that, after the Melbourne
boom, one of the over-numerous Victorian cos. lost
£130,000 in this or a very similar way. That experience
ought to sink very deep into the minds of Australian
trustees and the managers of Australian trustee cos. ;
also investors in trustee shares might do worse than give
it a thought now and again. The managers are power-
less to prevent these actions altogether ; and where there
are grounds for an action there are always possibilities of
an adverse verdict. But if they can’t prevent the actions
they can at least provide for them beforehand, by making
decent additions to reserves, and not the miserable trifle
that the Westralian co. puts by for the past term. And
a substantial addition is all the more called for because of
the rapid increase of business ; for it is obvious, as has
been repeatedly pointed out here, that the risks of a
trustee co. are not in proportion to its capital, but in
proportion to its business.

The selling wool-brokers of Sydney lately formed an asso-
ciation, which works in with similar bodies in Melbourne,
Adelaide and Brisbane, and among them they regulate
the supplies, preventing the market being over-crowded
at any onetime. This regulation is a good thing all round ;
the only sufferer is the speculator, who used to cut in
during a temporary glut. But along with this object the
Sydney brokers had another —to rearrange charges, so
that they would get a bit more out of the business when
wool prices are low, without getting any less when they
are high. However, this co. didn’t believe in any in-
creased charges at all, and wouldn’t come in ; so it goes
on playing a lone hand, as it has done for many years
past. And apparently it goes on playing successfully,
because for the past six years, at any rate, it has
every season increased the total of its sales.
The table above shows that for the past two years con-
siderable sums have been written off assets. It isn’t
clear whether the losses have been actually made, or
whether all or part has been put to a contingency
account in case a bad season should turn up. Appar-
ently the item which is liable to the depreciation is
“Sundry debtors,” now standing at £22,269. No hint is
given as to the character of these “ sundry debtors.” The
only other assets of any consequence are the buildings
(Phillip-street, Sydney, and Pyrmont); these stand in
the books at £40,740. As capital and reserves total
£45,023, or only £4283 more than the premises stand for,
it follows that the bulk of the “ sundry debtors ” has to
be financed with borrowed money. The earnings appear
to have been the best ever recorded :
1904. 1907. 1908. 1909.
Commissions, etc. £11,557 £20,190 £18,124, £20,926
Working Expenses 9,444 9,204 9,755' 11,030
Expenses p.c. of
corns 81’8 45-5 53 - 8 52’7
Besides these expenses, a small bonus is usually voted to
the staff. On the other hand, a bonus is paid to those
who provide the commissions : last year it amounted to
the usual rebate of 20 per cent. (30 per cent, was paid in
1907, but that was the boom year). The reserves, even if
there are none hidden in a contingency account, are now
very substantial; they have just shoved their nose ahead
of the paid capital—a fine performance, seeing that the
co. is still only 12 years old.

A new item appears in the balance-sheet, following the
reserves—“ New building account, £5000.” And on the
opposite side £5OOO has dropped into the fixed deposits.
The explanation appears to be that this co. owns Aarons’
Exchange Hotel, the lease of which expires next year ;
but a new 20-year lease has been let to Aarons’ Exchange
Hotel Co. on condition that it adds two storeys and makes
numerous improvements. The Hotel Co. estimated that
these things would cost about £5OOO, and apparently that
money has been put up, for payment presently to the
contractors. This may almost be regarded as a reserve,
though as nothing has been written off property account
for many years, some or all of it should probably be
applied to writing down this asset, which stands at
£70,000. The share capital of the Exchange Co. is £30,000 ;
and there is also £21,420 owing - on debentures. Con-
sidering the size of the reserves, the return on capital is
small; but the security is good. And, besides, the
Exchange isn’t exclusively a. money-making concern ; it
is a sort of club without liquor or food, where the Fat-
man can pour denunciations of Socialism into always-
sympathetic ears, and where the visitor can get
information about our great natural resources from the
merchants’ point of view.

In the previous year £2OO was appropriated for a bad-
debts fund ; but as it has disappeared it has now been
added to the amount written off as at April, 1908. The
past year has been disastrous. The season wasn’t too
good in Victoria, and the co-operative butter factories
which are interested in this wholesale co-operative con-
cern had a particularly bad time, it is said. But the real
trouble, as hitherto pointed out here, is that the financ-
ing has been bad. The co. started simply to do
co-operative marketing, which called for very little
capital ; but as it was getting along very
well, it launched out into freezing works and
butter-box making, and had to borrow the money to do
it. The accounts for this part of the business are kept
separate, and only the net result is transferred to profit
and loss account; and for the past year a deficit of £2359
is shown. The paid capital is only £3230, and the factory
and works have cost £19,473. Obviously this means a big
interest bill which has to be paid whether the season is
good or bad. Australian co-operators have still a lot to
learn. They very rarely give the people who manage their
concerns a fair chance. The thing is started, as a rule,
in a small, wheelbarrow sort of way ; and when it
grows till the business calls for a waggon at the very
least, the management still has to go on struggling
with the wheelbarrow. The only thing the management
can do most times is to hang on to profits, and thus get
the capital which the co-operators won’t put up other-
wise ; and even then they often have to do it in a furtive
way, by writing down assets heavily, because if they
showed the reserves, the co-operators would want to
collar them as a bonus. And yet the managements dare
not give the co-operators the straight talk they often
deserve, because, in spite of the great things co-operation
has done for them, crowds of them are prepared to go
over to the ever-waiting Private Enterprise middleman
on the smallest provocation.

Of the year’s profit £227, which has been passed to a re-
serve for renewals, etc., ought perhaps to be written off
to cover depreciation ; the only doubt in the matter arises
in consequence of £238 having already been charged to
Profit and Loss on account of “ improvement, mainte-
nance and repairs.” The co.’s factory has been declared
by the Government dairy expert to be under the standard
required ; hence, no doubt, the large additions made to
reserves. But as the present factory may have to be
more or less wiped out, it looks as though some of those
reserves will presently have to be wiped out too.
* * * *
Great Cobar, whose annual report ought to be
out from England about the end of this month, produced
450 cons copper and 1102 oz. gold during the past four
weeks ; but it still doesn’t say where the metal came
from. In the aggregate, though it has been getting ore
from the C.S.A. field and the Cobar Gold Mines, the
purchases can’t amount to much, comparatively speaking ;
but that ought to make the co. all the more ready to set
outthefacts. Theco., however, simply throws out its four-
weekly figures without a word of explanation ; even when,
early in April, it reported 510 tons of copper, it didn’t
say a word about the furnace bottoms that had been cleaned
up. Great alterations are still in progress at the mine
works, and managing director Kendall will hardly know
his furnaces when he sees them again. Evidently H. O.
Ballenger, the new manager, has more power than ever
late manager Blakemore was favored with. The changes
involve a very heavy expenditure, however, and the co.
had far too big a capital to struggle under even without
these additions. At Dec. 31, 1907, there was £555,836
owing to debenture holders ; and no doubt at the present
moment there is a heavy overdraft guaranteed by the
directors and supported by the unissued debentures
(£200,000). *
The search for the north and south continua-
tions of the Broken Hill main lode still goes merrily on,
and the same bald old lies continue to be dragged in by
their one remaining hair. “The cap of an immense lode,”
and “identical with the heavy cap of the Broken Hill
lode,” are two stock phrases to be seen on most of the
prospectuses. The people who make the comparison
probably never saw the real cap of the Broken Hill lode,
which was blasted off years ago. One of the latest
of these alleged fac-similes to be offered to the
British public is the Broken Hill North Blocks, which
was superficially prospected in the early days of the field,
and abandoned as a duffer. Of course, there is no con-
clusive evidence that the “immense lode” does not exist
somewhere below the surface, and speculators who have
faith somewhat larger than a grain of mustard-seed may
one day be rewarded for their enterprise in trying to find
it. The prospectus is, however, a marvel of presumption.
After stating that the insignificant ironstone excrescence
on the surface is evidently the cap of an immense
lode, the document explains that the work done on the
property consists of a costeen sft. deep and two shafts
20ft. and 38ft. deep respectively. From these, samples
have been broken, one of which assayed 46 per cent, lead,
16oz. silver, 3 per cent, copper, and a trace of gold.
The other sample lacked the gold; but it went
56 per cent. lead. Surely the investor doesn’t want
anything better than that! But that is not all.
The blocks adjoin the “B. H. North mine, peg and peg,
and extend north to the Silver Hill mine.” That
is about as good as the , case of the man who wanted
to raise a loan on the strength of being “ next door to
a millionaire.” The North mine is a good property to be
next door to ; but all the exploratory work dpne on the
north side of the boundary has failed to show any sign of
the continuation of the lode which the North mine is
working on, so the advantage of being next door on that
side isn’t very apparent. Then with regard to the assays :
samples of ore may be broken in a dozen abandoned out-
side show's that will assay high, but the trouble is that
there is not enough of the stuff to pay for the steel used
up in taking it out.
* * * *
There has been a great deal of talk on the part
of the Broken Hill Junction shareholders’ committee
about starting the mill. Such a proposal is utterly
ridiculous, at the present price of metals. It is quite
true that the assay values of the new western ore body
are good ; but when the ore comes to be put through the
mill, the chances are that it will not go any W'ay near
those figures. When the South Blocks mine was sampled
it showed about 22 p.c. lead ; but the mill average is only
16 p.c. Even under the very best mining conditions, the,
sample assay of ore broken in a crosscut or a drive can
very rarely be maintained when stoping is commenced
and the ore is sent in large quantities to the mill. When
the Junction mill was last working it was dealing with
ore assaying about 12 p.c. lead and 9oz. silver, and the
proportion of concentrates produced was one in eight.
At that time a loss was made with lead at £l6. Owing to
the way in which the Junction was knocked about in the
early days, it is like a rabbit-warren below, and ore-
raising there will be more expensive than on the other
mines until much money is spent in widening and
straightening the levels, putting down winzes, etc. That
being so, it Would be impossible to pull down costs
sufficiently to enable the mill to be worked at a profit
with lead at £l3, even supposing the grade of the ore
was raised to 14 per cent, lead and lOoz, silver —which is
as much as it would be for milling purposes.
■i * * *
Broken Hill South Blocks issues a memo, to the
effect that the profit over all expenditure for the past
three months has been : April, £1960; May, £2209;
June, £2190. Total for three months, £6359. That
would be at the rate of 4s. 6£d. per ton of ore,
£1 2s. 7d. per ton of concentrates, or £1 14s. 6d.
per ton of lead in the concentrates. The calculation of
profits is based on lead at £l3 and silver fine at 2s. 2d.
The contract with the B. H. Prop, provides for a return-
ing charge of £4 11s. per ton, with a rise of Is. 3d. per
ton for every £1 which lead goes beyond £l4, and a
fall of Is. 3d. for every £1 it gets below £l4. Allowing
for this, an improvement of £1 in lead would
have raised the profits for the three months to about
£9750 ; a fall of £1 below £l3 would, on the other hand,
reduce the profits to about £2BOO for the three months ;
and the profits w’ould disappear before £ll was reached.
The margiii is-none too wide—not wide enough to be
cheerful over. Costs are low, about 14s. 6d. per ton of
ore; but the silver contents are so wretchedly
small, averaging only 2£oz. per ton of ore.
The three mpnths’ profit was at the rate of £25,436 a
year. Deduct 10 per cent, depreciation, which would
amount to rather over £BOOO, and isn’t enough, and
£2436 for management and office expenses, etc., which is
also under the mark ; then £15,000 would be left. There
are 200,000 shares, and a profit of that size is only equal
to Is. 6d. per share. With the market at 335., it is quite
clear that probable future advances in metals, etc., are
already heavily discounted.

The Sulphide Corporation always has some
trouble on hand. Just now the Ore Concentration Co.,
owning the Elmore zinc process, has started an action
against it in Australia, alleging infringement of patent
rights. Upwards of half a million tons of Sulphide
Corporation tailings have been treated by the acid-oil
process, and the Elmore people want to collect royalty on
the lot. The two companies have been snarling at one
another for two years, but the fur has begun to fly now.

The erection of the new 300-ton Blast Smelting
Furnace for the Great Fitzroy mine, Q., is now rapidly
approaching completion at the mine. The dimensions of
this furnace are as follows—l6Bin. x 44in., and 24ft.
high to feed floor. It is water jacketed from top to
bottom. These jackets are made of steel plates flanged
in one piece. The furnace was designed by the mine
superintendent, and has many features of excellence and
novelty in the detail of its construction. The jackets are
made interchangeable, only one type and size of jacket
being used ; and they will further be constructed so that
they are reversible from top to bottom—thus practically
the life of the jackets is doubled. This furnace, as well as
the previous one, was built at the works of the Austral
Otis Engineering Company, South Melbourne.*

Of the various “Sarco” fuel-saving appliances,
for which Gibson, Battle and Co., Ltd., 7 Bent Street,
Sydney, and 128 William Street, Melbourne, are agents,
that in most general use is their 002 Recorder, which
has been installed in many factories and power houses.
This instrument speaks in’a very definite way against the
amount of fuel which is wasted in smoke, and a user
states that, by installing a “Sarco” Recorder, his fuel
consumption was reduced from 9751 b. to 8941 b. per
hour, although more power was actually being demanded
from the steam plant.*

* * * *
Those who looked for an increase in the Boulder
output will be disappointed. Addressing shareholders
from the chair, at the annual meeting, G. P. Doolette
said that the question had been carefully considered by
the Board, and, after consultation with the manager, it
had been “ turned down.” To largely increase the plant
would involve an outlay of anything from £lOO,OOO to
£150,000; and, even if they were willing to face that
expense, an attempt to force the mine beyond its present
output of 200,000 tons per year might lead to disaster.
He added that the present outlook indicated that the co.
would continue to distribute 3s. a year for many years to
come, thus making the security a good 12 per cent.,
investment at the present market price. But out of that
12 per cent, sinking fund has to be provided ; for even
the best of mines is a wasting asset.

Including pillars, the “ probable ore and broken
ore ” in the Great Fingall (W. A.) is now estimated by 8.,
M. and 00. at 240,000 tons, of a recoverable value of 345.
They make it clear, however, that the grade of the ore to
be handled must for some time be much less than the
above average, owing to the pillars which contain the
best stuff riot being available until the stopes have been
completely emptied. The 1908 accounts show a surplus
of £102,995 ; and after writing off depreciation of plant
£6270, depreciation of the co.’s interest in the Kooringa
Prospecting Syndicate £3608, and deducting £19,173 for in-
come and dividend taxes, £73,944 remains. Theco. brought
forward £81,643, so there is a total profit and loss balance
of £155,587. Dividends accounted for £131,250, leav-
ing'£24,337 to carry forward. This sum was further
reduced by payment on January 10 of interim dividend
No. 29, amounting to £12,500. Working costs, including
development, were Is. 3d. per ton more than in 1907. On
December 31 the co. stood in the following financial
position :—Assets other than mine plant and ore reserves :
Stores, £21,169 ; broken ore suspense account, £12,454 ;
shares in Kooringa Syndicate, £2890 ; debtors, £5580 ;
gold in transit (after deduction of advances and ex-
penses), £52 ; cash, £109,970. The total of this little lot
is £152,115. Liabilities: Sundry creditors, £18,032.
Surplus, £134,083. The co. is thus well ahead of it ;-and,
not being .able to effect heavy insurances on the life of its
Westralia property, it has scouts sniffing the ends of the
earth in search of a new one. Already the Board has
agreed to spend £70,000 in working the Tarquah Main
Reef on the West African Gold Coast, should prospects
warrant the completion of the option, and it is on the
look-out for other-investments.

There isn’t much fear of the Oroya-Brown Hill
(W.A.) making an early move into the workhouse. At
the end of 19Q8 it had £218,400 in ready cash ; £50,000
deposited in escrow for the purchase of La Leonesa mine
(Nicaragua); £8866 lent out at interest; £51,866in Oroya-
Black Range shares, valued at about £1 apiece ; £4126
worth of shares in other companies ; and £B5lB in stores.
Besides this it had the Kalgoorlie property, with its still
valuable plant and ore-reserves. The co. certainly owed
£15,681 at that time ; but this sum is a mere trifle when
ranged alongside its bundle of wealth. The year’s
receipts amounted to £183.610 ; and after allowing for all
expenditure and writing off £21,000 odd for development
and depreciation, £45,145 remains. Dividends and profits
on shares sold, less expenditure on properties prospected
and not taken up, netted £13,037. Take that and the
£9089 brought forward from 1907 and add them to the
mine profit, and an aggregate of £66,271 is got. Divi-
dends, taxes, etc., absorb £52,450, leaving £13,821 to
carry forward.
Vivien (W.A.) toils on. but doesn’t do much
good for itself. The co. started the last financial year
with cash £153, debtors £450, and creditors £6944. At
the close the cash had increased to £l7l, the debtors had
expanded to £420, and the creditors had shrunk to £1849.
Thus the excess of debts had been reduced during the 12
months from £6341 to £1258. Ore reserves are now esti-
mated at 50,343 tons, worth 28s. 9d. per ton ; but although
the management is all right, it is doubtful if theco. can
do much more than make ends meet on this grade.
* * * *
Marvel Loch (W.A.) is becoming notorious.
First of all, it tried to gaol its female secretary, and now
it has fallen foul of its local director. The latter has
resigned, and reported to the inspector of mines that the
show is unsafe, and is being worked contrary to the regu-
lations. He also asserts that a “ botch ” has been made
of the plant, and predicts in an ominous sort of way that
it won’t be long before shareholders are bowed down
with financial grief if the present system of working
continues.
Coolgardie Redemption (W.A.) held another
meeting in Kalgoqrlie the other day, when the auditors
reported that certain vouchers were missing, that the
share register was not complete, and that proper duty
stamps had not always been affixed to transfers, nor were
the transfers in order in other particulars. Some one
wanted to know what the Board was going to do about
these things ; somebody else asked how an item “ sundry-
expenses, £ll4 375. Id.” was made up ; while a third in-
quisitive person requested an all-round explanation. The
replies were not very convincing, and the limit was
reached when a shareholder asked for a copy of the
balance-sheet, and was told he could have one for ss.
The co. now owes a bit for wages and fuel, and its posses-
sions are understood to be mortgaged. The directors
have just tried to get an advance from the Minister for
Mines, but that gentleman wants a little more informa-
tion before he upends his money-bag. Probably he wants
to know, among other things, whether he will be charged
ss. for a balance-sheet too.
* * * *
The industrial agreement between the miners
and mineowners at Kalgoorlie expired on June 30 ; but it
holds good until one or both sides give a month’s notice
of retirement. Both parties, however, seem contented ;
and the industrial peace which has reigned in the district
since the agreement was signed two years ago doesn’t
look like being fractured.
* * * *
From mine-owner H. E. Rowe, on the subject
of searching the Victorian miner:—“ln ‘Wild Oat’
(B. 8/7/’O9) there is a paragraph which reflects with undue
severity on my recent speech to the Minister of Mines.
It appears to me to have been written under a misappre-
hension of the facts. The second sentence says that the
personal search of a miner would enable a ‘ mate ’ who
has a down on him to put him away. Now, that
is utterly impossible, No miner could put gold
into, say, the mouth or any other part of the person of
his mate without his knowledge. It is just possible,
though scarcely probable, that he could put it into his
pocket; yet, I understand, it isn’t the pocket search,
but the search of the person, that the miners
object to. In the old alluvial mining days personal
search was invariably adopted. It was taken as a
matter of course, as merely' a business precaution against
dishonesty, and no objections were raised. When alluvial
mining waned and quartz mining took its place, the cus-
tom was allowed to fall intodisuse, because it was thought
that in quartz mines there was no chance of gold being
stolen. The experience <>f the past few years has unfor-
tunately proved that thissupposition was quite erroneous.
The Chamber of Mines, the Bendigo Mine Owners’ Associ-
ation and the Ballarat Mine Owners’ Association, who
among them represent all the great mining interests in
this State, joined' in a deputation to ask the Minister
(amongst other things) to legalise the personal search of
miners ; but they only' want the right to search men of
whose honesty they are suspicious. As I was the first
speaker on that occasion, I have since been made the sub-
ject of a lot of personal abuse and vituperation ; but I
may say that the presidents of the Bendigo and Ballarat
Mine Owners’ Associations both pressed the same matter.
“ Referring to your other remarks, the Chamber
of Mines is aware that in some cases directors have
abused tbeir positions by selling tbeir own machinery
or stores to the mines, and by taking secret commissions ;
and it bas recently sent a letter to the Minister, asking
him to insert a section in the amending Mines Bill dealing
with this. If any companies, as you suggest, report a
less quantity crushed than actually goes through their
batteries, the Chamber has no possible check
on it. The only way' in which it could check it would
be to have three detectives on every mine
(one for each shift), and get them to count
every truck of quartz raised. This would be a truly
Herculean task, and then probably the mine managers
would resent it as an unwarranted imputation on their
honesty, and would order the detectives off the mines. I
believe, however, that the companies publish the true
tonnage. Ido not know one which does.not do so. It is
admitted that the passing of the Victorian Gold Buyers
Act has checked the stealing of gold ; but there is no
doubt that it is still going on to a very considerable
extent. This is proved by the quantities of gold sold by
buyers in the N.S.W. border towns during tbe past
12 months. Plentv of stolen gold can be bought
in Victoria now at from 40s. to 50s. an ounce provided no
questions are- asked. We look to The Bulletin to use its
powerful influence to help us to suppress the contempt-
ible crime of gold stealing ; and if you can show us any
means of doing so, other than the personal search of
miners, we shall be only too pleased to give it our most
favorable consideration. I would like to add that I did
not address the Minister in my' capacity- of Chairman of
the Long Tunnel Co., but as Vice-President of the
Chamber.”
Mr. Rowe’s letter puts his case no further ahead.
He admits that mine managers would probably resent a
check being put on their tonnages “ as an unwarranted
imputation on their honesty”; but an unwarranted
imputation on the honesty of a manager isn t
any more serious than one on the honesty- of a
miner. And if the miner, why not the direc-
tor? Directors visit mines; they are always shown
the choice specimens ; they very often finger the gold.
Mr. Rowe admits there are dishonest directors. Why
not, then, give shareholders the right to make a personal
search of directors as they leave tbe mine ? The Bulletin
deplores gold-stealing; but it wouldn’t stop it by picking
out one class of workmen (a fine, brave, hard-working,
ill-paid class on the whole) and fastening on to them an
indignity which people wouldn’t dream of fastening on
to other'classes—managers and directors, for example.
What this paper wants to do is to increase
respect of miners, as well as others ; and you can’t do
that by such measures as personal search.
* * * *
Daylesford (Vic.) is on the boom more than ever
as far as jobbers, brokers, and hangers-on of mining can
make it. Ajax pays its way-. So does Nuggety, but no
one boldly says how long the happy day-s will last. Ajax
has been judged by more experts than ever sat on Caesar’s
wife. Their verdict is from 255. to 40s. in sight, assuming
the mine to be worked in tip-top ptyle. Nuggety isn’t a
sure shot. A rich spur—or, as it is regarded locally, a
flat make—gives the best gold ; so there isn’t an expert
of any quality who doesn’t shirk the job of telling how
much stone is in sight, or the value of the mine from the
dividend standpoint. Nothing else at Daylesford is paying.
Vet float comes out on top of float. Plenty of contributing
shares ; of course, a whack of paids to the vendors, and a
mite of working capital. Brokers generally gobble up a
bit of the cash on the way. If the Stock Exchange re-
fused to quote a stock till the co. had been mining for 12
months, half the promotions would go by the board, and
the public would devote its spare cash to shows that are
not made specially for the market. Pitcher’s Reef is
the latest effort. Working capital under £5OO, of
which £lOO goes to pay for poppet legs and other trifles.
This is a revival of the Swiss Morent and Pitcher’s Reef,
which flickered out almost as soon as it was born, two
years back. This second advent promises to be about as
ill-favored as the first, despite the attempt to introduce a
new strain of blood.

to run a dredge at a greater cost than the average, and
as the Victorian average last year was £57 a week, the
first; Mitta Mitta dredging co. ought to be showing now
£5O a week profit.

Briseis (Tas.), including the Brothers Home
No. 1, puts up this record for the last four years :
1905. 1906. 1907. 1908.
Overburden (c. yds.) 551,548 519,262 523,800 076,000
Drift sluiced (c. yds.) 481,350 615,990 664,600 539,300
Tin metallic(tons).. 712 1,032 1,032 1,021
Briseis output (tons) 643 912 911 915
Net price metallic
tin (per ton) .. £149’6 £184‘5 £166 £133
Proceeds of tin .. £96,327 £168,123 £151,222 £121,815
Tin is a jumpy metal. Up £35 in 1906, down £lB in
1907, and back £33 in 1908. Even at £133 it is £3O above
what any safe, long-dated estimate ought to be based on.
When a dozen or so dredges are at work in Tongkah and
other parts of Siam, bringing out the reputed millions of
hidden tin, the fact may strike home with a solid thud.
New Brothers Home No. lis about done. Briseis proper
has 906,100 cubic yards to treat. Roughly, this is two
years’ work ; but only 650,000 cubic yards are in
the ancient drift from which the tin comes. Currie
and Lake estimate that at least 1000 tons of tin
remains, or 750 tons of metal. At £l3O per ton, this
means that £97,soogross is in the mine. Deduct £27 per
ton for expenses, and the total shrinks to £77,350. That,
added to £85,000 cash in hand, makes £162,350, or, say,
ss. in divs. The co. owns beyond the almost-exhausted
Briseis : (1) the Ringarooma ciaim, (2) Wallace’s dredging
sections in Victoria, and (3) the option over the Morinna
claim, near Derby (Tas.). At Ringarooma big progress is
being made with sluicing the overburden ; but neither
Currie nor Lindesay Clark is game to risk an estimate
of the tin there. At Wallace’s leases Clark estimates
that there are 19,300,000 cubic yards to be worked
at a profit of l£d. to 2Jd. per cubic yard. Assum-
ing this estimate to be correct, the co. has £160,000 more
profit to add here—say another ss. profit per share. As
the scrip is worth about 75., it would seem as if the
market discounts the Ringarooma estimates, or allows a
good deal for the risks of the metal market. If the Ringa-
rooma were to pan out even at 31b. of black tin per cubic
yard, scrip at the present rates would be very cheap,
because of the extent of the claim and the abundance of
cheap water-power available —always provided that
nothing bad happens to the tin market. But nobody
will guarantee that erratic factor in the calculation.

, The financial record of the Mungana Co. (Q.) for
the last few years is summed up in this: —

Of the net profit of £4lOO, however, £2989 is derived
from surpluses on ore shipped prior to June, 1908; still
it is a fair thing to count that in with the nine months’
revenue, as there may be surpluses to be received next
year from this year’s shipments. On the other hand,
although £11,1i.3 has been spent on development,
only £3876 has been charged to profit and loss;
in other words, if all the money spent on the mine
during the nine months had been charged against the
revenue, the gross profit would have been reduced from
£8214 to £977. The result of piling up development as
an asset in this way is that it now stands in the books at
£69,287 ; and whether it is worth that or nothing at all
only the future can tell. It certainly is worth nothing
at all if it cannot lead to profits, for a shaft obviously
isn’t of any value unless there are dividends at the bottom
of it. The co.’s surplus of so-called liquid assets on
March 31 was £7491 ; but £2649 was represented by stores
and firewood, which are only worth that much to the co.

Some past Hampden Cloncurry ore estimates
have been so much of a joke that those which are put
forward now don’t sink very deep into many minds. The
situation is, however, alleged to be :
Hampden. Duchess. Total.
Reserves, July, 1908 .. 10,636 20,024 30,660
~ Feb., 1909 .. 69,034 19,024 88,058
Hampden ore averages 7’4 per cent, copper, and the
Duchess 15 - 6 per cent. ; and the total value, on the basis of
copper at £55, is £443,709. The later developments at the
Duchess are reported to have been good ; and as the rail-
way is rushing along now (this co.’s contribution to the
cost is £40,000), there won’t be much longer any excuse
for delay in proving whether the mine is merely an
expensive duffer or not. The Duchess is, no doubt, all
right, even if it isn’t a big thing ; but as to the Hampden,
though it has been 10 years before the public, very little
is really known as yet, notwithstanding that almost
enough money has been frittered away on it to pay for a
Dreadnought.

Mills’ United, Charters Towers, crushed 1370
tons for £5289, and a clean up of the plates gave £6566 ;
total, £11,855. No. 13 level is being driven east again
beyond the 843 ft. mark. A reef more or less mineralised
has been followed, but nothing decidedly payable has
been struck. The reef cut in the New Brilliant Freehold
No. 2 crosscut is not such a good thing as was at first
believed ; and so the market feels sick. In sinking below
the Brilliant formation a number of small reefs and
leaders were cut, but none can compare with the Day
Dawn reef. No. 2 east (old No. 8) is out 382 ft. from Mills’
United boundary, and, as this level makes eastward, it
seems probable that it will have quite a number of event-
ful changes. No. 2 hasn’t been anything like so good as
No. 1, although rises connecting the two levels have
proved some payable stone. The output of stone should
increase now the regrading of crosscut and No. 1 level
has been finished.
Charters Towers is becoming interested again
in the Brilliant Extended’s No. 3 underlay shaft. This is
being punched down below No. 18, and at last report the
bottom showed 3£ft. of light mineral stone. No. 17 east
has been driven 391 ft., with 2ft. of stone on the hanging
wall and Ift. on the footwall. The cyanide plant is closed
down, to allow of alterations ; when the job is finished it
should be able to deal with all the tailings. Brilliant
Central crushed 470 tons for 3250 z. A few blocks of
ground on the hanging wall and footwall branches of the
Brilliant are being worked, but the future of the mine
bangs on the 1175 ft. formation. A rise has been put up
from where the mineral stone was met in the crosscut, and
has connected with the shaft. This reef has occasionally
shown good prospects. Brilliant St. George and Brilliant
Block continue to sink in hopes of striking the Day
Dawn reef. There has been some speculation in the latter
co.’s shares, as the sink is now more than 1800 ft. from the
surface ; besides, there is a chance of the Brilliant Deep
reef being found in the crosscut.

Director H. Jones, whose jam brand gave
Tongkah its first name —the I.X.L. Syndicate—has been
talking to shareholders in Sydney and Melbourne. Of
course he says that the prospects are tremendous, as
they very likely are, and he mentions that later on 50,000
new shares will probably be issued to shareholders at a
premium to pay for more dredges. First, however,
Engineer Bluck, of Araluen (N.S.W.), who has been ap-
pointed consulting engineer, will go up and report as to
the right sort of machine. Bluck came to Australia with
a good record as a dirt-shifter in connection with the
Manchester ship canal, although afterwards he did give
his blessi..g to the Araluen Steam-Shovel enterprise,
which is now very dead. The June output (35 tons)
brought in about £2BOO to the co. ; and as the total
output to that date was about 81 tons, it is
fairly clear that up to then the working profit hadn’t
reached £6soo—which makes the recently announced
dividend (£7500) look more premature than ever. The
capital is already £150,000 ; and before adding to it the
co. ought to have at least one dredge available to test the
harbor bottom outside the small dock and channel patch
to which the present dredges have to be kept in terms of
the concession. The idea of testing the area, by putting
down one bore to every 10 acres—a work which has now
been decided on —is almost ridiculous.
Following on the reports that engineers are
sending down about Tongltah, something like a Siamese
dredging boom appears to be setting in. C. H. James, of
8., M. and Co.'s staff, formerly of the Fitzroy (Q.) and now'
of the Burmah Mines, has two or three months’ leave,
which he will put in looking up some concessions he
has in view. His backers are the Straits Concessions
Syndicate, of Adelaide and elsewhere. Two other
engineers, Rae and Thomas, have gone out for the
Malaya Tin Dredging Prop., a Melbourne concern. Capt.
“ Teddy” Miles and Joo Tok, the Siamese who.is on the
Tongkah Board, are at the front of the Siam Kedah, a
mostly-Hobart S3'ndicate; and there are two other
syndicates, with headquarters at Hobart and Broken >
Hill.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“ Jimmie Pannikin ” : Foxes are going to
be a great trouble ere long West o’ Darling. They
have already found their way to the Broken Hill
district. Some . Outback squatters regard the
smelly little beast as a disguised blessing, because
he is thinning out the rabbits ; but, when bunny
is scarce, the imported curse will sample lamb ;
and then his very thin disguise will be pene-
trated. Already, in some parts of Further-
Out, the foxes are in thousands. Possibly in the
near and nasty future Abdul Sooghee Bhagg
will learn to sing “John Peel,” and go fox”
hunting on his riding camel.
A DISTINCTION.
THE SAINT: “Mr. MacPherson, you arc rich m
worldly goods, but are you rich in saving
grace?”
MACPHERSON: “ Aweel, Ah’m rich through a
saving deesposition.”
“Yarrum”: About 20 years ago, old
Murphy, after lighting his cutty, dropped what
was left of the lucifer into a dry tussock at
Mungerarie, on the Araluen side of Moruya
(N.S.W.). In 25 minutes, Gundary, eight miles
away, was in a blaze, and was something to
behold. That afternoon I learned how bush-fires
can travel: I saw a lighted limb fall from a
stringybark, and, carried by the wind across a
gully, I watched it flung into a gum-tree 170yds.
away. A newly-married couple, upon whose
rented house the fire was rushing, carried their
furniture out and stacked it carefully on a hill.
Then the fire took a turn and licked up that bit of
furniture, leaving the rented place intact. I
managed to get my leg broken that night, besides
meeting with other misfortunes too numerous to
mention.
“M.” speaks
A pet land-monopolist argument against the graduated
land-tax proposals of the Fisher party is the ruin such
a measure would bring, indirectly, on the raising of high-
class stud sheep. The big statir n-owner has been argu-
ing for years that the small man can’t emulate the breed-
ing feats of Widgiewa, Collaroy, and other unwieldy
expanses of country. Yet a grand champion at the
recent Sydney show was bred by one Merriman, who
holds less than 5000 acres. One result of having 100
sheep-farmers instead of one squatter, would be that
this country could carry about twenty times as many
sheep. On smallholdings, subdivided into small paddocks,
the sheep would be better provided for, and not swept
away by the first drought that comes along, as is gener-
ally the case with the squatter who has bitten off more
than he can chew’. And these thousands of extra farmers
would bring the population to start new factories, and
absorb Australian wool in Australia. They would mean
good times generally all round. The increased population,
and demand would also be ample justification for pro-
hibiting the export of our best sheep to South Africa to
build up rival industries there, as is being done by scores
of squatters to-day. Sylvan, one of the finest sheep bred
in the Commonwealth, and winner of the “Grand Cham-
pion ’’ at the Sydney show last year, is now in South Africa
with thousands of others of our best, monuments all to
our large landholders’ short-sightedness and lack of
patriotism.

“ Silas Snell ” : Young Sutton took over
Colliver’s selection, between the properties of
Yadd and Beenly at Carryup, and set to work with
tremendous energy to knock itintoshape. Carryup
watched his noble efforts with keen interest.
In fact, a fair proportion of the population of the
surrounding waste sat on logs, and spent hours
narrowly supervising the young man’s work.
Sometimes a sprinkling of the daughters of
Carryup occupied the logs ; but it did not concern
Sutton—he just hurried on, chopping, digging
delving, fencing, or building, for George had
started the erection of a two-roomed shingle
house in front of the bark-and-log humpy that had
been Colliver’s home. When the house was fin-
ished Sutton took a trip to town. He
returned two days later. Several men of
Carryup were on the siding when the
young fellow stepped off the train. He
handed a woman out. Yadd stepped up, gaping
like an empty tank. “My wife,” said George,
tersely. “Wha-a-t?” Yadd almost yelled the
query. “My wife. I was married yesterday,
up town.” “Your wife!” cried Yadd. “His
wife! ” said Yadd, addressing his fellow-
residents. “Well, if that ain’t a d—d cheek,
arsk me. His wife ! Been up to town for a
wife. Nothin’ here good enough for him—not
your girls. Beenly, nor my girls, ’n’ I’ve got five
of ’em. Goes t’ Mel bin fer his wife. No con-
sideration fer us ’n’ ours.” “ S’pose I can get
a wife where I like?” said Sutton, angrily.
“ Yes,” roared Yadd, “ yeh can ! Yeh can go up
t’ town fer yer wife; an’, damn me, yeh can go up
t’ town fer yer onyins too. Not another penn’orth
will yeh get from me.” Since coming to Carryup,
Sutton had bought one sixpen’orth of onions from
Yadd.

“ L’Fou ” : It is easier to make laws than
enforce them. Example: The Victorian Dairy
Inspection Act. Defendant is a dairy
farmer, whose name isn’t really Black.
Dairy inspector, accompanied by Gov-
ernment vet., calls to inspect Black’s
herd, etc. The vet. for a start, and to
try to ensure a full roll-call, inquires
how many cows Black is milking.
Now, Black is milking 46 ; but he thinks
hard, counts on his fingers, and answers
42. Then, at the request of the official,
he goes to yard them for inspection.
En route, however, he side-tracks into a
scrubby gully (a) cow that “looks as
if she had something th’ matter with
her ” ; (6) two tuberculous cows, which
are dangerous sources of infection to
Black’s whole herd and even his neigh-
bors’ herds—also to Black’s family if
they should be rash enough to use
their milk instead of sending it to the
factory; (c) cow, with well-developed
cancer, praying dumbly for a bullet. It
doesn’t strike the cocky in his blind-
ness that the vet. might give valuable
advice gratis about curable diseases;
whilst, as regards the incurable, the
cows are generally a dead loss in them-
selves, and a menace to purse as well
as health. However, since inspectors
cannot be expected to tramp all the
gullies, this inspector departs none the
wiser, and Black retains his diseases.

Australia’s “roaring days” are not all
done. The Never-Never correspondent of the
Southern Cross (W.A.) Times records these
stirring events in that way-out mining camp :
Only lour fights took place at Never-Never whilst the
Kev. Bray was holding Divine service there. “ The
Lizard ” and Micko Walsh left off their game of two-up to
join in the chorus of that beautiful old hymn, “Love
One Another.” David was very tactful, but it takes
more than tact to umpire four fights, gather in a church

bar till, and show interest in a church service at one and
the same time. Napoleon might have managed it, but—
he’s dead.
ON THRIFT.
“As for all this pinchin’ and savin', I think sometimes there ain't no use in it. An ’ I feel
inclined to chuck it all up and go the pace—like the rest."
“Sr” : “Jimmie Pannikin” (B. 3/6/’O9)
reckons he has discovered a bush freak in the
shape of a window in a chimney ; and he inquires
Whaffor? “Jimmie” cannot take much notice
of smoke flues, or he would have seen many a one
like that before to-day. The window isn’t put
there out of obscure cussedness, but for the simple
purpose of lighting the darkness over an oven or
stove. The recess of the chimney is dark, and, as
a rule, little light from the windows gets near the
fireplace opening, wherefore the cook spills much
good candle grease in finding out whether the
chop is burned or the spuds cooked. So the
window trick is now largely practised by archi-
tects.

“ Jacq.” : Since kangaroos have been pro-
tected in Victoria they have become
very tame and inquisitive, and will
feed out of the lunch-basket. Last
winter I was splitting palings in the
Upper Murray district. I used to
carry a rifle into the bush with me, in
the hope of getting a shot at a fox.
One day, as I was eating my lunch,
I saw a ’roo come down and regard
my rifle, which was leaning against a
tree, with profound curiosity. He
hopped round and round, sniffed it,
eyed it doubtfully, and then fear-
lessly put a hind paw on the trigger-
guard and tried to look down the
barrel. He succeeded in looking
down the barrel. Then his paw
slipped and caught the trigger, with
the result that there was another
good ’roo gone. After two kicks
and a spasm he lay as dead as
Freetrade, with the rifle on top of
him. I skinned the beast, took the
skin horn'', and pegged it out. A
few day-- .ater, the constable, who was
not a t'articular friend of mine, came
out collecting statistics, and as we
strolled round, counting the pigs and
hens, he noticed the skin, and re-
marked that it must have belonged to
a big wallaby. Going over to it, he
discovered the facts of the case. Of
course, I was prosecuted, and the
two local beaks, one of whom was a
notorious cattle-duffer, refused to
believe my story, spoke of the grow-
ing prevalence of perjury, fined me
£2 with costs, and confiscated the
skin.
“ F.C.” : I trust I am properly thankful
for the variegated information vouchsafed me in
answer to my query re shade trees ; but I cannot
say I am much further ahead. “Pick’s” ideal
shrub, the booby-aloe, would seem to meet the
case; but unfortunately I have no time to wander
round “ the South Coast,” wherever that may be,
gathering baby booby-aloes, and the Melbourne
Botanical Garden authorities, though they pos-
sess some ansemic twigs answering to that name,
could not enlighten me to any extent. I was
a trifle mashed on the currajong, advocated
by “XXX” and “Don,” until “San” chipped
in with his learned dissertation about various
kinds of “kurrajongs”— hibiscus lieterophyttus,
h. splendens, sterculia diversifolia, s. buckjumperus.
and so forth —then I became flurried. “San”
says the kurrajong grows most luxuriantly on a
bleak limestone or basalt ridge; if “San”
wouldn’t mind carting such a ridge—not to be
more than two chains long and one wide —to the
paddock I wish to ornament, I will cheerfully
bog in and grow a whole forest of “ sterculia of
noble dimensions. ” As for ‘ ‘ Leo Lear, ”c 1 ■ v iously
a Bananalander, I have some slight acquaintance
with the Moreton Bay chestnut, the ditto fig and
the Port Jackson fig ; but I wanted a shrub that
wouldn’t want a whole acre to each tree, and
would reach years of discretion inside a century.
So I am reluctantly thrown back on my first
choice, the Queensland sugar gum (I don’t know
its trade name, but I can get it from almost any
grower in Victoria); though if anybody has any
further information to offer, I will accept it in a
subdued and respectful spirit.
“C. 5.,” from Shepparton (Vic.), where
the floods have been :
The recent leakage in the big overhead water tank
caused the Goulburn River to overflow its banks, arid the
country for two or three miles west of the town has been
about 6ft. under water. With a couple of pals and a boat
I put in two or three hours chivvying the rabbits, which
had been driven on to log's, etc., to escape the wet death ;
and we had some sport of a kind not to be had once in
ten years in this district, and never in most districts. As
we drew near a log on which a bunny was keeping his
feet dry, he would leave it with a bound varying from 6ft.
to 12ft. The little beast can do 50,yds. in good time in
the water as well as on land, but tires soon after covering
that distance. Apart from his slackened pace, bunny
always informs you when his petrol is giving out,
by a roll of his hindquarters and a bob of his
tail. One old boy, when tiring, climbed 6ft. up a tree that
IRRIGATING THE DARK INTERIOR.
The impossibility of irrigating the dark interior stands
as a barrier to extensive settlement in Australia. — Eng-
lish Critic.
“ Impossibility of irrigating the dark interior!”
What nonsense.
rose direcbfrom the water line, before tumbling: back ; and
another ascended a tree about 20ft. to the first floor, but
was assisted by an oar prodding his posterior and a slight
bend in the back of the tree. I climbed the tree after
him ; but he didn’t hesitate about diving into the water
from that height; he evidentlv considered a wet skin
better than no skin at all. Ai'J he was right, for he beat
us in the end. He was a Ti. jmarck for tactics. As we
drew near him he feigned weariness, and we easy-oared,
making sure he was ours ; but when an arm was stretched
out to grab him he darted away in a different direction
and escaped, as it was difficult to turn the boat owing to
the current and the number of trees. He deserved to
live if any pest ever did.
A MEETING OF SOCIALISTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A long 1 discussion took place on a proposal by the Rain-
bow Agricultural Society to request the Railway Com-
missioners to allow a 50 per cent, rebate on profits earned
by special show trains.
Mr. D. Smith, who responded for the Legislative
Assembly, said he saw by the business-sheet that the con-
vention was going to appeal to the Government for this
and that. Well, that was quite right.
Mr. Sternberg, responding for the Legislative Council,
hoped that legislation would not be passed to hamper or
deter the men who had been all their lives on the soil.
- Melbourne Argus on Farmers' Convention.
Now the stout Socialists who grow the grain,
Gather and plot in conference again
How best to wring the last blade from the earth,
And get a rebate on the special train.
In order that our Jane may go in silk,
The cow must be induced to give more milk.
(So Jones, of Gippsland, counsel takes of Brown,
And Smith, and Green and others of that ilk.)
As tenderly as any clinging vine,
The farmers round the public purse entwine ;
They comfortably lean upon the State,
Leaving no room for your elbow—or mine.
Oil ! would that I had been a husbandman
When with this world to wrestle I began!
My only need to holler in the waste
For Socialism ! ’Tis a simple plan !
The primary producer is the one
Who fells the forest—gets up with the sun,
Milks cows, and ploughs, and tells the M.P. that
The country by the town is being done.
The brand of logic that these farmers use
The plain taxpayer tendeth to confuse ;
And, if in argument they are engaged,
Straightway their tempers do they rashly lose.
They cry : “ We heed not folks of any sort.
Of this fair land we are the main support 1
So, lest it fall, tee bid you hold us up 1 ”
To which Old Euclid hath left no retort.
The farmer shouts he is the good backbone
Of ev’ry nation. Freely do I own
He is a largish part. (But first-class spines—
Like props and walking-sticks—can stand alone I)
Freetrade’s his easy creed—and the good State
Must aid him when he calls. Early’or late
The city-worker, single-handed, fights
And pays his road tax and his water rate !
The pioneers that take the forest down,
Like good scene-shifters by the sun burnt brown,
When the work’s done put on their Sunday clothes,
And do the same sad office for the town.
N.S.W.

SAID TO RELIEVE ALL RHEUMATISM.
This simple prescription is said to perform remarkable
cures, in many cases after years of suffering.
Tincture Gentian Compound, one ounce; Syrup
Rhubarb, one ounce ; Liquid Barkola Compound, one
ounce ; and Syrup of Ginger, five ounces.
Mix by shaking' well in a bottle, and take in teaspoonful
doses after each meal and at bedtime. These ingredients
can be obtained at a moderate cost from any good
chemist, and are harmless, and will not injure the most
delicate stomach. ,
It has a peculiar effect upon the kidneys, assisting
these most important organs to filter and strain from the
blood the uric acid, urea, etc., which cause the pain and
misery of rheumatism. As a blood cleansing agent and
system tonic it is of unusual merit.*
When Autumn comes with golden ripening sheaf,
With glow’ring skies, and days becoming brief,
If from asthmatic pains you’d find relief,
Take Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure.
When Winter’s chills are at our very door,
And daily falls of rain give mud galore,
If consequential ailments you’d ignore,
Take Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure.*

“ W.D.” : Following are names of some
stations on the railway line from Perth to
Albany (W.A.). To a stranger they are rather
confusing :
Youraling, Kulyaling, Karping, Popanyinning, Your-
naning, Woodanilling, Moodjebing, Katanning, Perrin-
[rillup, Tambellup, Tingerup, Pootenup, Kendenup,
(Jarbarup, Malikupp, Marbellup.
The wholesale drowning of rabbits in the
recent Murray Valley floods should knock some
of the gas out of a sect of nature students who
foretell weather by the conduct of birds, beasts,
and reptiles. There is no reason why birds,
beasts and reptiles should know more of the
weather of next month, or the month after
next, than men know. On the contrary, they
seem to know less. Droughts and floods repeat-
edly kill them in legions. If they knew as much
about future weather as man, they would have
crowded him off the planet long ago. Man is able
to compile histories, and reason from analogies,
and provide against visitations ; and, unsatisfac-
tory as his conclusions may be when looking
months ahead, his knowledge is still a long way
before that of the rabbit or his kin.
« * * *
“ Hassam ” : “ Yarrum ” (B. 24/6/’O9) de-
clares that curses go with whiskers. Passing the
unkind inference as regards Editor'Ward, E. W.
Cole, and other heavily-timbered Australian
fauna, I humbly beg leave to differ. One of
the finest exhibitions of brilliant all-round the-
wieket cursing that I remember was that of a
friend of mine—a beardless boy of 18 who had
gone down to the sea in wind-jammers and studied
under the professorship of a Scotch mate, also
whiskerless. The occasion was a fishing ex-
cursion; the place, /Watson’s Bay, Sydney.
A troop of anthropoid persons from Surry
Hills had fouled our lines with an erratic
starboard oar, and my friend let go. One of the
hoodlums got in a weak obscenity edgeways, and
the resultant flow of rich Oriental tapestry knocked
him down on his seat. As the time wore on, the
dazed and admiring larrikins waited, with their
eyes bulging out, for the youthful orator to repeat
himself; but he never did. The pilot steamer
heeled over before the blast, and dead fish came
to the surface. After it was over, the one survivor
of the anthropoids rowed the corpses of his erring
brothers to the pier. I applauded the artist.
“ That ! ” he said. “ You ought to hear
old McDougal w'hen he lets go ! ” After
this, “Yarrum’s” samples fade into insignifi-
cance. Nor was the exhibition “only mud.’'
The score was from clean, crisp, vigorous hitting,
compiled in under 10 minutes, and included 40
boundary hits, and seven sixers. No ! —whiskers
are not necessary. My friend is now a first mate,
and still whiskerless. He swears with equal
fluency in Chinese, Spanish, Hindu, and Con-
necticut. For technique and variety I will back
him against anv of nine whiskered blasphemers
of the South Coast, the Castlereagh butcher, or
the Monaro champion, for £2500 a side.
A CASUIST.
THE TOUCHER (conscientiously): “If I didn't take 'em, probably they'd fall into the
hands of a thief.”
“ Pick ” : If “ Dandalup ” and “Milky
White ” have not yet ceased clutching each other’s
throats over their fire-sprint argument, I desire to
arbitrate between them. Firstly, wind is an
important factor in the bush-fire business, and
flames that scurry along at about “ Dandalup’s ”
fastest record with a hot westerly, will suddenly
flag to “ Milky White’s ” bullock-team gait when
a cool southerly buster turns up. Heat creates
heat, and that truisnj explains the whole thing.
On a sweltering summer day, fire will flv across
country where there is apparently nothing to
burn, and; will spring across roads and creeks as if
the air itself were alight; but on a cold, damp
. day the moisture-laden atmosphere exerts a lower-
ing effect on the spirit of the fire-fiend. Also,
where there is inflammable material: like , dry-
grass, stringybark or certain gaseous,leaves in the
forefront of a fire, the flames go ahead in gigantic
leaps ; but they proceed tardily and incline to die
out when confronted by green herbage or foliage.

The Cow-Worshippers of Bemboka (N.S.W.)
declare that the visitation of Hell that descended
upon them in.’o4 swept along at the rate of three
miles in as many minutes, the flames appearing to
fall in a sheet from the mountains into the valley
below; and the same summer, at Cobargo
(N.S.W.), I saw the Devil-element taking tree
after tree in a sparsely-wooded and ring-barked
paddock wherein there was nothing but the dead
timber and an occasional ’possum to be scorched.
Verdict for “ Dandalup.” No award as to Costs.
* * * *
“No. 3”: Can sympathise with “D.M.W.”
in his trouble about the growing propensities of
pumpkins. When I first went “on the land,” I
tried an experimental plot of them, and, as far as
growing was concerned they were a great success.
Oh, yes, they grew all right; for when I went to
see how they were getting on, they had grown out
of the paddock, crossed a river, and were well on
their road to B -, a distance of nine miles. It
was only by returning for a horse that I was
enabled to reach the end of them that day. Hav-
ing done so, I started them off again in the direc-
tion of iny estate. Inspected the position of
affairs in the morning, and found that they had
all reached home with the exception of one that
had wandered on to the railway line and been
killed by a passing train.
“ Gundy ” : Allow me to put a wedge into
the controversy re tree-felling and splitting (B.
17 and 24/6/’O9). I have contended with various
brands of our vegetables, and I don’t object to
any of them—for a consideration. There is some
difference, though, between battling with a stubby
old red-gum on the river flat and with the lofty
woolly-butt which grows on the mountains. Good
wages can be made splitting the latter into posts
or rails at £1 per 100 at the stump, all the tools
required being a saw for cutting up, and two axes
to do the rest, splitting included. When a log is
sawn off you stick an axe into the end, which
springs open slightly; follow, up the crack with
the other axe, and the first one falls free, to be
used again. Red gum is worth about £3 a hun-
dred to split into posts. Blasting powder is
mostly used for halving the logs, and the rest can
be done with maul and wedges. Never heard of
a cocky who wanted red-gum posts for £1 a hun-
dred. A splitter’s chief occasions for profanity-
are when the falling tree becomes entangled in
one or two useless neighbors, which must also be
brought to grass ; or when the trunk settles down
suddenly and jambs the saw. This often means
chopping the saw out and starting afresh, after
you have used up a lot of useful breath in violent
language.
THE ENEMIES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Australia, British ship, which sailed from a Mexican
port for Sydney seven months ago, has been posted miss-
ing at Lloyd’s. —News item.
R.M.S. Australia wrecked at Port Phillip Heads (Vic.).
— History.
The sea-god, blue, terrific,
The hidden, swimming foe
Who rules the wide Pacific
And all the winds that blow,
Hath cursed the ships that wear it
With pride on bow and stern,
And through all dangers bear it,
Men’s daily bread to earn.
The reef, black-fanged and deadly,
That lurks where sea-ways meet,
Foams when the good light, redly,
Guides well her tripping feet;
With haze the sea-god blinds it —
The bright, cliff-posted guide—
Till morn a traitor finds it
Above a traitor tide.
Afar upon their journey
The cloud-winged vessels fade,
Nor dream that for a tourney
The powers are all arrayed;
Like butterflies to battle,
Right daintily they float,
But in the block’s soft rattle
Death sounds its warning note.
He preys in ev’ry season,
Our ocean enemy ;
We can but guess the reason
For his great jealousy-;
Mayhap in bygone ages
This land —its hills and plains—
’Neath seas where now he rages,
Made part of his domains.
The cliff-edge swiftly crumbles
And, thro’ the gale’s fierce scream,
The landslide roars and rumbles,
So they that lie and dream
Rise from their rest affrighted
With faces drawn and pale,
For stormers’ shouts excited
Are sounding in the gale.
A gauge to that vast Power
Is ev’ry ship that sails ;
The giant waves that tower
White-lipped above their rails,
Like robbers seeking plunder
In some secluded way.
Spy ere they burst in thunder
And spring upon their prey.
With mist and shoal new-moulded,
With current, reef and gale ;
By coasts with fog enfolded,
All ships doth he assail;
But all his strength horrific
With age-long hate he flings,
When on the wide Pacific
Australia spreads her wings.
N.S.W.
E. FRANCIS.

POLITICAL HAPPENINGS.


FUSED FINANCES.
Treasurer Forrest: "Well, we'll have to get to work on the Financial Statement. What are your ideas?"
Alfred the Affable: "Well, these are the leading features of the case. We're pledged not to raise money by a land-tax. We've not to on any
more duties. We've promised to be liberal with the State Government. We've offered Britain £2,000,000 for a Dreadnought, and teh country seems to
object to us floating a loan. Now, you've got the money and the unpaid bills before you, and with these few points to guide you you should get on very well."
