PERSONAL
ITEMS
JOHN VERRAN, M.P., leader S.A. Labor
party, and a quaint Cousin Jack.
Daniel Prentice, one of the men who
helped to make Burns, Philp and Co. the big
concern that it is to-day, died a week or so back.
After James Burns had called in “Bob’’Philp
to do the affable, and thus help to extend the
young business, two of their first lieutenants were
Daniel Prentice and Adam Forsyth. The former
first managed the business at Cairns, and after-
wards at lownsville, where the headquarters were
in the beginning ; and he was kept in that billet
until a few years ago, when he was called down to
Sydney, practically as a pensioner, and appointed
a head-office director. “Bob” Philp has long
been out of the business; but Adam Forsyth
remains the dour right-hand man of the managing
Colonel.
The “King of AVaiou ” is again giving
trouble. Some 70 years ago, a loud-voiced U.S.
mariner was camped at Coromandel (M.L.), and
when the British took possession be offered his
own unsupported testimony to the effect that he
was the legal owner of almost all the country
thereabouts, partly through purchase and partly
through marriage with the daughter of a leading
cannibal described as Hooknose. He got some
land, but not the huge province he demanded,
and his descendants are still clamoring for
£500,000 compensation. The U.S. Committee on
Foreign Affairs, whose manners are not those of
the best society, has twice decided, on the unsup-
ported statements of the heirs of Hooknose’s son-
in-law, that the claim is just; and once it
threatened “ reprisals ’’—bombardment, perhaps
unless the cash was forthcoming. Now the row
has become so violent that a Maoriland law-official
has gone to England to see what it is all about.
“Liber ” gives a send-off to a bookworm ;
This week’s obituary includes J. H. B. Curtis, aged 80,
secretary ot the Melbourne Athenaeum. A worthy gentle-
man, universally esteemed, and profoundly learned in
Greek, Latin, French and Italian. He was one of Cape’s
boys in Sydney, and kept a school himself afterwards.
There is a general impression that J. H. B. was once a
Catholic priest. Anyhow’, he studied for it, and was inti-
mately acquainted with Rome. The Athenaeum was
founded over 60 years ago as the Mechanics’ Institute.
Early secretaries were Paterson and Gilbert, succeeded
by W. Thompson, father of the City Treasurer. When
W. Thompson passed out there were 150 candidates,
Garnet Walch got the billet, and he caused the mildewed
place to hum with new members. Whose fault it was
that Garnet left I don’t know'. Curtis followed, and held
on for a whole generation—3o years. He was even too
old in 1879—a square peg in a round hole. You don’t
want the learned pundit as secretary of an
Athenaeum! Curtis had w’on his spurs by showing some
energy as secretary of the Early Closing Association ;
but he never energised as Athenaeum secretary. The
place steadily waned, and three years ago it really seemed
that there was nothing for it but burial. Then came
salvation by the biograph—hall rented every night; entre-
preneurs tumbling over one another for it. Yet the read-
ing room continues usually a collocation of half a dozen
venerable and snoozy Struldbrugs, like those who hung
out in Luggnagg. Arcadian Cole’s second-hand depart-
ment is a Paradise to the library.
Denny, M.L.A., once sat with Kingston
in the Liberal interest for West Adelaide, but was
defeated by the block Labor vote in 1905. At the
declaration of the poll on that occasion he said he
saw he must join either the Labor party or the
National Ass. in order to get a seat. He did join
the Labor party, and was re-elected for Adelaide
in Nov., 1906. Recently he wrote a “limerick”
about Premier Peake, who, with the help of
Attorney-General Mitchell, retorted with this
effort of genius :
There was a young lawyer named Denny
Who once on a time tossed a penny
To find down which side
Of the rail he should slide
That was likely to best suit Bill Denny.
By the Oldest Inhabitant; —
Apropos. Chambers’ and Seymour’s Corner, Melbourne
(B. ]/7/’O9). On November 1, 1837, the half-acre on the
south-west corner of Swanston and Collins streets W'as
sold by auction, John Batman being the purchaser at
£6O. He also purchased the half-acre at the rear corner
of Flinders Lane for £s9—and invested £lOO in the half-
acre on the corner of Flinders-street and Swanston-street,
the latter at the punt, which was superseded by Princes’
Bridge. The old-time sign was “ Whitney, Chambersand
Co.” In 1888 the oppooite half-acre corner, known as
Nicholson’s, and for which Henry Howie gave £45 at
the first sale (June I,’ 18371, was valued at £493,500.
Henry Howie had a little history. He was a Goulburn
man. His brother, John Howie, had taken over one of the
first flocks of sheep from the Sydney side, and had
selected a fine run for himself south of the ranges, near
the present site of Gisborne. John then went to Sydney
to bring his wife and children to their new home; but
the schooner in which they sailed was never again heard
of. Henry Howey, as the name was originally spelled,
went from Sydney and took possession as heir-at-law. At
the first land sale he bought three half-acre lots—the
corner at £45, next at £29, and the next at £2O. The
three half-acres adjoined, and in 1888 they were valued
at £890,500.
Bananaland’s new Speaker, “ Jaw ” or
“Joey’’Bell, has twice been Chairman of Com-
mittees in the Assembly, and has for many
years had his eye (the one he wears the
monocle on—George Reid wears his Dry
Dog in the other eye) directed towards the
more lucrative job. His drawback as a
Minister in the Morgan and Kidston
Governments was his hatred of office work.
While he ran the Lands Office he dis-
played unprecedented energy outside the
building in hunting up settlers and inspect-
ing desirable country; but the officers of
the Department were always at their wits’
end to know how to get; him to reduce the
pile of papers which for weeks on end rested
on his table with all the solemnity of a tomb-
stone. A peculiarity of Bell’s is his Oxford
bleat, which he imbibed while at the Cold
Country’s great seat of learning, though
he is a Queenslander by birth. He has
been in Parliament for 16 years, repre-
senting Dalby, a place which his father
represented before him.
At last one British aristocrat is going
to do something for his cheese and beer)
The new governor of Worcester Gaol is
Harold Edward Fitzclarence, great-great-
grandson of William IV., and brother and
heir presumptive of the Earl of Munster.
The N.S.W. Premier struck bedrock last
Saturday, when he “ dedicated a horse-trough ” at
Lindfield and made a speech.
Laurence J. Harnett, who has just retired
from the job of Sergeant-at-Arms to the N.S.W.
Assembly, helped to “remove the honorable
member” at the bidding of six Speakers and ten
different chairmen of committees; and he removed
the honorable but disorderly member out of 18
different Parliaments.
The Australian Steamship Owners’ Federa-
tion lost its president last week, when “Dave”
Prosser died in Sydney, of septic pneumonia—a
trouble which began with a small carbuncle.
Prosser, who was still under 45, had battled his
way up from a junior clerk’s place in the Orient
office until he was chairman of G. S. Yuill
and Co., the people who, among many other
things, manage affairs for the Adelaide Steamship
Co. in N. S. Wales.
THE EARTHS BURDEN.—TOO MUCH
ROTHSCHILD.
Mr. Lloyd George, Chancellor of the Exchequer, said
he considered that the speeches made on the previous
day were only “the same drivel about Socialism.” Pro-
ceeding, he remarked, “ We are having too much Roths-
child."—Recent Cable.
This was the poet Samuel Rogers’ epigram
on Mr. Ward, afterwards Lord Dudley, the Aus-
tralian Governor-General’s ancestor :
They say that Ward’s no heart, but I deny it,
He has a heart and gets his speeches by it.
It was provoked by a way-Ward remark at the
table. Rogers explained that his carriage had
broken down and that he had been obliged to
come in a hackney coach. Ward whispered loudly,
“In a hearse, I should think”—in allusion to the
poet s corpse-like countenance. Rogers never for-
gave the remark, and spent a week coining the
retaliatory epigram. Sydney Smith said : “When
Rogers produces a couplet he goes to bed, and the
knocker is tied up, and straw is laid down, and
the caudle is made, and the answer to inquiries is
that Mr. Rogers is as well as can be expected.”
The father of the Tasmanian Bar, Charles
Butler, died at Hobart op Thursday, aged 89
years. He was a native of Tasmania, and his
father was the first solicitor who: pursued the six-
and-eightpenee in the Apple Isle. It is now 69
years since Charles Butler climbed the upper
branch of the profession after the same profitable
animal; but, after all, there isn’t much 6s. Bd.
in Tasmania, and what there is roosts pretty high,'
and even after 69 years a lawyer may not be
wildly affluent. C. Butler was the second father
that the Tasmanian Bar has lost in two years,
Adye Douglass being the other one.
A connecting link with the long past
snapped at The Cedars, Epping (N.S.W.), on
July 6, when Euphemia Catherine Hume died.
She had been the wife of Andrew Hamilton
Hume, and daughter of Captain Richard Tasker
Furlong, of the 80th Regiment. The regiment
arrived at Sydney in 1838, and Captain Furlong
was appointed police magistrate at Mudgee in
1840. He died and was buried there. Rightly or
wrongly, he was accused of being a hard master
and an enthusiastic flogger. So there was trouble
to get a grave dug for him, and even then friends
had to guard the place for a week, beause threats
were made*that the corpse would be dug up and
thrown away.
Horace Bastings, who passed over at
Auckland (M.L.j a few days ago, at the age of
78 years, started pis Australian life at Brunswick
(Melbourne), where his father ran a shoe business.
The father, who was almost a dwarf, came from
Islington (Londdh) in the, early ’fifties with the
family; and the son, recently dead, engaged for
a while in mining. He attained in the ’sixties to
the giddy eminence Of being Mayor of Brunswick.
Then the wanderlust came on him. He moved to
New South Wales-, returned to Victoria,, and then
went to Maoriland, where he bought an interest
in Cobb and Co. In 1876 he settled in Dunedin
as an auctioneer, land-dealer, agent, odd-jobber
in finance, and generally useful mercantile man.
In the very small days, when each province of
Maoriland ran its own government, Bastings held
many portfolios amid the little backyard politics
of Otago. The wanderlust took him again, and he
came back to Australia and built the North Sydney
cable tram. Finally—still wandering—Horace got
to Auckland, and died.
Garvin, N.S.W. Inspector-General of
Police, who has received thel.S.O. leather medal,
was a mounted trooper in ’62, nearly 50 years ago’
and has been in the force ever since, rising through
all the ranks until now he stands at the top. His
declining years are being filled with worry on
account of a persistent agitation to get the local
bobby an occasional Sunday off. Such liberality
was unknown when Garvin was earning his
stripes, and he can’t be got to see the sense of it
now. But in his young days the police didn’t
have votes, which should explain things to
Garvin, 1.5.0.
Maoriland begins to suspect that Joe
Ward has a “yellow streak” somewhere. When
a howling mob of Wellingtonians turned up to
protest against Joe’s intention to close down
Parliament while he strutted in the Imperial
slushlight, Joseph pleaded pressure of business
and shut himself up. In similar circumstances
old man Seddon would have stood on a gate post,
and ranted and roared until the multitude was
moved to cheers—even Loud Cheers. Also,
when Joey embarked he was surrounded by
cohorts of constables, lest some barbarian should
push him over the end of the wharf; and now it
“leaks out” that Detective Cassells went to the
Cold Country in his shadow, presumably to
protect him from assassination by some infuriated
tourist who had been enticed to Maoriland by the
seductive official pictures of Ward’s innocent
Maori maiden, and on arrival couldn’t find her.
A heroic English suffragette, who had been
gaoled for public disturbance, has cut her sentence
short. She refused all food for three days and 19
hours. It was ob\ious that she meant to perish as
a haggard skeleton in her cell, and no Government
could hope to last after a woman had starved her-
self to death on its hands. So the prison doors
were opened, and the captive tottered forth to the
nearest food emporium.
It was the first Earl of Dudley who wound
up an eloquent tribute to the vir-
tues of a deceased Baron of the
Exchequer with this pithy perora-
tion : “He had the best melted
butter I ever tasted in my life.”
In the cemetery attached to
St. Stephen’s Anglican tabernacle
at Penrith (N.S. W.) is a weather-
worn tombstone, on which may be
read :
John Jamison, M.D., R.N.,
H.M.S. Gordon,
- Physician to the Baltic Fleet,
Died at Regentville, 29th June, 1844.
The knight was a distinguished
colonist, who spent money right
royally. At Regentville Henry
Parkes had his first Australian
job as a laborer, with small wages,
short rations, and a leaky hut to
Jive m. Jamison also owned con-
siderable town property, which he
cut up and sold in 1831, Jamison-
street being opened up through
it. Jamison left many sons and
daughters, one of whom, R. T.
Jamison, sat in one of the very
early Parliaments as member for
the Nepean.
“Pindar” gives an idea of one
of the jobs that the Commonwealth
Government has to undertake
Over here in Westralia, Deputy-Commissioner Green
has to see to it that the public service adds up two and
two, and makes the result four, and that means a lar°e
contract in the way of travelling. Met Green recently in
Broome when he was completing a 3000-mile trip extend-
ing from Shark Bay to Hall’s Creek, and down to Derby.
Dressed in khaki, he looked like a bullocky down for
the races. He had made the long- journey per buckboard,
with only a native to look after the houses. Every post
office and custom house had been inspected, and the most
distant outposts of civilisation in the Far Nor’-West had
been visited. He gathered in some interesting- specimens
from natives on his tour, and seemed to g-et on well
with the nomadic but infrequent Black Brother in that
part. Hall’s Creek, which was the end of his journey
was, in the days of the Kimberley rush, a busy mining
camp with ten or twelve pubs, pianos, barmaids, billiard
tables, and other allurements ; now it has a solitary
drinking- shop. Long'-bearded goats, empty jam tins,
dead marines, and buck spinifex are its chief character-
istics ; and but for a few drovers, who make it a camping
place, Hall’s Creek would go light back into the desert
again.
Thus The Bulletin’s Oldest Inhabitant:—
Me Mossman’s purchase (B. l/7/’O9). By a slip of the
pen br a temporary lapse of memory, I said that Mossman
Duut The Rangers.” The house was erected about the
year 1840 by Oswald Bloxsonte. A few years later Mr.
Oswald Brierley, the artist, was a guest there, and
painted “ The Rattlesnake in a Hurricane ” on the dining-
room wall. It is there still. Later on Mr. Henry Stuart
Russell, a Queensland pioneer, spent a small fortune on
the place. The grounds now attached to the old house
are limited in extent, the old “ broad acres” having been
cut up and built upon. There is a tower wherein did
hang a bell (I think it hangs there still) which called the
people to work and (possibly) to prayer. Later tenants
used to say that the house was haunted, as the bell tolled
sometimes at midnight. Tradition says that Mrs.
Bloxsome would not have any prisoner-servants about
her; she stood out for free labor.
THE DREAD-
NOUGHTER
AS PICTURED
BY JOSIAH
SYMON.
He read from his notes the following opinion upon the
offer of a Dreadnought to the British Government: “It
was the outcome of hysterical emotion, not the con.
sidered proposal of sane minds”—a statement which is
nothing short of an insult to the large majority of Aus-
tralians who enthusiastically supported the movement.—
Argos on Josh Symon.
THE LAY OF THE ANCIENT RESIDENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On Friday last Mr. D. M'Leod, M.L.A., presented to
the Daylesford State School a large framed record of
incidents connected with the loss in the bush near the
town, in June, 1867, of three little boys, and the recovery
of their remains in September of that year. They had
taken shelter in a hollow tree, where they perished from
cold and exhaustion. The record is illustrated by photo-
graphic views and portraits. At last Friday’s gathering
at the school, several ladies and gentlemen were
present who had been among those who took part in the
search 42 years ago. Interesting speeches were made,
and the school children sang. “Dear Are the Memories
of Childhood.” — Vic. news item.
The tragedies of olden days
To me are ever dear,
Upon the treasured past I gaze
And bless each bygone year.
With trembling hand my harp I touch.
As back to mind I bring
The sad events which pleased me much.
And feelingly I sing—
All the things that happened in the long, long ago
Seem fresh to an old, old man,
Whose feiv remaining hairs are as white, white as snow;
Die cakes which to-day are invariably dough
Were sweet when my life began,
To whatsoever happened in the wayback time
My memory delights to cling ;
So I put my recollections in a rambling rhyme,
And give ’em to the kids to sing—
Ting, ting —
The chubby little kids to sing.
In ’46, the month of May,
A flood came swirling round ;
It swept my uncle’s hut away,
And Auntie Jane was drowned.
The blacks, who found her body, stole
Her clothes and wedding ring ;
So with the fervor of a ghoul
I now proceed to sing.
A bush-fire raged in ’4B
And burnt my father out;
The awful fate of sister Kate
Is shrouded still in doubt.
The girl went mad and fled from sight,
But didn’t long survive—
She either died of mortal fright,
Or else was baked alive.
My Bertha was a bonnie lass,
A prize and no mistake 1
In ’59 it came to pass
She trod upon a snake.
The Varmint turned and bit her leg,
Her bonnie leg—poor wife !
I had some whisky in a keg—
It didn’t save her life.
Our boy grew up and went to sea—
He shipped in ’74 :
He never said good-bye to me,
I never saw him more.
The gallant vessel struck a gale,
And of its human freight
Not one came back to tell the tale,
Except the second mate.
I’m thinking whilst my pipe I smoke
Of friends among the dead,
Who made a pile and then went broke
And had to beg their bread.
The past is bright, the future blue,
And Time is on the wing ;
The man who would his youth renew
Must look behind, and sing :
AU the things that happened in the long, long ago
Seem fresh to an old, old man,
Whose few remaining hairs are as white, white as snow ;
The cakes which to-day are invariably dough
Were sweet when my life began.
To whatsoever happened in the wayback time
My memory delights to cling.
So I put my recollections in a rambling rhyme
And give ’em to the kids to sing—
Ting, ting —
The chubby little kids to sing.
VICTORIA.
O. K.

All who are interested in Australian
national politics should read “ The Commonwealth
Crisis,” bv C. H. Kirmess, the great serial now
running through The Lone Hand. Forecasting
a “peaceful” Japanese colonisation of the
Northern Territory. Kirmess traces the political
and war consequences with a masterly hand.
See that you do not miss “The Commonwealth
Crisis.”*

CIRCULATION OF THE BLOOD.
Health, Hope and Happiness are three insepar-
able blessings—and yet few possess them. Excess
is the tyrant that destroys them all with one
stroke. By impairing the digestive organs, excess
destroys the perfect circulation of the blood. The
blood is the carrier that conveys nutriment to
every cell in the body. Let it be disturbed
by ever so slight a cause and the entire organ-
ism suffers. Have you a healthy circulation ?
FRANZ JOSEF Natural Aperient Water will
assist you to get it. A dose taken before break-
fast will tone the stomach, awaken the liver,
clear the intestines, and send the rich, new blood
bounding on its travels, carrying with it Health,
Hope and Happiness.
A reliable medicine for croup, and one that
should always be kept on hand for immediate use,
is Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy.*
Sciatica’s Siarp
Shooting Pains.
*
The Trouble is in the Sciatic
Nerve.
To Cure, Restore the Run Down
Nerves—This Man Cured
in this Way.
Sciatica, with it's dreaded, sharp, shooting pains
in the thigh and legs—pains like red-hot needles
in tender flesh—is a nervous disorder. The
trouble is in the sciatic nerve. It begins when
the nerves are run down and out of tone. Nerves
become run down when the blood becomes too
thin ; they do not get a proper amount of nourish-
ment. Therefore, to cure sciatica, build up the
blood; the impoverished nerves are fed and the
sciatic pains disappear. Absolute rest is the best
aid to proper medical treatment.
So many severe cases of sciatica have been cured
by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, that every sufferer is
justified in giving the treatment a thorough trial.
A recent cure is that of Mri Arthur Ernest White,
64 Rosehill Street, Redfern, Sydney.
“ I was ailing for about four months off and on
with sciatica,” said Mr. White. “I got a chill at
my work, and it brought on a severe pain in my
back, just across the loins. It would ease off
during the day, after I had a little exercise ; but
it was bad at night time. I could hardly close
my eyes. I’d be twisting and turning all ways to
try and get some relief. It was like a toothache
with the constant nagging, gnawing pain. The
skin would be very red and tender. I rubbed
myself with liniments, but found them no good.
The pain extended down the side of my left leg
and the back of my thigh. For about an hour
after I’d get out of bed I could only limp about.
It would start about five each night and last for
hours. I’d always be worse when warm in bed.
I couldn’t bend my back without the pain catching
me like a knife. The pain only seemed to settle
deeper as time went on. At last, on a friend’s
advice, I tried Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. I felt a
shade of ease with the first box, and so I got
another, and then I really began to feel distinct
benefit. My back began to get supple again. I
could bend down with less difficulty. I was
working on the railway line at Belmore then. By
the time I had finished the third box I was better
still. The pains had lessened so much that I
could sleep quite easily. The darting spasms of
pain down my leg went away, as did the stiffness
on rising. I took two more boxes, and they
finally drove away every sign of the sciatica, and
now I’m as supple as ever.”
Insist on getting Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
They are sold by most chemists and storekeepers,
or, if not locally obtainable, will be sent direct by
The Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co. of Australasia
Ltd., Sydney, for 2s. 9d. per box, six boxes 15s. 3d.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Meagher’s reception by the N.S.W. Labor
party has had its double in Westralia, where
J. W. Clydesdale, a barrister, who ran a few
times as a Radical Independent, has signed the
pledge, and is making a bid for the Perth seat in
the House of Reps.
LIKE THE SITE ITSELF.
The Advisory Board views the prospects of a satisfactory
water supply at the Canberra site with serious alarm.
No. 1: By the way. old chappie, what do you
think of the — aw — aw—arguments in favor of
Canbewwa as a Fedewal capital site?"
No. 2: “ Why, simply this: They won't hold
water! ”
Irrigation Mead, the Victorian cold water
expert, went to Strathmerton to lecture on irriga-
tion, the other day, and found Strathmerton eight
or ten feet under water. The lecture on irrigation
had to be postponed till Strathmerton was some-
what less excessively irrigated. Graham, Minister
of Water Supply, visited Strathmerton at about
the same time, and discovered himself to be the '
object of a bitter prejudice. The inhabitants, who
were living in the open air mainly—on the roof—
seemed to think the Minister of Water Supply
had overdone it. Strathmerton is a settlement
four years of age, and has now suffered three floods
and two droughts.
Poor old Dreadnought was again heard of
last Saturday. The unfortunate person was then
up to £81,143 17s. 6d.
There are in Victoria, according to
Premier Murray, 45 persons who own an average
of £158,000 worth of land apiece, and all the violent
effort which Murray is making to dislodge them
would hardly suffice to shift a cat off as much soil
as goes into a flower-pot.
“Henry Phelthatt” : Snakes aren’t dying
off, as “XXX” imagines. They are merely
moving out of the bush and into the boots of loan-
drunk Treasurers. The extra scarcity just now is
owing to the fact that the boots of the Fused
Government seem as if they would soon be open
to receive snakes.
The annual conference of Chambers of
Agriculture assembled at Bendigo demand that
the Federal Government should tackle the sparrow
pest, the State Governments having proved their
inability to even hit the imported bird with a bit
of road metal. And the State Premiers, for once,
will not object. They will argue that the sparrow,
as a dispute extending over more than one State,
is one of the few things that come within Federal
jurisdiction.
The Westralian Labor party counts on
winning all three seats at the next Senate elec-
tion, and is just now picking its bunch—a ballot
is being taken this month all over the State.
Sitting Senators De Largie and Henderson and
eight others are running, including State Labor
members J. B. Holman and “Mulga” Taylor.
The most ancient battler of the crowd is Joseph
Fabre, who did a lot of bullocking for the N.S. W.
Labor party just before it arrived in ’9l.
Thus “Vado” in the Fortnightly Re-
view, advocating an Australian fleet: —
If unhappily we ever become involved in a great war,
our brethren in those British Dominions Beyond the
Seas will cnafe and fume to be up and doing something
towards helping us in our hour of peril. But without the
instruments of war, without even the elementary know-
ledge of the art of those that go down to the sea in ships,
of what avail will be the brain and sinew of our kindred
fighting men to the Mother Country ? The sea-warrior’s
duties cannot be learned in a day.
“ Harrison O.” : The Shanghai Saturday
Review, in a recent issue, had some remarks to
make about the state of affairs in Manchuria.
The writer states :
So far as my experience goes, the Japanese are the most
cruel and the most brutal people on the whole earth. . . .
By far the biggest legacy of evil that came to Man-
churia was the crowd of Japanese women who found
their way to all parts of the province during the war, and
that legacy abides with us still. Japanese immorality, so
übiquitous, so bold, so brazen-faced, obtruding itself
everywhere, as if it were a specially-privileged member of
society, has lowered Chinese morality very seriously. In
fact, the Chinese, who are by no means sensitive upon a
subject of this kind, are disgusted with Japanese prosti-
tution, and they express their disgust openly and
strongly. And the strangest part of the whole vile busi-
ness is the utter absence of shame on the part of the
respectable portion of the Japanese community. To them
it seems a perfectly natural element of Japanese social
life.
From the fertile Western wastes of Vic-
toria :—•
While the Age, newly converted to the land-tax, is
prodding John Murray forward, the Argits keeps holding
him back with the yarn that the big estates are being cut
up “in a natural way”—whatever that is. Every now
and then the Tory paper prints a list of the big estates
that have been subdivided, and triumphantly shouts that
there is no need for “a confiscatory land-tax.” Butthese
voluntarily subdivided est ites are only a drop in the
bucket, and the subdivisions often simply mean that a
slice is cut off one big estate and added to another. For
example, the Argus made a lot of the subdivision of some
of the Currie estates, on the Western plains. Only small
slices from two out of the five Currie estates were, how-
ever, sold, and the buyers included only half a dozen real
farmers. One huge block was actually bought by an
adjoining brother Currie. Another was taken by a neigh-
boring squatter, and others were grabbed by people who
alreatty owned more land than they could use to advan-
tage. It is all very well having legislation to compel
land-owners to cut up their big- estates ; but what is just,
as badly wanted is legislation to keep them cut up, and
the best legislation for that purpose is a graduated land-
tax.
From “ Invasion and Conscription,” a
recent British publication, by James Anson
Farran :—■
I would entrust the defence of India mainly to Chinese
and Japanese mercenaries under English generals, and
if either country prohibited such service to its subjects,
I would obtain the same by payment of a direct sub-
sidy to their Governments. 1 ,.
Which is suggestive. India is “ the most precious
jewel in the Crown of Empire,” and so forth.
When the motherland’s war experts are impelled
to start talking of defending their country’s best-
loved asset by means of hired Asiatics, it is surely
time Australia fell to, jettisoned the gang of
reliers-on-England that disfigures its politics, and
waded in to defend itself.
Some of the richest land in Victoria is to
be found at Kooweerup Swamp, but it has its
drawbacks. When eager land-seekers first cast
hungry eyes on it it was subject to heavy floods
every winter. The State drained it, and the
settlers got to work, only to find their blocks
steadily sinking. Delving into the earth’s innards,
they found and rooted out a heavy crop of ti-tree
stumps. -But the land continued to sink, so
further explorations were made, and another
stratum of ti-tree stumps came up. The soil re-
mained stationary for a while ; but latterly it has
been falling away again, and a third crop of
stumps has now come to view—red gums this
time. Of course, with all this sinking, the drains
have had to be deepened, and still some of the
country is under water ; but if the crust will but
stay where it ought to be for a few years, the
plucky settlers still hope to “make a do of things.”
Meanwhile, the equally rich lands west of Mel-
bourne, where no floods come nor any sinking
happens, are locked against the men who spend
their lives and break their hearts in trying to
“ make a do of things.”
THE FRA N KEN STEIN DEAKIN HORROR.
Mr. Wilks said the Fusion wasan experiment.—Parlia-
mentary Report.
AUSTRALIA (catching sight of experiment): “Oo!
No!! Don't!!!"
Though the Butler party has decided to
support the Peake Government in S. Aus., the
Fusion will have a majority of only one vote in a
House of 42 members, That one vote is Bur-
goyne’s. He is 83 years old, and his son is editor
of the Adelaide Labor paper. So, one way and
another, the Peake party is still afraid to draw a
very long breath for fear of upsetting the balance.
From N.S.W. Pastoral Union secretary’s
annual report:—
As a result of the rates for shearing and shed work
fixed by the Federal Arbitration Court, yielding higher
wages than could be earned in any other pursuit, a large
amount of labor had been attracted to the industry, and
the season’s shearing operations had been carried through
expeditiously and satisfactorily.
In short, the pastoralists have made the amazing
discovery that it pays to pay good wages.
Good old Unearned Increment was in great
form when the Cleveland-street (Sydney) tram way
was opened the other day. For years past the
residents had been agitating for a tramway ex-
tension, and at last they got it. There was a
great shivoo at the opening ceremony, when all
the people who had done the agitating got up and
made speeches and subscribed for champagne out
of pure joy. Having cheerfully paid the bills for
the celebration, they went borne feeling like giants
refreshed—to find on their mantelpieces notes
from their landlords, informing them that, owing
to the additional facilities provided by the new
line, their rents were to be raised. It was not
only the big houses that were blackmailed in this
way. Nearly every residence within walking dis-
tance was assessed by the owner at something
extra. Even the occupiers of little three-roomed
terrace blocks were called upon to weigh out six-
pence a week more than formerly, as a result of
having stirred Parliament to action.
The Melbourne City Council wants a
tar-spraying machine, and has decided to
import one. To the few inquiring council-
lors who wanted to know why the foreign
workman should have the job of making it
in a foreign factory, and -thus help to pay
rates to a foreign municipality, the usual
vapid yarn was told—the £2OO machine to
spray tar was “too complicated” for
manufacture in Victoria, Victoria being
evidently an aboriginal country, which about
reaches its limit when it makes a wooden
skewer. And, of course, the committee that
worked the job was so sure the machine
couldn’t be made here that it didn’t trouble
to call for tenders, so as to be certain
about it. Or else it was so sure that it was a
poor, muddy, disreputable liar when it said
the machine couldn’t be made locally, that it
daren’t run the risk of being exposed. Is
it unpatriotism that makes men do things
like this? Or is it something else? Which-
ever it is, every voter ought to mark those
men. They are dangerous, anyhow.
While Wade, and Wade’s Minister for
Lands have been flattening out the N.S.W.
Labor Opposition with tales about the
thousands and thousands of settlers the
Government has put on the soil, out came
the Government Statistician’s agricultural
statistics. These show that in 1906 the area
under crop in N.S.W. was 2,840,235 acres,
and in 1907 it was 2,826,617 acres, whereas
for the past season it was only 2,716,871
acres. That sort of thing may mean settle-
ment, but it doesn’t exactly look like it.
N.S.W. has lately been reorganising its
prison system, with the result that the following
“permanent and reproductive works” (that is,
gaols) have been pitched on the waste dump:
Berrima, Wollongong, Deniliquin, Young, Trial
Bay, Mudgee, Forbes, Tarn worth,. Maitland,
Hay, Wagga, Yass, and Wilcannia. The special
retreat for shocking females at Biloela will also be
added to the list within the next few months, and
now the removal of the huge Darlinghurst pile is
mooted. The building of these now-useless
“jugs” ran away with very much borrowed cash,
and, while they themselves become little more
than glorified rat-holes, the interest on the loan
drunks that begot them, like the brook, runs on
for ever. In fact, it beats the brook hollow, for
even the driest weather doesn’t dry up the loan.

Last tear it seemed impossible that ever it should be
This soldier talk of bearing arms to hold our country free
No cash was in the project, nor stray Iv.C.M.G.
Now
Ward is off to London
A tinsel crown to bring,
McNab is teaching Maoriland
The song- that she should sing.
Up and down the windy coast
Hear the chorus ring,
We’ll all go McNabbing in the spring.
We’ll all go McNabbing,
So we’re all right,
Nab-nab-nabbing,
Till we learn to fight.
We’ll all go McNabbing, hear the chorus ring,
We’ll all go McNabbing in the spring !
Last year we felt too tired, too worn and weak with toil,
To learn to use a rifle to guard our patch of soil,
But, Lord ! the gleam of tinsel has taught our blood to
boil.
For soon
Ward comes back from London
Garlanded with tin,
The soldier tramp of Maoriland
Is ready to begin,
Round and round the crooked coast
Hear the chorus ring,
We’ll all go McNabbing in the spring.
We’ll all go McNabbing,
So bang the big drum,
Nab-nab-nabbing
From now till Kingdom Come,
For glory, Ward, and Cohen, hear the chorus ring—
We’ll all go McNabbing in the spring.

MUGGA MUGGA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The spot chosen by the “Advisory Board” for Aus.,
tralia’s Federal City is described as beimj somewhere near
the Mugga Mugg-a Mountain. The watersupply is doubt-
ful.
Candidly, I do not hug a
Wish to go to Mugga Mugga ;
To the Mugga Mugga Mountain by Yassberra’s desert
place,
Where they’re planning—more’s the pity—
To erect Australia’s city,
To upraise a drouthy city—monument to our disgrace.
’Tis proposed that we shall lug a
Myriad pipes to Mugga Mugga—
Water-pipes to get the wetness to the city’s thirsty crowd
Water to ablute and bathe in ?
Nay ! The language will be scathin’
When the Mugga mugs discover : “ Notice—Bathing Not
Allowed.”
Wearily, with jar or jug, a
Citizen at Mugga Mugga
Will await his turn for water—wait with bucket, billy-
can,
Kerosene-tin —any vessel
That the Cotter’s muddy mess ’ll
Safely keep in—O, the weepin’ of the Mugga Mugga man !
I can see a future Mugga
Resident arise and tug a
Show’r-bath chain without result, then curse aloud and
thirst for blood—-
Curse the crawling Cotter trickle.
For he will be in a pickle
When the Cotter isn’t cotting and Molonglo’s mostly
mud.
I’ve a yearn, within, to plug a
Jaw whenever Mugga Mugga
Mountain’s mentioned in my hearing, for it makes me
very sore.
When I realise Dalgety
Was thrown over for the petty
Claims of parish politicians I’m inclined to rage for gore.
I’d rejoice if someone dug a
Deep, wide grave at Mugga Mugga,
And interred all Canberranters, minus service, sob or
stone—
All nefarious State-Frighters !
Yassinine old nation-blighters !
Nay ; I’d lug a Mugga-fighter there and plant him on my
own !

I’ve been handling tea since H
was a boy—of course 1 always
say my own’s the best —perhaps
it is and perhaps it isn’t —anyhow
it’s the best for me to sell.
Putting aside personal interests,
1 find a lot of pleasure in selling
tea, and when l can’t sell my
own, 1 don’t mind selling any
of the packed teas —the thing
that annoys me most in business
is for a customer to buy
groceries from me and tea from
somebody else
I’m not going to blow about
the quality of my own tea, but
I’ll swear it isn’t possible for
any house-to-house canvasser to
sell tea equal to Rob u r, for
the same money—he couldn’t do
it, and make enough to keep
himself in shoe leather, let alone
tucker—l suppose you wonder
when there’s not much profit
on “Robur” why 1 sell it—
well, you see it’s like this,
if l didn’t sell it my customers
might go to another shop and
the chances are they’d buy their
other groceries there too, and
I’d rather sell an article for no
profit than lose a customer.
Talking about Robur, there’s
never been a tea that has given
such satisfaction —my people
like it —it is nicely packed and
the quality is always the same,
and once customers get used
to it, it’s the devil’s own job
to get them to take any other
tea if you happen to run out.
Some people like the No. 1
grade, and others the No. 2.
I’d say give an extra 3d. and
get the best.
The Grocer.

BUCHANAN WHISKY—
“BLACK & WHITE
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sidney, July 13, 1909.
My Dear Arini,—
Last week my morning daily tbld me that
“ several schools of whales have been seen from
the South Coast, passing north.” The news gave
me a rush of poesy to the head :
“ What are the wild whales learning?
And have they a rule of three ? ”
I asked with tender yearning
An old, old man of the sea.
“ What are the young whales learning
Par out in the howling gale,
As all the school goes churning
In line with the teacher’s tail?”
“ Learnin’! ” the old man mumbled,
And his voice was deep and hoarse.
“ Learnin’! Lord ! ain’t you tumbled ?
Learnin’ to blubber, o’ course ! ”
The Dudley Countess has gone on the ice of
the Glaeiarium, and is learning to cut the frozen
substance leaning on the wing—both wings—of
Mr. Jimmy Osborne. He would seem to be not too
expert an ice-pilot, but the Lady in White is an apt
pupil. She has the right type of figure to look
well at the Polar-bear exercise. The surprise of
certain of our native-born at seeing her Ex. learn-
ing to skate was comical. A few of the native-
born have an idea that Englanders have to skate
to and from their homes during their winter ; but
that’s nothing to the mistakes the Englander’s
make about people on this patch of the planet.
Possibly some of the Countess’s own friends in
her own country will be amazed at hearing that
“dear Rachel has found some ice in that torrid
country ! Hand-made ice, of course ; but things
down there can’t be so sunstruck after all.” Yet
if you go up to Gippsland’s Buffalo Lake in winter
you can get real ice—more of it than any one
human can ask for.
Mrs. Hore-Ruthven and Captain Gilliat are
part of the vice-regal atmosphere at the glacial
place; also a Madden girl from Melbourne. The
grey front lock in her dark hair signs her. She is
a plump and pleasing little party, and skates well.
Her sister, Miss Ruby Madden, is, or was, at Kosci-
usko. Some of the skaters, whose practices date
from the inauguration of the rink, are nearly as
awkward to-day as they were in their first week,
whilst others have taken to it as the duck’s eldest
egg-daughter takes to swimming. One damsel,
Miss Lyn Maclurcan, whirls with the waltzers,
and only a few have sufficient courage to waltz.
She was a beginner on this winter’s ice.
By the time these lines meet your eye the
Governor-General’s establishment will have trek-
ked Brisbane-wards, there to abide for a month
at least. It returns to this city in time for the
September revels, and stays for a couple of months.
It is remarked in the halls (likewise in the parlors
and two-pair backs) of our mightiest that, to date,
the Dudley’s have “made no mistakes.” Previous
Governors and their dames were constantly for-
getting the faces of awful-looking but socially
important people; also they were liable to start
friendly chats in re gyves or dungeons or gibbets
with the wrong aristocrats. And then those con-
cerned started as though they had been stung,
and wandered round in a distraught fashion for
weeks afterwards, and said harsh things about
the National Anthem, and even went so far as to
cast aspersions on the Flag. Some asset —whether
natural genius, or the amazingly adroit, know-
ledgable, and experienced Lord Dick Nevill, it is
hard to say—has shielded the latest gubernatorial
pair from any lapse of this sort. And the achiev-
ment is a notable one. Take him (or her) for all
in all, your average member of the indigenous
noblesse is about the most easily slighted, and
when so treated the most infuriated circumstance
to be found outside the London suburb of Balham,
not to mention Peckham Rye.
Artist Arthur Streeton may now hang a third
Paris Salon medal upon his chest. Award is for
his “Australia Eelix,” painted at Mount Macedon,
and shown in Sydney two years ago.
Judge A’Beckett and his consort, of Melbourne,
and the Erank Gavan Duffys, are in Sydney, at the
Wentworth Hotel; and the heavily-gilded Senior
Horsfall, of Widgiewa, is here for the sheep time.
Speaker McCourt has let his pastoral home at
Berrima, and has taken root with his wife and
family at Vaucluse, that historic water-frontaged
estate out Rose Bay wards.
Mr. Frederic Manning, youngest son of our
W. P. Manning, would seem to have achieved con-
siderable fame with his book, “Scenes and Por-
traits.” “Six episodes,” says Paris Daily Mail.
“ taken from the dust of the centuries.” The first
occurs in the garden where Adam lived ; the last
is an interview between Renan and the late Pope
Leo. The book is said to reveal scholarship, and
force and originality of intellect. The writer
abides in the older, colder land.
The Misses Ingiis Clark, two daughters of the
late Judge and eminent constitutional law
authority of the Supreme Court of Tasmania, are
at Petty’s Hotel for a short stay.
The committee people of the Hospice ball have
most likely collapsed, exhausted but triumphant,
into a rest-cure place. None of them has been
seen about Sydney since Wednesday night, when
the ball rolled merrily and successfully into the
morning of another day. The Chelmsford Excel-
lencies arrived early, to see the tableaux and hear
the singing that preceded the dancing. When
they had seen and heard, a beautiful aide was sent
forth into the densely-inhabited hall to track Mrs.
J. C. Williamson, who was vice-president of the
committee and responsible for the gallery attrac-
tions, and bring her to the dais for vice-regal
congratulations. On the dais Mrs. Allen Taylor
and committee members Mesdames Deery, John
Barlow, Macneil Simpson, J. Kearney, Boesen,
J. J. Smith, Muller. Hayes, Snell and Mac-
Elhone, saved their Excellencies from splendid
isolation. The galleries were packed and the hall
so crowded as to make any committee feel vain-
glorious.
The committee of the aforesaid ball is getting
one grey hair apieceas a result of the very success of
the function. Not dreaming that all the gallery
tickets sent broadcast would be sold, numbers
of persons were admitted by paying at the
doors. When late ticket-holders arrived they were
denied admittance, as the galleries were full.
Hence complaints from the turned-down and the
grey hairs for the committee. An Act which came
into force just seven days earlier forbidding stand-
ing exercise (or overcrowding) was enforced on the
ball night by the Town Hall officials. A general
refund of moneys to the disappointed is being
arranged.
Mayoress of Waverlev, Mrs.R. G. Watkins, was
“at home,” away from her home, on a day last
week in the -new pavilion at Waverley Park.
Guests attended in such numbers that the popular
Mayoress’s right glove wasn’t of much account at
the end of the festival, and the hand that it had
encased badly wanted the rest cure.
A hat authoress showed me some of her latest
originations and importations last week. The
models will make her spring show, but as it’s
months too early for that epic time the exhibition
is to remain a dead secret. That’s why I’m tell-
ing about it now. “The big hats are smaller,”
asserted the authority, “and the little hats are
bigger than last season.” She upreared a creation
closely related to the Gainsborough in shape, but
the gradient of its acclivity is much steeper, not
to say more precipitous, than the turn-up of the
hat Gainsborough piled on the head of his Duchess
of Devonshire. The hat under mention was of
white Leghorn straw on its upper surfacb and
black underneath, and it carried a wreath of
nearly full-blown pink roses with all their foliage
separate and standing up above the flowers like a
green hedge. Some sort of an un-precious stone
finished the up-turn of the brim.
Hat 2 : “This,” explained the lady, “is the
‘ cabriolet ’ shape. It really reminds of the shape
of a one-horse Chaise. Notice the huge crowns on
most of the newest hats. And the coarse Straws
—not coarse in texture, but in size. These straws
are quite ornamental in themselves, often shading
opalescently, the strands nearly an inch wide.”
This “cabriolet” was decorated with wide, pale-
green velvet ribbon and daisies —big marguerites,
and the ordinary garden-border daisies, down at
last to tiny, gone-wild daisies. “Ribbon
velvet,” remarked the priestess of the hattery
(just now she was an authoress, but let it pass), as
she directed an inspired gaze at a certain card-
board box, “ ribbon-velvet will be a feature of the
new season’s hats. You see, shades that would
give one neuralgia of the eyes if worn in any other
material, look exquisitely soft and becoming in
velvet. Shades of cherry pink, for instance—
almost anyone may wear cherry pink in soft,
miror velvet ribbon. And wide strings—not
to tie under the chin—but to loop across in any
old becoming way.”
Her eyes grew misty and she seemed to sleep-
walk towards the cardboard box. As in a dream
she opened it, and from under layer upon layer of
tissue paper she withdrew a Something that
might have been named Trophy de Paris. “ This ”
murmured the hatterina, “is a cavalier shape
with tapestry roses. These tapestry flowers will
be largely worn, and the roses in this shade of
mauve pink are beautiful. This is oazrtle straw —
a warm shade of reddish brown. Yes, of course,
I suppose it is named after the deer thing.
Gazelle straw is in, anyhow.” But the next
creation the lady raised aloft on one hand was
the startler—the hat of my troubled dreams.
It was a grey beehive. The greyness was
due to grey silk cut into millions of
petals, and into this rustling mass—this
thicket of petals—were embedded sundry tight
little bunches of pink moss-roses. It was the
cutest thing in head lids, a joke in millinery,
and I hope I’ll see it again—at the spring races.
She is a friend of mine, and her normal appear-
ance suggests that she is the sole daily care of a
hairdresser, a ladies’ tailor, a shoeing artist, and
other experts. She came to where I was seated
on the ferry steamer and asked if she might read
“ Daphne,” Mrs. Humphrey Ward’s latest. “I
am taking it back to the iibrary, ” I answered,
“ but you may come with me and capture it.”
She protested that she would read it in five
minutes. “In fact, I never waste more than 15
minutes over any book.” After studying the
picture of Daphne on the cover, she quickly
hit upon the page whereon the hero
is admiring from a distance the (costly)
simplicity of the heroine’s frocking. Then
as she restrapped the book mv friend remarked :
“You’ll see me turn out in a ‘Daphne’ get up
when summer comes. Mrs. Humphrey Ward
doesn’t go in much for gowning her characters, but
when she does mention the thing, she does it
prettj' well, but without details. You guess that
she’s seen some titled debutante at a garden party
or dance, and has noted her frocking for future
literary use.” The “ Daphne ” girl in the picture
was in white (as she had millions of dollars it must
have been expensive white), with a rose-colored
sash and a wide black hat with white flowers.
Having noted this in five minutes’ inspection
of the book, my friend of the impeccable
appearance was heard, at a tea-lap next dav,
criticising Mrs. Ward’s latest. Someone said,
“A ou do keep up to date. I didn’t even know
the book was out.” And the glossy one swallowed
the compliment as easily as she drank her tea.
In this book, which I wouldn’t be surprised to
hear the critics call “ pot-boiler,” the hair of Mrs.
Ward’s heroine “ tumbles ” but once. In “ Diana
Mallory ” its immediate predecessor, the principal
girl s black hair is “ tumbling ” in every chapter.
It is interesting, if hypercritical, to spot the way
various writers repeat themselves in unimportant
points. Miles Justus Forman, whose last book,
The Quest,’ is now here, has a great weakness
for making his engaged and married lovers walk
up and down in the moonlight in a peculiar
manner. The girl leans her head back on the
man’s shoulder or shirt front, and walks
at an angle in front of him. It sounds a tiring
attitude for both persons, and must suggest that
the man is shoving the lady ahead of him; but
probably Mr. Forman himself has tried, and liked
it, for he introduces it in several of his stories.
Whilst carping it may be mentioned that an
American reviewer has entreated writers to give
up the “twisted smile.” Numerous bookmakers
have been afflicting their men characters with the
twisted smile, and the reviewer simply commands
them to pleat the smile in future, or frill it, or
merely cut it plain smile—do anything with it but
twist it.
Mrs. Robert Brough’s marriage to Mr. Bell, an
electrician in the same company in which she was
recently playing, was a very quiet one. Besides the
two principals only the mother of the bridegroom
was present. National Park honeymoons the
happy pair.
Miss Nellie Stewart, wrapped in white furs and
with her golden hair encroaching on her face, took
the air (she didn’t take it all, of course) at Manly
on Sunday from the vantage of a brougham, and
all beholders vowed that she might be nineteen
next birthday.
Misses Ethel Warwick and Dorothy Grimston,
the two principal feminines in “ The Flag
Lieutenant,” alighted upon this city last Satur-
day. One promptly took her couch with an attack
of bronchitis, and the other spent the Sabbath
fishing for the wary and frequent-limbed octo-
pus. Strange how our distinguished visitors
run after strange saltwater monsters first
thing. The Countess of Dudley is supposed
to be unhappy till she hooks a shark. Any-
way, both imported charmers, Warwick and
Grimston, turned up dimpling to rehearsal
on Monday. Both are tall, very slight, and un-
commonly good-looking, and neither has monkeyed
with her hair; so none will be able to call them
Chemical Floras. Miss Warwick has been limned
by Whistler. Some Pointer —whether Potts or
Darling or Piper I now know not, but it wasn’t
Glebe—once showed me an etching of the famous
painter’s Green Girl, a mysterious-eyed damsel
who in the original picture is swathed, hair and
body, with green, wave-like drapery. The
girl inside the drapery is our visitor
with the early-English kingmaker’s name.
She is dark, with long, full-lidded hazel eyes
and an oval face. Her dramatic record is good,
and if she had not come hither she would have
taken the Nathalie rdle in “ The Merry Widow ”
from George Edwardes, which was hers for the
not asking. A voice is an extra.. Miss Warwick
is a Mrs. ; the wife of Mr. Lewis Waller, junior,
but neither she nor Mrs. Grimston has
the worried married look. They have quite the
debutantish air, although they possess a dimpled
infant or two apiece. Miss Grimston is a daughter
of the well-known Kendalls, and is married to Mr.
Oscar Asche’s general manager. She is a brown-
eyed American-like young party, and will play the
millionaire widow in “The Flag Lieutenant.”
She looks as if she would do an all-conquering
(stage) relict to perfection, and give the Sydney
women palpitation of the heart with her dressing.
Miss Grimston made her debut with Rejane.
Hurrying through Melbourne, hitherwards, the
trio saw “The King of Cadonia,” and Mr. Waller
(whose salmon-colored charmeuse shirt and big
black satin tie are an education in color sense) re-
lucantly acknowledges that, in comparison, it
made the London production look shabby.
Miss Madge Titheradge must be making good
in London. She has been engaged as leading
feminine for the new Repertory Co. A most
affluent person, Lord Howard de Walden, is said
to be the bulwark and sheet-anchor and main-
deck of the enterprise. He had an ancestor who
helped to discover the Gunpowder Plot. Now the
latest of the line has done a nobler feat; he has
discovered Madge Titheradge.
Baronet Rupert Clarke went through to Mel-
bourne on Sunday night, hurrying over to say the
pleasant thing to his valuable imports, Oscar
Ascheand Lily Brayton.
Margaret Anglin’s wallaby died on the voyage
between India and England, but her rosella
parrot is alive and chattering. They were the
only Australian live stock she took from here, and
Americans are sympathetic over what they call
“both little animals.” The star told her inter-
viewers that nothing is too good to say about
Australia and its people.
The brothers Martin, barristers-at-law, vindi-
cated the liberty of the cab-hirer in heroic fashion
last week. It appears that on June 18 Mr. C. B.
Martin hailed, at the witching hour of 11.30 p.m.,
one Thomas, a licensed steerer of cabs, and bade
him drive him (C. B. Martin) and brother (George)
to Durno’s chemist’s shop in Pitt-street. The
varlet did so without demur. Outside Durno’s
lingered a lady (name unspecified), who en-
tered the vehicle and was driven with the
brothers to Phillip-street (portion, etc., unspeci-
fied). And here the conveyance discharged its
human cargo and trouble began. Mr. C. B. Martin
proffered 2s. in full discharge of all liabilities in-
curred on the trip. The driver rejected the same
with scorn, contumely and loathing, and set up
a sordid clamor for “three bob.” The brothers
essayed spiritedly to compromise for half-a-crown.
In vain. The fellow proved himself insatiable.
The position was now extremely strained, and
diplomatic relations were in danger of being
broken off. In fact, the Martin brothers nearly
recalled their ambassador from the cabman’s
court. A chill blast howled down the deserted
street; vagrom dogs, desisting for a space from
their fossickings among the rubbish-bins of the
Phillip bailiwick, gathered inquisitively about the
little band and yelped eerily ; and amid it all rose
and fell the passionate heart-cry or scream of the
horse-controller—“My fare’s three bob, an’ I’m
goin’ ter have it ! ” And at last, since an infuriated
cabman in a lonely thoroughfare at midnight or
thereabouts is an intolerable circumstance where-
with to bandy words, the three bob was unloosed.
Butlater —aswasonly meet and just—thedemander
of it got a writ, and at the Water Police Court
(6/7/’O9) the Mill of Retribution, turned by the
practised hand of S.M. Macfarlane, ground 40s.
out of him, together with 7s. costs. I only knew
one man who had the front, hide, rind or what
not to habitually hand cab-propellers the mini-
mum wage at a late hour ; but he was a speedy
and tireless runner, never drove with ladies, and
was almost stone deaf, so that he did not hear the
howls of surprise and so forth that the jehus in-
variably emitted on discovering the exact legality
of his payments.
Miss Decima Moore and her Major Gordon
Guggisberg—she married rather recently—are
pictured in a late Book Monthly over the inti-
mation that they have written a volume about “We
Two in West Africa.” The environment of the
pair is a West African village, but the warrior is
so beautifully groomed that his dressing-case was
evidently not a day’s march in the rear, or else
the heathen, despite his alleged blindness, as
mentioned in the hymn, must have been a good
valet. The picture suggested evening dress in the
Sahara.
A bitter cry from the “Servants Wanted”
column of the Otago (M.L.) Daily Times
■TyANTED, Young- LADY just from Home; no
washing ; no children ; the mistress cooks and
the boss cleans the boots.—North End, Times Office.
It’s always “the boss” who drafts advts. like
that. “Do remember to put an advertisement in
to-day,” Mrs. Can’tgetamaid says as she dishes
up the fried sausage in the morning; and the
satirical wretch, who doesn’t know what a serious
business he’s fooling with, goes and does things
like that!
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.
