MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, July 12, 1909.
My Dear Yarrik,—
Wednesday, at Flernington. was grisly weather
—grisly enough to frighten off the butterfly section
of society. But, all the same, the sports among
the people called Best turned up briskly. Mrs.
Alistair Clarke, cosily tucked into a squirrel coat
and turban, looked a match for the chilliest winds
that blow. A tall, thin bridesmaid Power,
dressed in drab, elbowed the new bride, Mrs.
Reggie Power, who trotted about in a short blue
frock, and held court among spinster pals. Sport-
ing Nancy Price topped her slender length with a
pink-and-grey lid like a Galah parrot. Maggie
Nihil, another lengthy spinster, chummed about
with a small Purvis maiden, in brown. There
were a power of tall women. Mrs. Cecil
Fisken lengthened her blue-clad self with
a monster blue turban, and played about
with Mrs. Donald Currie. Another Mrs.
Currie (warm entrees to be scattered about on a
cold day) floated an expensive, cruel and fascinat-
ing Bird of Paradise, tinted to a mermaiden bl ue, and
matched herself against a six-footer —Miss Box. A
daughter of the Douglas lady, from the Southern
Island, challenged the weather with sportive pink
roses in her hat. “Tiny” McKean, of the
six-feet-seven gear, brought his small wife and a
big woman dragging a tailored tail. Mrs. George
Russell was a pale lady. She is just venturing
out after her lengthy iliness. Mrs. Irquhurt was
briskly saying farewells preparatory to her gad
abrawd. She is a favorite dame. The Spinners,
those haw-ty fore-gatherers, who claw together
clothes for the Heathen, are passing round the
plate for a farewell present. The Raleigh beauty
pair were up to the starting point, as usual. They
never miss a butterfly flutter. Widow Durant
•clad herself in clinging black, and coupled with
Mrs. Montgomery’s white feather hat. All the
ladies ran in couples tucked under each other’s fur
wings for warmth, the sporting males having
abandoned them and rushed to back the slow
horse which leadeth unto destruction.
Pretty Mrs. George Chirnside has not 'put in an
appearance yet this winter whirl. She is another
convalescent after a lengthy illness. There was
nothing gubernatorial in sight on Wednesday.
The Box shivered desolately, wrapped in its blue
•and white toggery.
Flernington on Saturday was made up partly
of races, and partly of people looking at Lily
Brayton. The Brayton husband was there —a
bulldog, assertive, dominant type of man—but
the wife was the star in the ascendant. She is
beautiful in the fair appealing beauty that turns
the he person into a jellyfish ; and she is smart
in the frocky way that drags a woman’s admiration.
A woman may be beautiful as an houri, but if her
clothes don’t pass she’s done for feministically.
Lily Brayton has the air for clothes. Her amethyst
colored coat hung to perfection, and her ankles
and shoes and skirts were above reproach. It
matters not what they were made of —they had
the air. Her hat was a shovel—an ugly, beautiful
thing crammed on her head like an extinguisher,
and made up of the last two colors that have been
driven to harmonise in the face of long opposition
—pale purple (an anomaly in itself) and a striking
blue. There was a blue rose perched on her lid,
and she looked like Browning’s perverse maiden
who “ bid me gather her blue roses.” Sep. Miller
did the honors and showed the lady abroad,
whereat the men chuckled. Trust Sep. to
grab the beautifullest lady! Also a drab-
colored she of the clan made herself a constant
foil. Sep paraded his prize in the birdcage. He
trotted her behind the barrier railing to see
Workmaster—the horse to whom his whole soul
(and pocket) had gone out (then he carried off the
mummer lady to the far wilds and out toward the
Footscray Gate—why, Heaven only knows, unless
it was to introduce her to the Smell family.
All the admiring crew buzzed over to Grafnax,
the favorite, parading in superb indifference.
Tom Hogan was almost weeping on its shoulder.
Grafnax is such a replica of his horse Lomond,
which was grandsire, or granduncle, or some such
ancestral connection of Grafnax, and ran second
in the same race (some years ago) that Grafnax
should have won on Saturday, but didn’t.
The Malcolm McEacharns, of mayoral and
other fame hereabouts, are establishing themselves
with all due pomp at Galloway House, an estate
in Wigtonshire (Scotland) that the lord of
Goathlands has recently acquired. With great
outpouring of money the old place has been
modernised almost out of all decency and recog-
nition, and the old Scotch ghosts have trekked
away from it in disgust. Preparations are going on
for the wedding of the McEaeharn chicken and
her freshly-skewered legal M.P. Malcolm him-
self has been a sick man. His latest heart-attack
was so severe that he was for some time in a
hospital at Edinburgh. The latest news is that he
can now be moved to Galloway House, there to
finish his physical repairs.
The Art Students’ Evening last week, Was a
earnivalistic song and dance in the Guild Hall.
It was a sort of baby-understudy to the gorgeous
Mask Ball of the artists that rips up things in
Melbourne early in September. The youngsters
took the Guild Hall, raked out what fantasy they
could in dress, and prepared—with the help of a
particularly weak and aneemic orchestra—to be
devilishly wild in mask and domino. The balcony
was packed with spectators, lured there by a
passionate hope of the lurid. Assiduous Bohem-
ians, who pursue the Latin-Quartier idea with the
devotion of a Wagnerite for his cult, arrived with a
most anxious yearn for devilry. Architect-student
Synot exploited himself as pantomime Mrs.
Drinkwater. “ Baby ” Boyd, who made history
in the penniless push by selling £6O worth of can-
vas and paint in a “private view,” got himself
into his sister’s best nightie and kimona. Colin
Campbell showed plump nether limbs below a
short skirt, and used his pink cheeks as a girl gag.
Ettie Dyson hid herself behind the winkers of a
pair of gigantic Moorish earrings, and Brother
Bill strode defiantly in a black villainish cloak
and mask. Nell Mackenzie had Souter’s cat
careering madly over her white satin gown. Will
Moore did an imposing Napoleonic stunt. Hugh
Patterson’s pretty daughter, the youngest student,
Greenaway-ed with MacGinnises. The crowd
kept up its giddy gaiety till cockcrow—as is
written in all Bohemian precedent.
A poster carnival drew a swarm of human, and
some inhuman fantasies to the South Yarra Skat-
ing Rink last week. Quite the best part of the
suburb packed the somewhat exaggerated shed to
watch the revellers, but it was patent that sheer
revelry for its own sake had perished from ex-
posure to the chill night air. Scarcely a monster
smiled—the most deliberate nightmares could not
even laugli at themselves—and the mirthless mien
of the spectators was more in keeping with a Dead
March than with the pretty, sparkling lamps, and
the ruui-ti-tnm of the hired orchestra. Still the
serious beings in gay attire spread out and whirled
their best for the prizes, soon to be presented by
a reckless management. A damsel with scarlet
lips, brilliant clothes, and a coat 'of artificial sun-
burn, chased through the throng as an ad. for
a well-known boarding-house beverage. She was
closely followed by a skeleton. Then came an equine
oddity with Hackenschmidt legs and a bray. A
Beelzebub, in hopeless, moaning black, lost his
terrors beside the elegant and familiar vision
of The Lone Hand beauty winner—Miss
Buckridge. The lady, garbed in frugal white,
might have been a sorceress from a cool
store or a Polar region, and was the simplest and
most attractive figure on the rink. Australia
and Ireland were badly represented, the
first by the red shirt peculiar to War
Cry almanacs; and the second by the green
swallow-tiiil coat and knee-breeches of popular
fancy. An interesting set of freaks was a com-
munity of black bears. Near by was a bunch of
footballers, a Dutchman crowned by a whirring
windmill, and a Spanish hidalgo like an illus-
tration on a piece of music. Also there was a
creature, half man and half donkey, which Was
probably a parable, or a believer in the giving
away of Dreadnoughts.
Mrs. Best is breaking into the political ranks
alongside her energetic senatorial hubby. She is
laying the foundations of a political salon. The
idea will be a “musical programme, interspersed
with the discussion of current politics,” and the
first brawny and sky-towering attempt was to
occur on Monday, July 12th, at the Best mansion,
Oratava, in East St. Kilda. Recently, I men-
tioned “ tramless East St. Kilda,” and an unkind
printer made it brainless. That printer evidently
doesn’t know the finesse and mental capacity
which collect in that subbub. Both these
qualities are about to be let loose on “current
politics.”
A granddaughter of one of the most imposing
chiffon stores is darkly suspected by her girl pals
of cunningly concealing an engagement. They
watch eagerly to pounce on the sedate public
announcement which the maiden tantalisingly
delays. Moreover, they can’t understand why
they are kept waiting. Schemes for kidnapping
the suspect and extracting a confession by torture
are rife, but it is impossible to decide whether to
stick hot needles into her or to stick them in cold,
and this dissension in the camp of the supposed
fiancee's best friends prevents anything being
done.
One of the last of “ Bijou ” Wilson daughters not
yet bagged matrimonially has promised to slip her
graceful form into the noose. Flo Wilson, a
handsome Amazon who has been an inseparable
pal to her sister, Mrs. Softgoods Butler, has
promised her excellent tennis and golf hand to
Maoriland squatter Poison. Poison boasts several
inches over a fathom, so he towers even over his
tail fiancee.
Matrimonial noosings float about, like so many
spider gossamers on a frosty morning. Roland
Church, son of philanthropist Church, has toed
the chalk line and laid his fortune and quantity
of good looks at the feet of Irene, daughter of
Donaldson, the magnate of the Widows’ Fund
Life Assurance lookout.
There is a big crowd getting its smile ready for
a welcome to the Ward company of comedians.
It is not only Hugh Ward who is a spanking
favorite, but there are also our own local Rosie
Musgrove and Celia Ghiloni, and the extra load
of “how do” that will come to the Palotta. The
crowd arrives on Friday and opens on Saturday,
so the big reception—the real event—is postponed
till Monday. Then the Grand is to be turned
topsy turvy. Mr. McPhairson, who is husband to
the Ghiloni, is already in Melbourne strutting the
streets impatiently until the arrival of his bride.
Manager Goldie has lost hold on his girl
also—she rushed over to Sydney to her people,
but returns before the First “Night. Duicie
Deamer Goldie and her husband intend to live in
Sydney and simply knock things with their house.
They have collected 183 J cases of goods—tapes-
tries and bronzes and rugs, from the black-yellow
portion of the world whither they have been
wandering.
The ballet in “ The King of Cadonia” is purely
Slavonic and most picturesque. It is decked with
short, soft, parti-colored frocks and high (so-called)
Russian boots of red leather. On the first night
there were eitrht girls—eight little ballet girls all
in a row. They danced the Slavonic dance and then
there were six. Jennie Brennan went to much
trouble teaching certain steps in this most strenuous
dance, and two girls, not quite perfect, strained
sinews in their legs. They were carried off the
stage, and are in the doctor’s hands. The
management cut out the worst movement, and
then ordered the rest of the ballet to be massaged
after every performance at its (the management’s)
expense. The stock theatrical joke about grand-
mothers in the ballet does not get a look in with
this lot. They are all very young girls—charm-
ingly young and fresh—who take their dancing
very seriously.
The University took itself frivolously on Thurs-
day, and had a night out at Her Majesty's Theatre.
The whole theatre was theirs for the time being
—also, the King of Cadonia and all his minions,
who interlarded their lines with University jokes.
Students packed the gallery, and behaved as well
as could be expected, while other students got
themselves into the current uniform of black
and white gear, and behaved as proper
escorts for their prettily-dressed mothers and
sisters. They are a handsorne well-groomed
lot, on the whole, the womenkind of Pro-
fessordom. One Prof, had apparently been
dining with too red a wine. The students near
by wreathed his neck in hero fashion with some of
the vegetable festoons that adorned the balcony,
and then stuck red roses in his crush hat. It
looked quite effective when he floundered out at
the interval. The Chief Justice and his lady, with
a party of youngsters, had the manager’s box,
which was packed in with violets. Wattle and gum
leaves hid the staple support of the balcony, and
scarlet roses were.-wreathed round the gilt posts.
All the mummer ladies wore the purple and black
of the University colors somewhere about their
toggery. The students had the stage and a drop
curtain to themselves in the interval, and ex-
changed the giddy song with the still giddier gag
of fantastic students “in front.”
Spent a gladsome afternoon lately, perched
up on a heap of coke in Web Gilbert’s foundry,
watching Melbourne’s own sculptor cast a bronze.
It was the head of John Shirlow, the well-known
etcher, and it was a successful cast—a bit sand-
burnt, but wonderfully good, the expression
being marvellous. The company kept at a
respectable distance whilst the handsome sculptor
raised aloft in a great clay vessel 901 b., good
measure, of molten copper, and poured it into the
sand.
Web Gilbert is a genius, and he makes an im-
pressive figure in his foundry. He is a study in
copper color—a beautiful blend of the Village
Blacksmith in his young and j^bd-looking days,
and an alchemist of old. His pretty Titian-hailed
wife is a golden talker, and an ideal hostess.
Saw a model of Gilbert’s Hew statue. It has a
remarkable and dramatic motive—a man terrified
at the approach of Death —and the attitude is
'that of a man with a strange fear upon him.
Our Greeks, including the pedlars of the bounty
of Pomona (one must live up to the language of
Pericles and Oleon, the tanner) and the acolytes
of Flora, are jumping on their greasy caps with
delight because their community has been per-
sonally smiled on by their Queen, Olga of Hellas
has sent a valuable present of church Vestments fed
be worn by the Greek parson here in Melb'Oiir’rie.
The present person who is Greek for pars'Oh
wallows in the simple battle of th'e Rev.
Archimandrite TheophilosPapacobstantino, and if
your Johanna is sweating unbeknownst, she hopes
to be forgiven. Probably Olga was so pleased at
being able to leatH all that off by heart that she
sent the gorgeous robes just to show off. Mr.
Archie Theo P. Constantine has the honor to be a
personal pal of the royal lady of Athens.
Majah Bargrave Wilson gave the bride away,
with a charmeuse frock and a lace veil and a
coupon. The new Mrs. Henniker Wilson went
off honeymooning to the Isle of Wight in Polar
furs and a Venetian blue frock.
From “Rosna,” of Adelaide :
Between dances our new Admiral-Governor has taken
train to Oodnadatta ; not because there is anything par-
ticular to see at, Oodnadatta, but simply because you
can’t go any farther than that by rail, and his Ex. wanted
to make a complete jolt of his northern explorations while
he was about them. How he got there and what he did
after the local publican had shaken him by the hand and
asked him to give it a name, I don’t know; the farthest
we have got in the way of news is that, on the long road
up, numerous flags and addresses were waved at the
party ; and at one place his Ex. was urged to come again
and bring the girls with him.
The Exhibition building is a big barn to fill; yet the
Seamen’s Mission Ball did it on Tuesday night. The
dancing populace had its work to get round in comfort,
and even then there were several tiers of wall flowers in
the lower galleries all the evening. As for the common
or Sarah Ann galleries, they were let out at 2s. a head,
and were filled at the price. A big batch of fancy sets
had been arranged by an energetic dame or two. Mrs.
Lendon had a Dutch set, with her own damsel, Alice
Campbell, the Arthur Rymills,' and a dozen more in it,
and everything about that garden was lovely. Mrs.
Herbert Rymill’s Early Victorian dames had ringlets and
poke bonnets, and little tight satin bodices, flounced
muslin skirts and short mittens. They looked demure
and twenty, though there were only two unmarried
maids among them. Another set that took the eye for
its beauty was devoted to “The Merry Widow.” Miss
Waite, as hon. sec., put tons of energy into the organising ;
and for once in a lifetime the Seamen’s Mission will be
almost affluent.
The Alexander Hays, mother and daughter, having
been burned out of house and home and Victor Harbor,
sailed off by the Waratah last week to see how England
feels about it. Since their homeful of statuary and
beautiful things was cremated, they have lived at the
South Australian, and it’s nob likely they’ll go back to
Port Victor. They have always been persistent gadders,
with a fondness for suddenly darting off to the small and
(in patches) crowded Isles of Edward.
Adelaide ’Varsity has long been in need of a Sports
Ground of its own, as befitteth its dignity. And it is
going, to have one at last. Hitherto the undergrad has
had to do his morning sprint on alien soil, and to wipe
some other oval with his enemies ; but soon a special
portion of the earth will be dedicated to these rites. It
is down in the picturesque neighborhood of Frome-road,
and its proximity to the Zoo is said to have no signifi-
cance. A pavilion is already being erected on it to pro-
tect the best millinery of Mrs. ’Varsity and the girls.
Ola Jane Humphrey wept her way out of Adelaide on
Thursday by the Macedonia, and a select and sighing
audience laid flowers in her cabin, and hoped that she
wouldn’t he too long absent from Australia. In return
Ola Jane, who knows she has made many friends in these
parts, held out some misty promises.
The H. C. Swans are on their way back to the Kan-
garoo country after two years spent in looking for some
foggy islands mentioned by Julius Caesar in his despatches
to Rome—it was some time ago ; I forget the date. They
—tiiat is, the Swans—found the islands all right; so now
they are drifting back, pausing a moment in Singapore,
where the eldest son manages a dock or some unwieldy
thing like that.
Ex-Agent-General Jenkins bustled in from England the
other day, and said that if he had come a minute sooner
he’d have had a run for his old district; for the late
Premier Price was a colleague of his. Having come too
late for that, he darted on to Melbourne and the other
States for a glance round before settling down in S. A.
The musical drought is being broken by some Chamber
Music matinees, given by those two ardent musical p ds,
Willie Silver and Eugene Alderman. They happen in the
banqueting-room of the Town Hall, and the musical Elect
go to them and listen in ecstasy to Pvzqrstyhmntrswhkb-
ski and other dead but living composers.
Talking of noise, though, we are soon to get it by the
furlong; for the Cliapinan-Alexander missioners are on
our front-door mat, and the advance guard is already
asking us to sing hymns.
Adelaide, scribbler Beaumont Smith, who went to
Britain as private sec/to Win. Anderson, the gentleman
who provides Australia with its theatrical thrills, had
some excitement in Colombo, where a fierce stallion
seized him by the chest and waved him round the island.
There were hysterics from the women of the party, and
all manner of thrilling accompaniments to the adventure,
including some d ys under the eye of the doctor.
There is a good deal of the comic opera about us in
Adelaide. When we go forth to rob and plunder, we
always do it decently clothed in our Sunday masks and
.other villainly accoutrements. The other evening two
frightful desperadoes climbed up into the Fred Scarfes’
house, while the owners were out at a ball. The timidest
maid heard a noise, and going up-stairs confronted the
masked villains, who fled without stealing so much even
as a diamofid tiara.
Little Salisbury has had its day out, and has been
jubilating in the presence of many members of Parlia-
ment and much roast pig and fowl. The little township
on the Northern line has a rather curious history.
Fifteen years or so ago it had visions of being one great
undertaker’s shop and monumental mason’s yard ; fora
new and vast cemetery for the city was laid out there,
and planted with mournful trees. Then suddenly the
Government changed its mind, or the State changed i's
Government, or something, and it was decided that Salis-
bury was too remote from the city. But just when it was
thinking over the chance it had missed, somebody poinled
to a solitary garden which was flourishing amazingly, and
asked why they shouldn’t plant oranges since they were
forbidden to plant corpses. Apparently nobody could
tell why they shouldn’t; so orange groves sprang up by
the dozen, and the very finest fruit sold in S. Australia
(and we can grow fruit) comes from the banks of the
Para. Ih'fadt, hj*reht traveller, who perietrkted as far as
Sydney the6ther week, tells me thkt “Salisbury oranges”
a're how g‘i veil the honor in Sydney shops—arid
Pm wondering ivhpt has become of Parramatta. ,
Austrhlih’s Crossley got a new brother-in-law last
week, When one of the Muecke girls—a sister of Ada's 'tall
doctor—was married to Voss Smith, who general-managed
one of the Broken Hill mines.

KODAK’S NEW HOME
IN SYDNEY.
Opened Ist July, 1909.
JHE consistent (development of a business is a
sure sign of its character, and the splendid
growth of this Firm is ample testimony to the care-
ful organisation and business methods back of the
Company. Not only that, but it is significant of
the great popularity enjoyed by the “Kodak"
goods—Cameras and Photographic Supplies that
have a world-wide reputation for high quality.
The new building will enable us to give even
greater consideration to our customers.
The Shop Floor is intended for General
Selling purposes, and is handsomely finished. The
main portion of the First Floor will be given
almost wholly to the interests of Professional
Photographers. The Second Floor is a Stock
Room. Demonstration Rooms, splendidly equipped
and fitted, are provided on the Third Floor ; and
the lop Floor is filled with Reserve Stock, and
provides room for a Camera Repairing and
Building Workshop.
Many amateurs entrust their developing and
printing to us, and we have made superior accom-
modation for this work in the New Building;. A
Gallery for the display of Cinematograph Films is
also installed ; and we have, in fact, done everything
we could possibly think of to make the Establish-
ment convenient to our Customers.
This is the Finest Building wholly devoted
to Photographic Work in the Southern Hemi-
sphere, and we extend a cordial invitation to you to
call and go through the building.
The famous Kodak, Brownie, Premo and
Graflex Cameras are well displayed, and samples
of the work they can do are freely exhibited. Any
day will do for a visit ; but make a point of
inspecting the handsome building and viewing the
interior arrangements.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Bribery’s victory at Flemington last Saturday
places the Malvolio gelding second to Redleap in the
Grand National records. Redleap won the Hurdle Race
twice and the Steeple once, putting: another Steeple to
his credit at Caulfield a month after he had scored the
double at Flemington. Bribery has now won both a
Hurdle (in 1907) and Steeple. With the phenomenal
Redleap exception, this new hero is the only jumper that
has won over the little sticks as well as over the fences,
and, moreover, his weight last Saturday was within 61b.
of the heaviest that ever was humped to the fore. On
his best days Bribery has no rival at the game, and
Saturday must have "been one of his very best. The
allegation that Grafnax was bound to have won if he had
“ stood up ”is only a surmise, anyhow. The favorite, as
it happened, came to grief at the second last jump when
he was leading- the field, but the top-weight was then not
more than three lengths behind. And it is more than
likely that Grafnax was beginning to have enough of it
when he hit a panel.
A said-to-be-dangerou 3 light-weight in Ripon
(9st. 51b.) blundered early in the race, as might have been
expected. Ripon was the unhappiest customer of all the
crowd. Before he left the “ birdcage” he was trembling
like aT jelly on a jaunting-car, and before he faced the
barrier the nervous novice was in a sweat. Of the 10 nags
that stood up nothing had a chance against the winner,
the most conspicuous failure being Pilot, who probably
hurt himself somehow. On the day of the race Pilot was
the popular fancy, and 200 admirers, more or less, stood
around him in a ring to see him saddled up. After the
race Bribery had the circle of admirers, and then it was
wisely remarked that he was far and away the hand-
somest steeplechaser in the business. He has only one un-
handsome characteristic. Like many other good jumpers,
the level-backed son of Malvolio is rather pigeon-toed in
front.
* * * *
A good while ago, that irrepressible identity,
Bob Sutton Sievier, in his audacious Cockney sporting
paper, the Winning Post, made some lamentably truthful
references to what is called “sport ” in the most sporting
districts of this country. Mr. G. G. Stead, the Maori-
lander, had been remarking that the big or small race-
courses in England are not nearly such well-appointed
show-places as the principal enclosed courses in Aus-
tralia. Mr. Bob Sutton, without directly contradicting
what he knows, from personal experience, to be a fact,
went on to “draw Mr. Stead’s attention to those dis-
graceful, ramping places which, in the colonies, are
designated ‘up country’ courses.” Bob’s utterances
on this point can be commended to the thoughtful
consideration of all the honest horse-loving Jugginses
who Suffer for their annual gamble at a petty race meet-
ing. “ Can Mr. Stead,” he asked, scornfully, “ name any
racecourse in England where similar surroundings pre-
vail? It is common knowledge that all sorts of nefarious
practices take place at these gatherings, and that
trickery reigns in place of sport when a ‘double’ or
‘treble’ has to be accomplished by hook or crook.
‘Cutting up’ is too delicate a term to apply to the
majority of up-country meetings, ‘ chopping up ’ would
better express the common situation.” Although Bob
Sutton has been 20 years away from Australia, his know-
ledge of his subject is quite up to date. The ramping-
places he speaks of have doubled in number since he yelled
the odds at Flemington and Randwick, and the rampers
are twice as brazen as they were in his day. The extra-
ordinary thing is that folks, like Australians, who really
have a keen liking for a good horse-race should find any
sort of pleasure in witnessing these choppings-up. They
wouldn’t waste their time and money on any other kind
of notoriously “cronk” contest, but because horses are
concerned in'the rogue meetings they cheerfully submit
to be robbed. If, perchance, they back a winner they
haven’t even the satisfaction of supposing that the losers
were having a go. The Lord only knows where their
notion of “sport” comes in.
i m * * *
In some respects, the sculling match between
Messenger and Kemp on .Parramatta River on Saturday
was unique. During the week both scullers were laid out
with influenza, and at one time Messenger saw the Pearly
Gates quite distinctly. They were in the act of being
opened for him—in other words, his condition was critical.
Yet both men recovered sufficiently to come to the start-
ing post;. Veteran -Beach was referee, and N. J. Macdonald
was judge. There were two false beginnings, but at the
third time of asking they got away to a good start,
Messenger going to the front. Then Kemp came with a
great rush, drew level, and presently was a little way
ahead ; but Messenger hung to the stern of his rival with
grim persistency. At the mile he again drew level. At
Gabarita Messenger was a good five lengths to the front;
but at Gladesville Kemp began to fairly rush through the
water. On shore and afloat the spectators went
absolutely mad ; and howled to the Deity to say
had He ever seen anything like it. So fast did
Kemp come that the gap was soon reduced to just
half a length. This was the crisis of the race. Messenger
pulled himself together for the final spurt, and sculled
for his life, gaining about half a length at the first. Kemp
made frantic efforts, but Messenger just managed to
drive his shell across the winning line about a length to
the good. Then he collapsed. Kemp saw his distress,
and grasped the rigger of the boat just in time to prevent
the fainting victor falling overboard. After that the men
shook hands, and the defeated sculler held his conqueror
up till a motor launch came along and took him on
board. Kemp was badly done up also, but was able to
paddle back to his father’s shed amidst thunders of
applause. As a matter of sober fact, neither man was
really fit to row, though the time was 21min. 35sec.
That final rush will be long remembered.
* * * *
The Pearce-Mitchell sculling match takes place
on the Parramatta River (Syd.), Saturday next. The
chief incentive is the £lOO a side and the championship of
Australia: and as both men have made the final deposits
everything in the garden is beautiful. A minor item in
the struggle is “ The William Beach Cup,” which will
serve to keep the memory of the veteran green after he
has ceased to take an interest in the sport. At present,
he is going strong. *
Just how much reliance can be placed on the
average newspaper statement as to form was very ap-
parent in the Kemp-Messenger race. On the Wednesday
before the race Sydney Tklegraph announced: —“Both
scullers have undergone a thorough preparation, and are
toned up to ‘concert pitch.’” And just at the precise
moment that statement appeared in print both men were
bad with’flu’.
The news that sundry Webb backers have in-
timated that they are ready and willing to put up a third
stake for James of that ilk, if he desires to have another
contest with Arnst, suggests several things. One is that,
to certain minds, the athlete becomes a fetich. Webb has
been thoroughly beaten by Arnst on two occasions, and
even though he should win at the third time of asking it
would not prove that Webb was the better man, but
merely that he had luck for once. This same blind
worship is probably one of the secrets of the long run of
some politicians.
* * * *
After severe wallopings on the previous Satur-
day and Wednesday, Queensland Inter-State Rugby
Union team showed an amazing and unexpected improve-
ment of form on Saturday last. In the first half it gal-
loped over the astounded N.S.W. men, and scored 11
points to the home team’s five. Being tired of walk-over
games, the public was almost unanimous in keeping away.
A miserable 10,000 only rolled up—less than trots along
to see a Test cricket match in the Cold Country. The
speed and ferocity of the visiting forwards was good to
see ; and right through the first half it looked as though
Queensland had arrived at the end of the long lane. But
the second half saw it peter out badly; and, to crown
disaster, that sterling half-back, J. Carmichael, received
a bump that resulted in temporary concussion, and he
had to retire. This was the last straw ; one man short,
the struggling team had no chance, and the Waters, who,
all through the second spell, had been gaining steadily,
now rolled the Northerners right up, and finally won
by 21 points to 11.
That there was the makings of a first-class team in
the Queensland XV. was obvious by the way the men im-
proved. Apparently they were quite raw when they
arrived in Sydney, and had had no practice together.
Which reflects considerably on the management of the
Queensland Rugby Union. The result was that after the
first match public interest dropped, and after the second
it died right out. Queensland’s reputation suffers; the
pockets of the two Unions suffer ; and the men are made
a holy show of because the Rugby Union consists of
muddlers, who send practically untrained players against
a veteran force. Queensland should speak severely to its
Rugby Union.
• • * *
The Soccer competition for the Rawson Cup
raged, as usual, on Saturday, with the result that the
same old Pvrmont retains its position right on the apex
of the heap,, with 21 competition points; having, this
week, fulfilled the saying of the local prophets that
Ultimo would have no blanky hope. And it hadn’t. At
half-time, Pvrmont had one goal to nil, and also had
Ultimo about dead to the world. In the second half the
foredoomed team cracked right up, and the Pyrmonters
added six more goals, and won by seven goals to nil.
Next in the competition is Rozelle, with 17 point*. On
Saturday it also won its match, downing Western
Suburbs by three goals to one. This game was specially
attractive, for both sides indulged in some very pretty
dribbling, and did it under difficulties. But it was
superior shooting that won the match for Rozelle. Syd-
ney occupies third place in the competition, with 15
points; it did not play in the competition on Saturday,
but filled in the time by walloping a team from the s.s.
Moldavia by three goals to one. Granville downed
Northern Suburbs by three goals to one. Of course, there
is no question about the destination of the Cup ; barring
a miracle Pvrmont has it, with its 10 wins and one, draw
out of 11 games, for the season. The only miracle, even,
that can affect it is the death of the whole club.
* * * *
Sydney Metropolitan Rugby Union did a climb
down with the Svdnev Cricket Ground Trustees, and
swallowed the Trust’s terms wholesale—ls per cent, gross
takings and all exes, paid by the Union. It also agrees to
provide one first-grade match there every Saturday during-
the next three seasons. Of course, the Rugby Union didn’t
really want the ground—it has more grounds now than it
can use ; but it did want to stop the League getting it,
and the fear that the League would get it caused the
Union to swallow the whole leek. There are nine
district clubs in the Union and it controls a whole
host of grounds, chief of which are Sydney Cricket
Ground, the Sports Ground, University Oval, Rushcutters’
Bav, North Sydney Oval, Hamnden Park, Upping Ground,
Erskineville Oval, St. Luke’s Park, besides grounds
at Parramatta, Mosman, Manly, Randwick and other
places. This huge grab is going to bring trouble one of
these days. Sooner or later there will be a revolt against
a system which allows any single body to get a monopoly
like this of the public recreation areas, for the purpose
of preventing rivals supplying the public with amuse-
ments.
* * * *
The Rugby League matches on Saturday last
were worthy "of better public support than they
received; the ga,mes at Sydney Agricultural Ground
being first-class exhibitions, especially that between
South Sydney and Eastern Suburbs. Both teams
were in the pink of condition. The forwards played
admirably, and, although the game was fast, both
teams were in such excellent order that the slightest slip
on the opposing side meant disaster. South Sydney had
the extra pound of excellence, and finally won by 16
points to five. The score would indicate an uneven game
to watch ; but it wasn’t; it was all excitement from the
jump to the finish. The wreckage of North Sydney by
Western Suburbs on the same ground was also an ex-
citing struggle ; West won by seven points to three.
Across at Wentworth Park about 500 people rolled up to
watch Glebe just scrape out on top of Newtown with 10
points to eight; and at Birchgrove, Balmain fell on New-
castle, and rolled it up by five to two, after a game which
bristled with deeds of derring-do.
As to Melbourne’s ’ball, there was as much play
of a different sort through the week as on the half-
holiday. Those “ points ” that a ring-referee would have
counted—referred to last week—were duly noted, where-
fore four Richmondites and a Carltonian were cited
before the delegates to excuse themselves. Some suc-
ceeded, others failed hopelessly. One Riehmondite was
passed out of the League “for all time,” another was
suspended for a month, and a third cautioned; one was
acquitted and another remanded, as it were. This is
about the stage in the football season when a couple
of defeats, sustained by an inferior team, mean the
loss of all semi-final chances, consequently the
players rush into the fray something extra, and the
barrackers lose their heads entirely. Unfortunately for
themselves the League’bailers have overlooked the recent
formation of an Umpires’ Association, the effect of which is
now beginning to be felt. Nevertheless, this writer has
no congratulations to spare on either umpires or League
delegates for their judicial performances. There were
other ’bailers besides the four from Richmond who badly
wanted reporting, and the permanent disqualification of
a man with a hitherto unblemished record only serves to
call attention to the recent remission of a sentence passed
on a player with a much worse football past. A sentence of
suspension for the rest of the season would have satisfied
everybody, except the culprit.
There must have been some pugnacious element
in the atmosphere of the Cabbage Garden, Saturday
week, for, besides the Richmond-Melbourne disturb-
ance, the discussion between Newtown and North Gee-
long, at the Sleepy City, was capsized by barrackers,
who smote all and sundry and kept three Johns busy for
a full half-hour. Also at Benalla, one of the locals
“jobbed” a Burramine ’bailer, whereupon the latter’s
old man jumped the fence, biffed the assailant
in the eye, and started a gorgeous free-fight. After
that the valiant barrackers chased the umpire to
his hotel and thence to the train, and the air was full of
eggs. Also, at Bendigo, Eaglehawk and California Gully
teams got into holts and set out to slay each other. As
for North Melbourne, of the Ass., that club’s ground will
presently have to be disqualified. The players are all
right, but the gangs of young hoodlums who call them-
selves “ supporters” require grape-shot. The blue metal
the City Council puts on the roads disappears so fast that
it is no Wonder the tracks in that locality are a disgrace.
For nearly all these outbreaks the blastiferous
“ barracker ” is responsible, and on Saturday lie let him-
self loose with fearful violence at the Carlton-South con-
cussion ; but the calamity of Richmond had impressed
itself on the ’bailers, and they heeded the fool-barracker
not at all. The performance drew a 33,000 “ house,”
worth £619, and turned out an episode worth}’ of deep
thought. There was considerable betting through the
week at evens, but on Saturday morning South’s backers
were asking for 5 and 6 to 4, or they would take a couple
of goals, though on form the odds should have been
reversed. Anyhow, the South men went off with
a rush, and the first change saw’ them with 20 points
to Carlton’s four. Then they slowed down a bit,
but at the interval the board remarked : South 4 goals
4 behinds, Carlton 11 behinds. Up to then the latter
team couldn’t kick a goal to save its life ; but even allow-
ing for that, South was more than holding its own in the
play. Returning to the fray, Carlton took complete pos-
session of affairs, Skinner’s Hirelings being apparently-
lost. In 20 minutes it potted nine straight goals, scoring
56 points to South’s one in the quarter. Then it was that
the staggered barrackers raged and blasphemed, urging
their pets to “get to them,” and so forth. The flaunted
red-and-w'hite flags were torn dowm and trampled in the
mud, the cabbage-sized rosettes snatched and tossed
away in anger; but the South men heeded not.
They were beaten and done. There wasn’t a kick left
in them till within five minutes of time ; then a couple of
goals made the disaster look less awful than before.
The final points—6B to. 44—are nothing out of the way,
but that 20 minutes, when nine goals were scored against
the mighty South, remains unaccountable. Beside this
tragedy the collisions of ’Wood and St. Hilda (84 to 44),
’Roy and Geelong (87 to 61), and ’Varsity and Richmond
(81 to 50) were mere details. The Essendon-Melbourne
encounter was interesting inasmuch as it indicated the
improvement of the “Same Old” and the decadence of
Melbourne—4o to 30 being the tally. Each of these two
clubs is now’ doing its darnedest to keep the other out of
the semi-finals ; while ’Varsity still has hopes of outing
both of them.
The Association collisions were mostly of a
placid order, though the meeting of ’Town and Port w'as
the ciuse of much “blime” and fervid expectoration.
Most of ’Town’s team crossed to the Port by the Gem or
Queen or whatever Deadhorse it is that now sails the
wintry seas of the soup tureen, and when they started
bogging-in unpleasant memories of their sea voyage over-
came them, and they couldn’t bog worth tuppence.
Consequently they never got sufficient of a lead to swear
bv, and though they finally placed three more points on
the board than Port*—37 to 34—they were glad when the
upheaval was over. Melbourne’s soup tureen is not a thing
to be trifled with by an athlete about to undertake an
afternoon’s toil. Brighton tasted the bitterness of defeat at
th ■ feet of Essendon (42 to 57); Brunswick polished off
Northcote, 8S to 24 being the notification to all whom it
concerned ; and ’Scrav genially spread itself over Pres-
ton’s ground. and casually acquired 71 points to the locals’
39. North visited the mighty P’ran and put up the best
game it has played this season, scoring 70 points to 92.
North is understood to have no bought players, but to be
training the local youths in the way of the good ’bailer ;
and though that way is long and rocky, the club is here-
by adjured to get into the breach and to continue there,
in the sure and certain knowledge that there is more
virtue in one day’s hard play than in ten victories gained
by bought players.
* * * *
The interesting feature of the Maoriland Rugby
League team in N.S.W. and Queensland was its record for
the trip. Of 10 games it won five and lost five. It scored
181 points against its opponents, and they scored exactly
the same number against it. What are the chances of
that sort of thing occurring again ?
■* * * *
The Maori team, which is to play in Sydney
under the banner of the League, is now in being; and
last Saturday it went down before Auckland by 14 points
to 24. The Auckland team was a particularly strong
one, containing several of the All Blacks who toured
England ; and the Maoris are credited with having put
up a good strugglement.
■*** *■
N.S.W. Rugby Union has invited Wales to
send along a team to get a head put on it. Welshmen
rarely let an invitation like that go by, so there will pro-
bably be a band of Taffies here next season.
* * * *
N.S.W. Rugby League is standing up and
hitting out from the shoulder this season. It has written
in blood that any Leaguer who applies to the Union for
reinstatement shall be disqualified for life —and also in
the next world, if possible.
* * * *
With the advent of fine weather the Children of
the Suh who are playing cricket in England began to
buck up to considerable purpose. Since England rubbed
the Kangaroo’s nose in the slush in the first Test the XI.
has not lost a match, and the record now stands: Of 19
matches played eight have been won, three lost, and
eight drawn.' The team is now exactly half way through
its appointed tour, and if the sun continues to shine, the
first Board of Control XI. will probably do as well asmost
of its predecessors.
* * * *
In the Worcestershire cricket match, Ransford
(138) added another century to the long list of similar
performances. This is the twelfth occasion on which
Ransford has reached three figures since he started in
1904 ; and it is his third century in England. His highest
score was 182 against N.S.W. for Victoria at Sydney in
the 1908-9 season.
*• * * *
N.S.W. Cricket Association is still keeping the
bristles on its back fairly stiff when it looks at the Cricket
Ground Trust. The Trust demands 10 per cent, on gross
takings, all exes, to be paidby the Association, free seating
for all ground members, and an agreement for two years.
Rushcutters’ Bay asks five per cent, on gross takings
for five years; no members. Wentworth Park asked for
five per cent, on the gross, and agreed to share proceeds
of booths with the Association. It asked for the right to
issue 200 members’ tickets, and offered the same right to
the Association, five per cent, of the money to go the
Trustees or the Association from each others members
tickets. The executive committee of the N.S.W.O.A.
recommended that the offer be accepted ; but the
Association decided to give the Cricket Ground Trust just
one more chance. The Trust, however, apparently still
believes that the Cricket Association is only bluffing, and
doesn’t seem to want any chance.
* * * *
“Silas”: The Vic. Amateur Athletic Ass.’s
boxing competitions have drawn well at the local
Athenaeum, and up to time of writing had. produced a
couple who should make large black marks in Common-
wealth competitions. Robinson, a bantam, is a Ballarat
product, with professional neatness of execution, and he
plugs with the jov of a heaven-born pug. The other is
Fullalove, a middleweight of 18, with remarkable dash,
and a pair of good hands. On the third night of the com-
petition he beat McGuinness, a heavyweight with a bonus
of two stone and a great reputation as a rougher-up of
things. The heavyweight was swiped out in less than
two rounds,\and then Fullalove looked hungrily around
to see if there were any more giants on the horizon. It
will take a man with some zeal and strength and a fair
amount of science to stop him.

Bill Squires, writing as an aspiring Hayseed to
one of the Porter brothers, asks to have a battle arranged
for him in Melbourne at Cup time. He says he is bigger
and stronger and fitter than he has been for a long time,
and moreover he has GOT his poncii back. Bill got Ins
punch back in most of his recent fights—with interest.
Ttys healthy state of Melbourne pedestrianism
was Shibited on Saturday, when 78 enthusiasts had a
run over four miles of the Bulla-road, North Essendon.
The pack included the two Marathonians, George Blake and
W. V. Aitken, who both fled over the course in fine time.
Blake ate up the distance in the record space of
21min. 7sec., while Aitken consumed it in 21min. 37sec.
Two or three others, all belonging to the Essendon
Harriers, by the way, and numbering among them A. W.
McMicken and J. A. Stillwell, completed their trouble in
times varying from 21miij. 47sec. to 22min. 7sec.
* * * *
N.S.W. Hockey Association is not going to
take any chances over the invasion of Victoria. Four
practice matches will decide which players will be picked ;
and then only those who are in a position to promise to
go without fail will be chosen. When the choice has been
made the team will' be properly exercised together; so
that if it does get walloped it will not be the fault of the
Association, but the malign conduct of the Victorians in
selecting a better team. The Hockey Association might
lend its managers to some of the Australian Rugby Unions
—they want men of foresight very badly.
* * ■* *
H. D. Mclntosh is bringing ancient warrior
Bob Fitzsimmons to Australia with an idea of finding a
few budding world champions. In his pictures, Robert
looks like an up-to-date stockbroker, or something of that
sort; and Mrs. Bob is just a dream. When Bob fought
O’Brien and got beaten, Mrs. Bob had temporarily left
him ; and Bob explained after the battle that he couldn’t
hit O’Brien because he could not see the prancing man
from Philadelphia ; he saw only his wife’s lovely face ; and
to hit that, although it was only a phantom, was imposs-
ible. After gazing on the photo, of the lady, writer quite
understands how Robert felt.
* * » ■*
“S.S.”: The Bulletin has printed the sad
story of Joe Grimm, the Italian pug from Chicago, who
was recently exhibiting his ability to withstand assault
and battery in the Australian ring, being carried howling
to a lunatic asylum in Perth. From the first it was ob-
vious that there existed a kink in Grimm’s mental make-
up, and I hinted that the game was doing itself black
discredit in putting a crank between the ropes merely to
discover if bigger, stronger, and saner men could possibly
belt him into unconsciousness. It would have been more
humane and as good sport to have hired Joseph to be
kicked out by a horse. Grimm’s is not an isolated case ;
I know of one other minor pug who is notoriously dotty,
and who is borne with in the ring only because of his
eccentricities.

“ He who to be an expert motorist aspires,
Must first learn all there is to know about his tyres.”
—Proverbs of the Barnett Glass Rubber Co*
Let us take the little cuts—the little cuts that spoil
the tyres. They are of ter} quite insignificant in appearance,
but if neglected they will destroy the tyre. They collect all
manner of tiny trifles and form them into a hard mass,
which soon bores right through the rind, divides the rubber
from the canvas, and ruins the whole show. So to avoid
this, at the.end of each run look for the little cuts. Clean
them out and fill them with mastic ; and the days of that
tyre shall be long in the land. But do it properly. Apply
benzine, prop the edges of the'-cut apart with a match,
and wait till the benzine has dried the surface of the cut.
Then smear the wound with the mastic. Allow.it to dry
(10 minutes), then finish off by completely filling up the
cut. In six hours the tyre will be ready to make another
start—although a longer spell will not hurt. These
things are done while the tyre is fully inflated. But of
course, to get the best results, use Barnett .Glass Rubber
Co.’s mastic.* 1

“ Skull ” : A private word from New York tells
of an injury to Jim Jeffries/sustained in a boxing bout
incidental to his show business. Jim took a clout on the
chin when dreaming of better things, and nearly bit his
tongue off. This is expected to put the meeting with
John Johnson still further into dim distance. In America
an injury to the tongue incapacitates a pugilist quicker
than anything else. For months Jeffries has been hurling
opprobious epithets at Johnson ; and he is now assumed
to be incapable of following his calling, as it were.
“ On these rude islands, among the simple
natives, I found something of the caste of Vere de
Vere,” says a Melbourne missionary. The
Bulletin would not be surprised if he meant
the half-caste of Vere de Vere.
AN AUSTRALIAN INDUSTRY.
For the past five years Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills have been manufactured in Australia, and
we believe they are the only purgative pills on the
market that are made in this country by the pro-
prietors. Most other pills are made by manu-
facturing chemists, and sold in bulk to the
speculative pill companies to be boxed and
labelled. We take no chance of this kind with
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pill, and we know that
the formula does not vary one-hundredth part of a
grain. We also know that the ingredients are up
to the standard, both in strength and quality.
The crude material is imported direct, being
ground by our own machines in our American
laboratory, where the greatest care is observed in
the selection of the roots and gums from which
this crude, material is made. We have both in
Sydney and Wellington (New Zealand) up-to-date
plants for the manufacture of these pills, and
should any of our friends be interested, we will be
only too pleased to show them the process of
manufacture.
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are a perfect
blood purifier, and a safe and reliable remedy for
biliousness, constipation, indigestion, headaches,
sallow complexion, liver and kidney troubles,
piles, pimples, boils, and for female ailments.

'SA-VO-LA 5 in a few words is best described
as “the Greatest Skin Healer of the Century.”
All medical experts declare the great skin salve, SA-YO-LA to be the most
reliable of skin doctors, and invaluable to all persons in every grade of life
wherever accidents may happen. It is a disinfectant, antidote to blood poison,
and a skin-healer combined. It prevents blood poison arising from sores,
wounds, cuts, abrasions, etc., by reducing heat and inflammation and leaving
the injured parts soft, sweet, and cool. Workers in factories, warehouses,
or on farms, cattle-sheds, sheep-stations, mining camps or shearing sheds,

Business Notice.
Messrs, william cooper and
NEPHEWS, proprietors of the famous
Cooper’s Sheep Dips, who were also almost the
sole proprietors of the Cooper Sheep Shearing
Machine Proprietary, Limited, have decided upon
winding up the Company, which was practically a
department of their business, and to carry it on
entirely on their own account under the name of
the COOPER ENGINEERING COMPANY.
The Cooper Shearing Machines and Fairbanks-
Morse Engines, for which the firm is also the
agent in Australia, have now been installed on a
large number of stations throughout the Common-
wealth, and the business, for the present, will be
carried on at the premises, 16 Young St., Sydney.

Doctors of Two Continents Could
Make No Impression on Torturing
Skin Disease —All Treatments
Failed for Nineteen Years,
THEN CUTICURA CURED
SWIFTLY AND EASILY
41 1 contracted my disease at school
in London, England, when I was four-
teen years old I am now thirty-
three. My father put me under the
. care of our family physician who diag-
nosed my disease as itch. But after
several months’ treatment without
receiving any benefit, I was advised
to go to a hospital. I attended the
Hospital, London. Then and on
two other occasions, without my get-
ting any benefit, they treated me for
eczema. My next attempt was trying
different ointments from doctors and
druggists. Then I tried patent medicines,
including , and .
“I passed examinations for govern-
ment situations but was finally re-
jected at the medical examinations.
After three attempts to enlist in the
British army I succeeded at last but was
discharged on account of my eczema.
The disease was confined to face, arms
and legs, my face being actually raw at
times. I came to America thirteen
years ago, but my face became worse,
if possible. When in New York City I
was advised to go to Hospital,
whence I was sent to the Hospital
in Brooklyn. I was put under the care of
Professor but this also added to the
number of unsuccessful attempts. I
returned home to England and tried the
Hospital, Liverpool, the
Hospital, London, also the world-famed
London Hospital, but without get-
ting cured. I came back to America
five years ago. I had overlooked the
Cuticura Remedies dozens of times,
but three months ago I decided to give
Cuticura a trial and I can safely say I
am cured of one of the most torturing
skin diseases a man ever had. Richard
Curley, New Canaan, Conn., U. S. A.,
Jan. 10, 1909.”
“I am familiar with Mr. Curlev’s
case and his cure and believe his state-
ment to be true. John F. Burke, New
Canaan, Conn.”

The Inky Way.
Adelaide Critic, which struggles to earn its sixpence
chiefly by chronicling- the “very pretty wedding-” and
the pink chiffon worn by Mrs. H.vphen-Jones, has had
four editors within twelve months-—surely a record for
a fairly flourishing paper. The first was Conrad Eitel,
formerly (and since) of the Advertiser ; he took over
the show on the death of Alfred McKain, who founded
the paper twelve years ago. Then Holtze, of the Garden
and Field, tried to run the odd pair in double harness,
hut soon gave up the attempt. Next was Moore-
Robinson, a free-lance who also tried to bring off a
sporting double ; his second string was a financial paper
with the hopeless title of Money, Limited. The last is
Illingworth, who is rather stronger on the balance-sheet
side of the question than some of his predecessors. But
under all of them the Critic serenely goes on recording
the things that don’t matter for the benefit of the people
who don’t signify.
* * * *
. -fiho increasing rigor with which Australian editors
insist that literary matter must be cheerful is perhaps an
idea derived from Turkey, the land of outrage and assas-
sination. A Press regulation drawn up by the last Minister
of Instruction there threatened ruin to any newspaper
that disseminated distressing information, “as it tended
to make people sick.” Officials, if named, were always
to be referred to as enjoying g*ood health and happiness.
‘ If the harvest is good, say so,” ordered one clause ; “ but
if poor, avoid hinting at the fact in anyway.” Floods
and droughts are hard to bear, but the Minister
pointed out that published accounts of them make for
general gloom, and general gloom isn’t allowed.
As for fiction of a sorrowful character, the regulation
implied that the punishment for such a monstrous offence
was Death —either plain decease or burial with trimmings.
* * * *
“ Oudeis ” : The Comet is the title of London’s newest
penny paper. Bernard Espinasse, late of Australia, sees
that it lifts its fiery tail once a week, in an attempt to be
“ shorter, brighter, and better” than anything else of the
kind. Like M.A.P., and one or two more of the same
kind, it issues from the office of Pearson’s Ltd.
* * * *
When John Winthrop Hackett, formerly lecturer on
dry bones, sawdust, or something of that sort at Mel-
bourne University, put up for Sandridge against Dr.
John Madden and finished a bad second, he decided to go
West. On arriving in Perth he made the acquaintance of
Charles Harper, owner (or nearly so) of the West Austra-
lian. They struck up a partnership—Harper finding the
money, Hackett the brains or a colorable imitation. For a
long time the paper had asevere struggle, but the Coolgar-
die rush put the owners on their feet, and now profits are
anything between £lO,OOO and £15,000 yearlv. Hackett
went to Ireland—his native place—some years ago, and
was given an ad eundem degree—D.C.L. He was so
proud of this distinction that he had a brass plate affixed
to his office door, “Dr. Hackett,” and should any re-
porter fall to give him that title when writing up his
copy he had better look out for another billet.
Sometimes one is tempted to wonder if there are really
any short-story writers in England, or if the whole supply
of brief fiction is dishonestly acquired. As a stealer of
tales, verse and pictures a certain class of English paper
is the earth’s record. The ordinary thief borrows the
tale, or verse or picture, and acknowledges it to the paper
it was stolen from ; also, he gives the writer or artist
credit for his work. The English thief-paper, of the Ally
Sloper or Bits, or Snips or Horse Items variety, makes no
acknowledgments. It doesn’t mention that the tale or
picture was lifted from any other publication. It omits
the writer’s name from the literary matter, and it care-
fully cuts the artist’s name off the picture. Then it sets
its cheap hired lout to work to “localise” things, with
remarkable results. For instance, a London paper once
took hold of one Of Henry Lawson’s far-western
tales, and made the events occur on Holborn
Hill, London ! And Ally Sloper, of April 3, annexed
Alp Brascii’s tale, “ A Yellow Terror,” out of last
Christmas Bulletin, and shifted the scene from Mel-
bourne to anywhere in England. The central figure of
the story was a Chinaman, and Chinamen are scarce in
England, but if the central character had been an Aus-
tralian aboriginal with a boomerang, that would have
made very little difference to Sloper.. He would have
sloped away with the proceeds of his steal just the same.
* * * *
The cry of the overworked journalist. “Dagonet”
Sims describes in London Referee how, happening to be
at the window of his club one Saturday afternoon, when a
procession passed by, he was greeted with a veil of
“ Loafer 1 ” The banner of the processionists proclaimed
them to he “ Yiddish-speaking Anarchists.” And “my
average of hard manual labor,” says Sims plaintively, “is
from 14 to 16 hours a day ; and I have only had three
days’ holiday in four years.”
* * * *
“P.S.” writes: Victorian Labor-Leader Prendergast
showed me recently an interesting relic of Victor Daley.
It is the original of “ My name is Labor, but they call me
Christ.” This was written on the hack of two baker’s
bills. Daley was very hard up at the time, and, chancing
in the Tocsin office, he was pressed to write verses.
Those were struggling days both for the Tocsin and
for Daley. After he had finished the lines, an
exploration of the till resulted in the discovery
of 2s. • 6d. At this period, Dalev lived in a cot-
tage in Bog Terrace, Collingwood Flat. His furniture
consisted almost solely of packing cases. A friend visited
him one Sunday and found him making a railing screen
for his front door. “I’m trying to keep the darned
fowls out of my drawing-room," he explained.
THE TURNIP PRESS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MONSTER MUSHROOM.
Circumference of 22 Inches.
Egerton, Saturday.—The largest mushroom known in
this district has been picked up at Mount Doran. When
measured, it was found to be over 22in. in circumference.
—Melbourne Argus.
The little wars go whirling' on—the big wars threaten,
yet ;
The King to Marienbad has gone, by rheumatiz beset;
The marvels of the passing show are one-day wonders all;
But, though the Chief be rushed —although celestial
ramparts fall,
Big type will he,
Undoubtedly, \
Not fail to use
For Turnip news.
When fagged and worn from gabbling for the aged Tory
cause,
Though Labor does not cease to score, the editor will
pause
From pestling blends of deadly myth to blast the
Socialist,
And scare-head Turnips boldly with a firm yet gentle fist.
When Labor shoves
Too far, he loves
To drown his blues
In Turnip news.
The splendid phrase, the massive word condemning all
things new—
The Tories heed them not whan the vast Turnip heaves in
view.
They read the news of Slowgress, and they certainly
approve
Of what they have at egg-time scanned—how Turnip
matters move 1
When Tories eat
Their breakfast-meat,
This does amuse
Them—Turnip news I
Although Australia’s Parliament be loud with party strife,
Though tecs be hot upon the scent when evil deeds
are rife,
The premier place is not possessed by Politics nor Crime ;
For news of solid interest—why, Turnips, every time !
The Chief does not
Approve of rot—
His faith he glues
To Turnip news !
VICTORIA.
