THE BIG BLOCK,
BRISBANE
IN BANANA LAND
North Queensland —where the white man
can’t live—has got another industry. During the
reign of the crowd that wanted to get rich by
using cheap colored labor, the North was a plane
where only sugarcane, cocoanuts, bananas and
crocodiles could grow. Now Atherton, back from
Cairns, has got its butter-factory, and the chief
dairies are getting in milking-machines. Not ten
years ago, if a man had said that butter could be
made in the North he would have been howled at
as a mere clumsy liar ; to-day the industry is well
on its feet, and is being run on such up-to-date
lines that within the next 10 years Cairns will,
almost for a certainty, rank among the biggest
butter-shipping ports in Australia, with a first
call on the grease-trade with Asia.
The oldest officer in the Queensland Rail-
way Department, Mr. H. Horniblow, completed
45 years’ service on June 28. When he arrived
in Queensland from the Cold Country, in 1864, to
be foreman in charge of tools, no railways were
open for traffic, and he helped to build the first
four locomotives, which were employed in ballast-
ing the section between Ipswich and Grand-
chester. This was opened eight months after his
arrival, and was the pioneer railway of Banana-
land. He also supervised the erection of the first
machinery in the Ipswich workshops, and most of
the work since done there has been under his eye
in the capacity of locomotive engineer.
“ Itan ” : Senator McColl has come back
to talk about the priceless boon which prickly
pear is in America. McColl’s yarn is that on
some of the ranches the pear is treated to a gaso-
line flare, which scorches off the prickles, where-
upon the cattle chase the operator until he burns
off more prickles from more pear. It was after
it had heard the same yarn some years ago
that Queensland tried pear-feeding on scientific
lines at Gatton Agricultural College, and proved
conclusively that if stock got nothing else
they would die in a few weeks. Still, on
the strength of McColl’s new “discovery,”
there is an idea in some quarters that Burbank’s
“spineless” pear should be planted broadcast.
The only trouble about this wonderful creation is
A FRUIT ITEM.
REV. CODLIN: “ Let us pray !”
YOUTHFUL ORCHARDIST ( first time in church): “ What's he goin’ ter spray , dad?”
that, when put out to give an account of itself on
the American ranches, it quickly degenerated to
the common whiskered original. Four years ago
Botanist Bailey, of Brisbane, after a lifetime
spent in trying these newly-developed freaks,
predicted this failure, and warned Queensland
against monkeying with it; so McColl has arrived
too late with his wonderful yarn.
“ Flinderino ” : Two very remarkable
specimens of marine life are the johnnie-jumper
and the rifle-fish, both of which are plentiful along
the North Queensland coast. The johnnie-jumper,
or mud-skipper (I don’t know its scientific name),
is a thing with lungs, and is as much at home on
land as in the briny. Jack jumper, when fully
grown, is about a foot in length. With the aid of
two side-flippers it works its passage about the
mud along the banks of the creeks and rivers,
seeking flies and other dainties. The rifle-fish
also feeds on insects; but, not possessing lungs,
it is unable to leave the water as does the mud-
skipper. It shoots its victims. The shooting
fish swims leisurely along the stream an
inch or two below the surface and close
to the bank. It is ever on the watch for a fat fly
or a juicy mosquito. When one of these items of
food comes within reach the rifle-fish fires a small
ball of water. The liquid shot seldom misses its
mark, and the insect falls into the stream, and is
straightway gathered in by the shooter. And
considering the smallness of the fish—it is only
from 3in. to lOin. in length—it is wonderful what
a long distance it can shoot, often scoring its
dinner at 3ft. and more. This fish is known in
some of the northern rivers as the spotted bream.
Owing to its habit of swimming so close to the
surface, the rifle-fish falls an easy prey to the fish
hawk and other sea-fowl, which are also good
shots.
Lost.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHEN I was a child I thought of a song
that some day I should write ;
’Twould be read by the gleam of a
gibbous moon, or by star-lantern
light.
’Twas to be the song of a bushman’s life, of cattle
upon the move,
Of the traveling sheep in the dust-white lane —
the lilt of a leafy love ;
A tale of the camp fire by the track, the stock-
horse on the rise,
Of the wallaby in the underbrush, and the way
the goshawk flies.
Of the orchid clustering on the stump, the bloom
on the prickly pear,
Of the thudding of the brumby’s feet as he scales
the mountain stair,
Of the dingo pup by the hollow log, the plover
upon the plain,
Of the water-hen by the shallow creek, and the
lashing summer rain,
Of the cloud-white ibis along the pool, where
chaliced lilies lie,
The splash of pink on the Floors of God, when
day has left the sky.
Oh ! I had a song for the men to hear who know
their country’s heart,
Who lift their eyes to a roof of blue where gold
dawn-banners start.
Oh, I had a song of the ready hand, the quick,
courageous brain,
Of the trail of buds from the dew-dark bough, the
loosened bridle rein;
Of the stockwhip bird in the cool, still green, the
doves with a dreamy croon.
The hurry of crowded morning hours, the linger-
ing afternoon. . . .
For my brain was full of the hush of stars, the
twitter of the day.
When birds swing up in the silky oaks and
branches creak and sway ;
Oh, I had a song for all men to hear—played on a
pipe of reeds,
And my pen was dipped in the oozing sap where
the stricken bloodwood bleeds.
I wonder where it has gone to-day—the song I
thought to sing ?
Did some spirit harry its web away, a floating,
broken thing?
And though it was never a rhyme of price, but
only a simple song,
How I miss it out of my heart to-day where it lay
hid so long !
I went to the park this morning, where the tulip
tree pricks red,
And I caught the sound of my song’s frail wing
far drifting overhead.
And I strained my arms to the tulip bloom, like
flame against the sky,
But only a shadow touched my cheek, and the
song went voiceless by.
I sought it deep in the river tides, under the bridge
they sang,
And I heard no sound but the rush of cars, where
great steel girders rang,
At night, while the brain of the city slept, and
houses shut their eyes,
I looked for my song in the empty street and in
the smoke-grey skies.
But it did not lurk in the silent lane, nor under
the murky eaves.
My song was a-tune to the clean grass blades, or
ripple of summer leaves,
And this morn, in the Gardens' sunlit slopes,
where circled pinangs throng,
Came a sudden clashing of pixie bells—a murmur
of my song !
But I never shall find it all again—when I take
up my pen
I frame but words of the chaffering world and the
devious lives of men ;
Of the silken ways and scented air, the lights on
a polished floor;
Of gold and tinsel where hearts should be—trinkets
arid nothing more.
I went forth at noon with a leaping hope, where
blossom ways stretch fair,
To show all my grief to your limpid eyes—to find
my music there;
For over your head was an arch of green, under
your feet were flowers,
And all about you was lilac-gloom from the jaca-
randa showers.
And I searched your soul with my yearning gaze ;
I held you close and long ;
And I read your heart in your tender eyes—but I
did not find my song!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, .July 10, 1900.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
Already there is a flutter in the air. The
Dudley menage will arrive at the vice-regal shanty
on the 15th, and rumors of a Government House
ball are in the air. A garden party is likely to
follow, and Mrs. Speaker Bell is said to be con-
templating a big dance on account of her pretty
and lately-fluffed daughter. After that come the
political conference and chaos.
Already, too, the Lady of Dudley is being sought
by charity committees, and she has promised that
her pale-clothed figure will grace the chair at the
annual meeting at the Woman’s Hospital. The
committee of the kindergarten and crbche is also,
I hear, on her tracks. Even as the Siberian
escapee is supposed to listen to the wolf in the
distance, so does the fleeing modern Governoress
hear the baying of the charitable committee afar
off, and though she may run at her utmost speed,
with her hair flying in the wind, it overtakes her
in the end.
The death of A. W. Compigne, last survivor
but one of the original Queensland Legislature,
happened on Sunday, the morning of the glorious
4th. A.W.C. was a kindly soul, whose worst
friend was himself, for he had no idea of business.
He held a commission in the first infantry bri-
gade in N.S.W., and for that matter held it to
the day of his death, for, by some curious over-
sight, he was never formally retired. He saw
service as a police magistrate for 22 years,
at Gayndah, Mount Perry, Blackall, Beenleigh,
and Southport, and had reached the ripe age of
91 years and seven months when the call came.
His chief interests lay in yachting and shooting.
He was a fine shot, and would neglect any work
he had on hand for the sake of tackling a wild
duck at long range. Even the wildest and most
infuriated duck usually became his prey.
The Chapman-Alexanders, of hymn-yelling,
Bible-banging fame, are in our midst, and a sec-
tion of otherwise sensible citizens is indulging in
an orgy of penitence. At a recent mass meeting
for men. Archpriest Chapman was paralysed to
find, in response to his demand that social sinners
should rise, the whole of his congregation on its feet.
It was probably the most unanimous upheaval in
the Chapzander experience.
However, even these lugubrious gatherings have
their humorous moments, one being when Chap-
man gravely announced to some 3000 aged, halt
and blind folk on Thursday that he knew all about
babies, having had three himself. To which an
elderly dame was heard to murmur “Justice at
last.”
This saving of souls is, no doubt, a very laudable
occupation, but it seems to entail a considerable
deal of tripping round the world, lodging at the
most expensive hotels, and being generally fed
and feted between whiles. But then these
Christians, liberally endowed with the earth’s
goods, are so self-sacrificing.
The ladies’ gallery at the local talking-shop has
been thronged throughout the week, for storms
are rapidly brewing. Mistress Ividston sat out
one whole afternoon as a moral support to her own
overlord, and Mrs. Joey Bell is yet a faithful
watcher, despite the fact that an occasional
“ Haw. Order ! Order ! ! The hon. gentleman
must address the chair ! ” is all that is left to Joey
of his silver oratory.
Heavy is the hand of Providence upon
the Ministry. One day last week no less
than three Parliamentary rooms might have
been mistaken for private-hospital wards.
’Flu’ and other vague complaints had laid
the scanty Ministerial majority low, and with
their opponents mostly in good health, the whips
(there are three of them these rocky times)
are taking no chances. So the victims were
gathered in from the various private hospitals to
which they had fled in their anguish, and were
put to bed within the precincts of the House, so
that they might be hauled even from the bunk of
death if necessary to save the situation. Politics
are strenuous these days.
Lieutenant-Colonel McLaughlan, who is to wed
bonnie Winnie Hickson the 20tb, descended upon
this city per mail train on Thursday. The in-
tended bridegroom is a strapping defender, not-
withstanding that the hand of Time has been laid
upon his wool. How early these military heroes
go grey.
The onslaught and retirement of the “Jack and
Jill” co. has left the hearts of many local males
empty. I hear that the telephone lines between
Toowoomba and here were blocked for hours dur-
ing the short sojourn of the Jills in the Highland
city. One big mercantile potentate is said to
have Bought up his own junior clerk’s “ turn ” on
the wire for a guinea. Which was fairly decent;
some men would have taken it by rapine and the
strong hand.
Yours affectionately,
MARIO.
Bananaland Lands Department has been
working overtime lately, and will shortly have
ready for settlement 30,000 acres at Atherton,
38,000 at Bowen, 30,000 at Bundaberg, 22,000 at
Gayndah, 7000 at Ingham, 23,000 at Inglewood,
118,000 at Nanango, 40,000 at Jimbour, (50,000 at
Proston and Cumbanga, 37,000 at Roma, and
45,000 at Wallam and Albany; and there ought
to be enough in that lot to satisfy the appetite of
a few hundred hungry farmers.
William Henry Warman, an 80-years-old
compositor in the Brisbane Government Printing
Office, is said to be the patriarch of his craft in
Australia. He was born in Cumberland-street, Syd-
ney, but was apprenticed in Melbourne to George
Cavanougb, and subsequently worked in the
Patriot office. He moved to response
to an advt. in the Argus for 20 comps. It
was inserted by Robert Nichols, who had
some notion of starting a daily paper.
The project, however, fell through, but War-
man got a job on Parkes’ precarious Empire.
In that office he was implicated in the so-called
Parkes Conspiracy Strike, and got a week’s gaol
for his share in it. When he came out he worked
on the S. M. Herald, and later on the Melbourne
Morning Herald. In 1861 he went to Brisbane,
and snatched type for a while on the Guardian,
then owned by Belbridgeand Fairfax. Belbridge
was appointed first Queensland Government
Printer, and Warman joined him, and off and on
has been there ever since. He has worked under
every Government Printer in Queensland, and
still has no use for spectacles.
“ Twig ” : Out in Central Queensland the
rabbit has not yet found his way, but when he
does he will be there in millions—that is, if the
pig, who is there before him, allows it. As yet it
is only on some stations that the pig is alarmingly
numerous. In dry times the pigs keep well to the
water-courses, and a few shots can be got in before
they all disappear. In case of a litter, we shoot
the old pig, and knock as many of the young on
the head as possible, but if they are very young,
and get away, they soon get weak and fall
victims to some of their hungry relations. It
was just after a big slaughter that our
old collie Lubra left her four pups to
take a run in the scrub. We saw her coming
home. She would walk a few yards and stop and
look back, and when she came closer we found
that she had adopted a motherless sucking pig.
The carpenter had had a week off in town, and
arrived home some days after the pig was installed
in Lubra’s kennel. He was evidently recovering,
but he looked particularly bad when he went to the
storekeeper next morning. “I’ve done it this
time,” he said ; “I wish you would keep an eye
on me for a day or so, and not mention it to any-
one. I’m willing to pay you for your trouble.
Look there ! I know as well as you that it’s a
pup, but I could swear it was a pig.’’ The mean
wretch of a storekeeper did keep an eye on him
for two days at Is. per day. And then he crowned
his infamy by killing and eating the adopted pig,
for which the carpenter, now fully recovered,
belted him severely.


A SYMBOL OF PROGRESS.
The Queensland State Railways supply the want of
river and water communication with the in-
' terior, just as artesian ivater corrects the sur-
' face aridity of the Western areas, and makes
the West of Queensland a garden.
The Communal railways of Queensland are a mili-
tary necessity for the Commonwealth.
The principle of national ownership of the Queens-
land railways was at one period in doubt.
It is now settled finally, so that a bold
policy of railway extension is in the
programme of every Government.
The Queensland State railivays are a visible symbol
of the innate energy of the Queenslander, and
of his determination to open up the country.
Through these lines the wealth and trade
of the great State pulsate as the life
blood through the veins and arteries of
the human body.
Soon the whole State will be linked up, and the lines
will extend from sea to sea.
This will develop internal trade and ex-
change of the wonderfully varied pro-
ducts of the State, while it makes pos-
« sible a huge foreign export trade.
Queensland To-day: the Queen State
of the Commonwealth.
No. XXVIII.
THE RAILWAY SYSTEM.—IV.
By J. D. FITZGERALD.
The gauge question for the Queensland railways
was not one to be decided by rule of thumb. It
had been eagerly debated both in England and in
the Southern Colonies (as they were then). It
had been the subject of a despatch from the late
W. E. Gladstone when he was Colonial Secretary
of England. In that despatch the Colonies were
ROMA STREET RAILWAY STATION , BRISBANE.
Photo, by C. E. S. Fryer.
advised to adopt the 4ft. gauge. An Act
was passed in N.S.W., which adopted the sft. 3in.
gauge, and that gauge was made compulsory upon
all railway construction in the mother State.
This determination was communicated to the
colonies -of Victoria and South Australia, and
they both adopted that broad gauge. But in the
meantime N.S.W. changed its own mind, and
began to construct upon the 4ft. B|in. gauge.
Victoria and South Australia continued construct-
ing upon the broad gauge principle, though the
latter State departed from it in regard to
some of its later lines. Whether the mistake
made generally .in not having uniformity,
THE RAILWAY ON THE BLACK ALL RANGES.
A zone of scenic bexuty.
Photo, by C. E. S. Fryer.
especially in view of military exigencies,
will be corrected by adapting the main lines of
connection to the medium or N.S.W. gauge, may
be left to the future. This mode of securing uni-
fortuity would probably be far more costly than
the reduction of all gauges to the Queensland
3ft. 6in., because immense sums would have to be
spent in reconstructing or altering tunnels, cut-
tings and bridges on the narrow gauge railways,
which have now a greater mileage than either of
the other gauges.
The gauge having been decided upon, construc-
tion was begun. From the moment of the turning of
the first sod of the Queensland State railways a
new path of progress was opened out for the State
—a path which has been steadily trodden, through
many vicissitudes, until it has debouched into the
splendid road with the magnificent vista of to-day.
These were vicissitudes, and serious ones. One
early mistake
cost a high
price; but the
men in com-
mand had their
m i stakes to
make and their
experience to
gain; and the
generations and
Governments of
to-day are the
gainers. The
line was begun
at Ipswich in
February, 1804,
and in a little
over a year the
first section to
Grandchester,
28 miles, was
opened. Th e
Main Range
had then to be
climbed, and
this was suc-
cessfully accom-
plished, and that
splendidly fer-
tile tableland,
the Darling
Downs, was
brought into
communication
with the sea-
port—and with
the world.
Shortly after
this a financial
crisis came upon
Queensland,
which stopped
railwa.y building for a while, as the Government
was unable to find the funds to continue. Advant-
age was taken of the necessitous condition of the
Government to attempt again to introduce the
“land-grant” system. But again the grit of the
pioneer statesmen was shown. They steadfastly
rejected the private enterprise proposal; and also
a second proposal to give them financial accom-
modation on condition that they should give up
their public works policy. Meanwhile, immigra-
tion proceeded apace ; but land settlement was
not properly provided for until 1868 ; and then
the State begun to go ahead. The line was ex-
tended from Toowoomba to Dal by, and from Too-
woomba to Warwick. In 1867 the Central line
was opened from Rockhampton to Westwood.
The Townsville line was begun in 1879, and
opened in 1880, while a line from Cooktown to
Laura was begun in 1884 and completed in 1887.
Again, in the years 1874 onwards, the struggle
of public ownership of railways as against the
land-grant system was renewed, and after a fierce
struggle, we may safely assume, finally settled.
We find associated with this final struggle one of
the big personalities of Queensland politics—Sir
Thomas Mcllwraith. Mcllwraith was certainly
one of the boldest and most masterful statesmen
that Australia has produced. He was a man of
big ideas, and often rash in his methods of carry-
ing them out. His period of power was marked
by some of the most sensational episodes that
have occurred in the history of Australia. He was
on several occasions in conflict with the Imperial
authorities; and, whether right or wrong,
usually got his own way. In the one case in
which he did not succeed in getting his own
way, an Imperial blunder resulted, which lost
a rich island to Britain, and gave a foothold
to neighbors (now happily in friendly relations
with us), whose presence near our coasts may
some day cause us embarrassment. Whether right
or wrong in his policy, Mcllwraith is one of our
great political figures.
Mcllwraith, during his tenure of the portfolio
of Public Works, revived the land-grant railway

Photo, by C. E. S. Fryer
scheme with a
gigantic project
fora transconti-
nental railway.
He had received
a proposal from
a syndicate of
Melbourne capi-
talists who, in
consideration of
grants of land
along the line,
proposed to
build a railway
from Roma, in
the Southern
District, right
through Queens-
land to the
shores of the
Gulf of Carpen-
taria. Mr. Mac-
alister was Mc-
llwraith’s poli-
tical chief, and
upon inquiry,
and behind the
back of his col-
league, the chief
publicly an-
nounced that he
had rejected the
project. Mcll-
wraith resigned
and waited. In
1870 he came
into power as
Premier, and
without delay
passed through
Parliament a
bold measure,
which ran directly counter to public opinion,
which was then slowly but surely crystallis-
ing upon the subject of State ownership of
railways. Mcllwraith’s measure was called
“An Act to Provide for and Encourage the Con-
struction of Railways by Private Enterprise.”
It provided for land grants in alternate blocks
to be given to individual capitalists or syndi-
cates that would come forward and construct rail-
ways. There was a power reserved to the Govern-
RAILWAY STATION YARD, CAIRNS. The star tiny point of the mountain railways,
which run through lovely scenery.
Photo, by C. E. S. Fryer.
ment to purchase each railway after it had been in
private use for five years ; or, in the alternative,
the private syndicate could run it for 21 years,
and at the end of that time it automatically
became the property of the Government.
The working out of this scheme was watched
with intense interest in the other States. Cer-
tainly the great Northern State has been the cyno-
sure of all eyes on many occasions ; and in the
working out of its problems it has had a large
audience. Being a self-governing State, no
interference was possible by the people of the
other States, except that of public discussion
in the press. In this crisis. The Bulletin
proved itself a power. By its attacks
upon the new scheme, and by its powerful
SPRING BLUFF.—A BUSH STATION ON THE QUEENSLAND RAILWAYS.
The focus of the bushmen’s communication with the world’s markets.
Photo. by C. E. S. Fryer.
cartoons—which embodied some of Hopkins’
most pungent satire—the continuance of the
scheme was rendered impossible. The mind
of the Queensland elector was aroused. The
Opposition leader, Sir Samuel Griffith, made the
question a test, and the country declared for him,
and in favor of communal enterprise in railway
building. The transcontinental railway project
was killed, the “ land sharks,” as the railway
syndicates were termed, defeated ; and Sir Samuel
Griffith came in with a majority and a
proposition to borrow £10,000,000 to carry
out a bold State railway construction
scheme. There was a later period at which,
on the coalition of Mcllwraith and Griffith,
these leaders asked Parliament for and
were granted power to negotiate with syndicates
for private rail way construction ; but, doubtless,
speculators knew better than politicians how
strong a feeling existed against the abroga-
tion of the principle of public ownership,
and they wisely refrained from risking the fruit-
less toil which General Fielding expended on
the transcontinental railway scheme of the Mcll-
wraith regime.
Since that time, Federation, though it has not
taken the railways over from the States, has given
the people in the other States a voice in the affairs
of their neighbors. Reinforced by the strong
opinion which exists in the other States, the feel-
ing for communal ownership of railways in
Queensland will be fanned into higher flame. It
is not merely in regard to railways that this
feeling exists. There is an objection to the ex-
ploitation in any way of the great opportunities
afforded in the Australian waste spaces by large
aggregations of private capital. As an instance,
though the necessity of peopling and developing
the Northern Territory—Queensland’s neighbor on
the north-west—is strongly felt throughout the
Commonwealth, as a measure of self-preservation,
a strong resentment is always expressed at any
colonising scheme which is associated with large
grants of the public lands to private syndicates.
It may be confidently said, that the two greatest
problems of public policy yet decided in Australia
were associated with the politics of Queensland.
The prospective immigrant may well rejoice that
the White Australia policy will bring to Queens-
land thousands of industrious white yeomen,
working their own small estates, instead of a few
planters and overseers, or perhaps absentee capi-
talists, employing factors on their huge estates,
with a horde of servile colored laborers. And he
may rejoice equally that State ownership of the
great railway service of Queensland means de-
velopment of the lines for the benefit of the
settler, and of the country generally, rather than
a private monopoly interested only in earning
dividends for the shareholders and fees for the
directors.
I have dwelt on the railway question at some
length because, in this great national service, the
magnificent energy of the young State is seen in
action. In these articles I have refrained from
making comparisons detrimental to the other
Australian States ; but in one respect it cannot be
helped. The distribution of the railway system
of Queensland with a view to the opening up of
the country, the encouragement of settlement,
production, and transport to the markets, is far
superior to that of any of the splendid systems of
the other States. Centralism has been carefully
avoided ; and, so far as access to markets is con-
cerned, nothing better could be devised than the
plan in operation in the northern State. In time,
too, some of these lines will become transcon-
tinental. The line now under construction from
Cairns to Georgetown, when completed, can
easily be extended to meet the line running from
Normanton, in the Gulf, to the Croydon gold-
fields. The Burketown to Camooweal project will
in time be extended to Cloncurry, and, in a
measurable distance of time, the “grey Gulf
waters ” will be connected by rail with the
eastern coast. Above all, the building by
the Commonwealth Government of the trans-
continental railway to Port Darwin will
prove a substantial benefit to Queensland, and
will open up millions of acres of magnificent coun-
try in the western areas of that State. The
Queensland railway system is a magnificent
object-lesson of the energy of the people ; it is a
living token of the confidence which they have in
the future of their State. It is an instrument of
development and of progress which must encourage
any person in Europe who is looking abroad for a
suitable field for his labor or his capital.

ADVANCE. DEPARTMENT.
THIS Department offers most advantageous terms to settlers or intending settlers on the land, a few of
the important features being :
(a) Advances made from £5O to £2OOO.
( b ) Low AND FIXED rate of interest during
currency of loan.
(c) Money may be lent for any term up to
31 years.
( d ) Loan instalments (interest and principal)
are payable half-yearly.
(i e ) Borrower has the right to repay the loan
on any instalment date; interest to that
date only being charged.
The tenures upon which the Advance Department may lend are: — (l) Freeholds, (2) Certificated Con-
ditional Purchases (with or without associated Conditional Leases), (3) Uncertificated Conditional
Purchases (with or without assoc.ated Conditional Leases), (4) Homestead Selections (before or after
issue of the Homestead Crant), (5) Settlement Leases, (6) Settlement Purchases, (7) Conditional
Purchase Leases.

“The Song of the Cheap,” by G. It.
Sims, reprinted from London Referee:—
Wife to Husband.
“I’ve saved a penny on the toy
I bought for baby Anne,
And fourpenoe on the pair of boots
I bought for little Dan.
I know that England’s task is hard
Her Unemployed to keep,
But foreign goods are cheap, my love,
But foreign goods are cheap.
“ Our home was ‘ made in Germany,’
One sees it at a glance ;
Our food comes from Ameria,
Our ornaments from France.
I know that to their foodless homes
Our starving workers creep,
But foreign goods are cheap, my love,
But foreign goods are cheap.
“ The shops are full of all the things
That come from foreign lands,
It’s hard to find an article
That’s made by English hands.
Of course it’s very sad, but then
The benefit we reap
By buying foreign goods, my love,
For foreign goods are cheap.”
Husband to Wife.
“The shops are full of foreign goods
While English workers starve,
Our pasture lands are desolate
While foreign beef we carve ;
The works where British goods are made
Are closing fast, I hear ;
So foreign goods are dear, my love,
So foreign goods are dear.
“The children of the Unemployed
Upon the rates are thrown,
And we must feed them every day
As well as feed our own ;
Their fathers cannot find them food
If out of work, it’s clear ;
So foreign good* are dear, my love,
So foreign goods are dear.
“The trade by which the English lived
Has gone across tne sea,
The State must kill the little left
To get its £ s. d.
A crushing rate we pay to help
The idle workers here ;
So foreign goods are dear, my love,
So foreign goods are dear.
“ Each foreign thing you purchase robs
A Briton of his bread ;
And we must pay in rate and tax
That still he may be fed.
Our toilers to the workhouse go,
Their end a pauper’s bier ;
So foreign goods are dear, my love,
So foreign goods are dear.
“ For every penny that you save
The State must pay a pound,
Because the wage our workers lose
By State aid must be found ;
While work our toilers seek in vain,
And starving women weep,
No foreign goods are cheap, my love,
No foreign goods are cheap.
“ I Itnow you have a frugal mind,
My best of gentle wives,
But are the paltry pence you save
Worth English homes and lives?
When Labor frets till angry threats
To lips that famish leap,
No foreign goods are cheap, my love,
No foreign goods are cheap.”
Geo. R. Sims.


MICK SIMMONS
Haymarket, Sydney, N.iS.W.

Doctors Enthusiastic Over New Discovery.
DR. HARTMANN SOLVES NEURASTHENIC MYSTERY.
Explains the Brilliant Success of the New Treatment for Nerve Wrecks.
How Nervous Disorders are Cured by Brain Feeding.
“Neurasthenia has been the Central Africa of medicine
—an unexplored territory into which few men enter.”
These words are eloquent testimony to the great mystery
that surrounds the most common of human ailments.
VVe are able, however, to chronicle the fact that at
least one scientist has penetrated to the heart of that
mystery, and has brought us back a warning and —health.
There can be no more fascinating study than the study
of that great mysterious force the ordinary worker is
content to call “ the nervous system.” The hidden force
that guides our every action, that controls our hopes,
that decides our ambitions and aspirations—it is this
power that we desire chained down and shielded ; then
we “ know ” our battle is over.
When it is remembered that the percentage of illness
grouped under “nervous weakness” or neurasthenia, is
higher than any other class of ailment, it will be realised
how serious a matter is dealt with here, and this will lend
an added greatness to Dr. Hartmann’s brilliant discovery.
The cure for neurasthenia, says Dr. Hartmann, is food
—brain food,
In these days of high-pressure living, with fierce social
and commercial competition, there is a strain put upon
the nervous system which not one man in a hundred can
healthily withstand.
The effect of the strain is shown either in extreme
irritability, lack of concentration, irresponsible thought,
and finally insanity. The story of the neurasthenic is,
perhaps, best told in the following chart, which shows
clearly the descent and how swiftly we are taken to the
end :
Sensitiveness
Restlessness j Irritability
Melancholia | Nervousness | Pessimism
Hysteria I Fearfulness I Inaptitude for I Dizziness
| | Steady Work |
Brain Fag | Insomnia
Mental Derangement
Suicidal Tendency
Somewhere in that chart the trouble of nine out of
every ten men and women is placed. It is the neurasthenic
chart, and once a person can recognise his symptoms
thereon, he should know that -within him is the worst of
all diseases, a disease that unchecked can but lead to one
end —total collapse.
Dr. Hartmann’s cure for the neurasthenic is brain-feed-
ing. That this treatment, under Dr. Hartmann’s
method, is effective is proved by the splendid testimony
voluntarily affirmed by medical men in the highest
position.
This method of treatment, that Dr. Hartmann has
proved to be truly successful, may be thus detailed.
Food, when taken into the body, is used in various
ways for general nourishment. Certain foods are used for
fat production, others for the manufacture of bone ; and,
yet again, other parts of food are used up in the produc-
tion of brain tissue. Now, since all nervous affections
arise in the brain, and are caused by malnutrition— i.e. ,
its starvation—the cure for neurasthenia lies in the ques-
tion of supplying- the nerve centre with proper food to
allow due strengthening-.
Now, in all foods—in some more than others—there are
brain-forming materials. They are scientifically known
as Mylene or Lecithine. Under natural conditions the
best food contains but a trace of this wonderful substance,
and, therefore, it is impossible in the ordinary way to
absorb into the system a sufficient quantity to effect an
appreciable improvement in a neurasthenic case.
“ It is, in brief, a question of administering a sufficient
supply of nerve-feeding elements to balance the rapid
waste of nerve tissue, caused by the unnatural strain of
present day life, by a rapid repair.”
The result of Dr. Hartmann’s brilliant experiments has
been to prepare a nerve food in which the brain-forming
element is sufficiently strong to at once have a beneficial
effect.
To his new discovery Dr. Hartmann has given the title
“ Antineurasthin,” and already most remarkable cures
have been effected by its administration.
Antineurasthin is a preparation which contains all the
nerve-building materials “Lecithine,” in a highly con-
centrated form ; it is easily assimilated, and the benefits
it confers are lasting. It has, therefore, nothing in com-
mon with medicines, drugs, or other stimulants—it is
the natural nerve food which nourishes the nerve system
independently of the body. To everyone requiring a
genuine tonic—to the man whose nerves have become
unstrung through business worries, to the woman who
has become supersensitive or morbid—this simple remedy
will prove an inestimable benefit.
The following testimony will certainly interest those
who are nervous patients. It comes from one, of the
most brilliant physicians in Europe—Dr. Guiseppe Lap-
poni, Physician-in-Ordinary to his Holiness the Pope :
. “ Rome.
“ Dear Sir, —I made trial in my private practice of
Antineurasthin, and shall prescribe it henceforward, as it
is an admirable agent for toning and strengthening the
nervous system. I take the liberty of congratulating,
you on your discoverv.
(Signed) “Dr. GUISEPPE LAPPONI,
“ Physician-in-Ordinary to his Holiness Pope Pius X.”
This is praise indeed, and the most satisfactory feature
of Dr. Hartmann’s success is that the congratulation of.
his medical confreres is overwhelmingly supported by the
testimony of actual users of Antineurasthin.
The fact cannot be too strongly impressed upon the
minds of readers that nervous disorder, no matter how-
slight, must not be neglected or played with. The small
worry of to-day becomes the morbid melancholia or break-
down of to-morrow. It is useless to endeavor to seeli
temporary stimulation in chemical or so-called tonics,
which can have but a serious after effect—a Loss or
Nervous Force.
Nervous disorders must be cured, and if, on once
glancing at the chart given, you recognise your trouble,
we advise you to at once investigate the claims ,of
Antineurasthin.

W W HA Tis the Australian’s Fatherland ?
M/jfy' Is H England .?
r r Is it Ireland ?
Is it where the haggises bloom ?
Or where the distant Taffy waves his whiskers in
the breeze?
It is where the great Pacific beats with Titan
strokes terrific—
It is where the tall hills loom ;
And the Toilers and the Thinkers shape the
Nation’s destinies !
It is where the axes swinging psalms of hopeful
strength are singing—
Where the choir of rolling hammers chants of
mighty Days ahead.
It is Here, in our great Lone Land,
It is here within our own land
Where we raise old Freedom’s standard, calling
Peoples from the dead.
Where is the Australian’s Fatherland l
Back in Europe l
Or in Britain ?
Is it far beyond the sea ?
Not in Deutschland, France or Denmark, or the
Netherlands or Spain ;
It is Here where Toil is blessed, and the skies are
cirrus-tressed—
It is here where men are free,
And the gods that humans worship are not ghastly
gods of gain !
It is here where blazing forges fling their chal-
lenge to the gorges—
Where the mountain-heads are humbled and, are
withed with bolt and rail;
It is here, O sons Australian,
Where we face the countless alien—
It is Here, O Young Republic—hail, Australia !
Homeland, hail!
What is the Australian’s Fatherland ?
Is it Queensland ?
* South Australia ?
Is it Vici or New South Wales ?
Is it Groperland or Tassy—north or south or west
or east?
’Tis the Continent United, where the wrongs of
men are righted—
And where superstition fails,
And the free man fronts defiant unto diddering
king and priest !
Where the forthright word is spoken, where the
ancient lies are broken—
Hoary falsehood’s helots bowed to in the tortured
Long Ago;
Where the White Man’s Commonweal is,
There a Nation strong and leal is—
’Tis the Fatherland we strive for, hearts a-phant
and souls a-glow !
Who builds the Australian s Fatherland l
Do the paltry ?■
Do the Insects ?
Is it raised by little men ?
Wades and Moores, and Philps and Kidstons—,
puny creatures of the States ?
Nay, the Builders are the Toilers—they who feed
the engine-boilers,
They who work with brain and pen—
Those whose anvils clang exultant where the
, foundry palpitates 1
Not the idlers and the shirkers—hail the Army
Corps ,of Workers,,
Hail the blacksmith and the plumber—all who
toil with patient zeal;
Where the steelworks’ furnace brightens,
See the Builders —hail the Titans !
Hail the Bismarcks clad in basil who uplift the
Commonweal 1 , (

Do the Fatmeh

With their flapping cargo-lists—
Those who drag the foreign shoddy from the lands
beyond the sea ?
Do the sainted thieves and sweaters, binding men
with bitter . , . \
Do the land-monopolists,
Those to whom the sheep ;is sacred far above
humanity ?
Nay, the helpers are the strivers—are the blue-,
clad engine-drivers,
They who turn the wheels of Industry and rush
j the looms of trade ;
\ Those whose steam-impacted pistons
Smite the miles of rail-linked distance— ■'
These are hauling White Australia up the Future’s
shining grade !
Whose is the Australian’s Fatherland t
j Is it Asia’s ?■
Is it Europe’s ?
Unto whom does it belong ?
It is Ours, and we are stewards for the White
Men yet to come;
It is ours to toil and graft for, turning wheel and
crank and shaft for—
It is ours to fill with song,
Till the armed and strong Republic beats its
world-defying drum !
Here the hearts beat true and eager—we shall ;
face the battle-leaguer,
We shall roll away all evil, cheering noble deeds
and right;
All the small mean things abhorring,
All the greater things adoring,
If our Fatherland be threatened, we shall dare to
keep it White !
Hail ye the Australian’s Fatherland!
’Tis the White Man s !
’ Tis the Toiler’s!

VICTORIA.
GRANT HERVEY.
Apiti.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A PITI slid from the bush, and crept to
the water’s edge. At his feet the Pacific
caressed the white sand of Mono Bay ;
overhead stars infinite diffused a subtle light. In
the marshy ground on the left of the bay a bittern
boomed loudly and persistently. Apiti was an
outlaw. That was what half a dozen daily papers
journals, shameless in their ultra-
respectability—had termed him. Being journals
with a stake in the country and reputations to
match, their readers believed in them implicitly.
It was a slack day when a wire from Gisborne
first introduced Apiti to their august notice, and
a shoal of callow youths with reputations to
acquire were turned loose to write up the phe-
nomenon. At the end of six months, Apiti, still
at large, had evolved from a bushranger into an
outlaw. Then the callow youths clamored in
united chorus for Danby.
Thomas Brookham Danby was plethoric. Also
he knew the private history of half the vagabonds
in the Dominion, and with them he was mostly on
friendly terms. Hence his success. But Danby
had limitations which none realised better than
himself, • As a city sleuth, a frequenter of frowsy,
disreputable urban districts and a demolisher of
two-up schools, he had no equal. This Danby
knew. Outside that environment he had had
little experience. The papers which had made
his reputation, and the people who accepted the
papers’ opinions as they never accepted the
Gospel, did not recognise this fact. To them
Danby was invincible —a combination of Sfherlock
Holmes and Buffalo Bill. Therefore let Danby
the Great go out into the wilds and capture the
desperado that the people might fall down and
Worship him some more. It was a great chance for
Danby, so the dailies said.
Apiti stood with the Pacific laving his bare
feet, and waited. As he waited he thought.
Mostly it was of Marama the Moon-child and
Marama’s baby, little Mere. It was three months
since Apiti had seen them. With the coming of
Danby, Apiti had been obliged to leave the
kaingas, which had given him temporary sanctuary,
and take to the bush. Twenty miles to the south-
ward of Mono Bay was the kainga of the
Ruarangis, which sheltered Marama and little
Mere. Somewhere between those two places was
Danby the Sleuth. This Apiti knew. Two years
before Apiti, then a peaceful dweller in the land
of his people and a mighty shearer of the palceha’s
sheep, had first seen Marama. Flushed with
success and flush of money, Apiti was good to look
upon, and Marama—well, she was Marama the
Moon-child, Only Haere Tutiki, the half-caste
trooper who had marked Marama down as his
own, objected. Perhaps his objection fanned the
flame of passion. At the end of a three days’
spree in Gisborne, Apiti, his blood afire with
waipiro,, had heard a story of trouble at the kainga
—trouble between Marama and Haere Tutiki. It
was then that he took another man’s horse and
rricie hell-for-leather to the kainga, only to find
the story false. Apiti was a horse-thief. The
respectable daily papers were short of copy, so
they outlawed him.
Apiti, with a tryst to keep, crouched at the
water s edge and waited. Two days before, Tame,
the Thin One, evading the searchers in the bush,
had found Apiti in the old whare at Mono Bay.
And Tame was the bearer of bad tidings. Winter,
the bete noir of the brown people, had fastened his
relentless fangs in little Mere. Apiti had heard
the news stolidly, with indifference, seemingly;
but he was going back. It was useless for Tame
to point out the danger, the vigilance of Haere
Tutijki, the cleverness of Danby the Great.
The creak of oars in rowlocks broke the silence.
A boat with two figures in it loomed up and
the beach. Apiti pushed off and
climbed in. They pulled out of the bay, hardly
exchanging a greeting, and, catching the breeze,
hoisted sail. The boat sped southward.
Ttie kainga of the Ruaringas formed three sides
of a square, the south end being curtained off by
greejfi bush,; In the centre was a dusty compound
strewn wiih garbage and other insanitary matter.
Ganges drawn up by the shore end of a skeleton-
like 1 jetty; indiciited that the Ruarangis were
fishefr folkv Oyer everything hung the stench
of decaying fish.
Old Tamatea ran the boat alongside the jetty,
and! Apiti climbed out. An oar silenced the
‘dozen curs which yelped a greeting, and Apiti
saw, on-the far side of the compound, a light in
.Marama’s whare. The rest of the kainga was in
darkness. With fear in his heart, he walked
boldly across the dusty .space. Outside the door
he paused a second, then pushed it open and
stepped in. The whare, illuminated by three
candles, was empty./ To Apiti’s brain/ quickened
by fear, the emptiness spelled treachery; but
vyhose—Marama’s—Haere Tutiki’so-r ? With
a mighty spring he was through the door and
facing his enemies. In his hand was a heavy
manuka pole. He met the first charge valiantly,
and old Tamatea went down with a broken head.
They came again, and Apiti drove them back,
blind berserk. . The dust rose.in clouds, biting the
lungs of the combatants and obscuring one from
the other. Thrice he drove them back, and each
time someone' bit'‘the'dlist. The fourth time there
was only Tame and Haere Tutiki and Danby.
The smell of ancient conger was suffocating.
Apiti was close to the bush now, and Tame was
down. Apiti turned. Danby’s revolver spoke
The outlaw stiffened a moment,
them tumbled forward, loosely. Over the kainga
a great silence settled. The red blood was sucked
up. by the fast settling dust. And after that
Danby’s reputation rose in the daily papers till
-the-greatness of it blotted out the horizon.

Evasi.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THIS spot wherein the weeds abound
Is called unconsecrated ground—
Yet on it shines the sun as fair
As on yon sanctified parterre.
And softly falls the gentle rain
Upon these graves of men self-slain,
As on the blessed quilt of sward
Of those who perished in the Lord.
Come good, come bad, come great or small,
The kindly earth cares nought at all—
She has no feud with Live or Dead,
She keeps for each an equal bed.
And Mother Nature, frank and free,
Is full of strangest irony,
And from the ashes of a Pope
May grow a hangman and his rope.
The proud Archbishop, on whose fine,
Eat fingers white a ring doth shine,
May, some day, with his amethyst.
Become a howling Atheist.
The old Egyptians knew right well
This irony, and would compel
The Grey Ape Time to lift the lids
Of Everlasting Pyramids.
In Boulak, in a case of state,
Stands Rameses, whom men called Great ;
But what Museum has in store
The frame of William Conqueror?
God made, and may His worlds destroy,
But Helen—Helena of Troy !
His fairest work He dare not mar,
And of her dust He made a star.
And if He had no larger scope
The promise keeps, and keeps the hope
That He will, in His gracious way,
Our enemies—God bless them !—slay.
The bitter laurels brought the scene
Again. A god he must have been
Who stood amidst that coward clan
Erect. A god ? By God, a Man !
I spoke to him—and then said He,
“ I know —I died at Thirty-Three.
Then came the Cross which ye adore. . .
I trembled on the Mount before. ”
I saw a cripple half-alive,
Yet blithe and keen at sixty-five.
“ What could that youngster know? ” said he,
“ He chucked the game at Thirty-Three,”
“Evasi!” It was well to say,
-Yet, when we meet on Judgment Day,
You’ll lightly throw a rose to me,
I shall not miss it. .. you shall see.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
Jobling’s Topometer.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
PEOPLE who have travelled much at
night by train will have realised the want
which Jobling tried to fill with his top-
ometer.
It was designed on similar lines to a cyclometer,
and was intended to show exactly where a train
was at any given moment. One machine was to
be fitted in each carriage, so that a drowsy pas-
senger for, say, Ashburton, could, by lifting his
eyes to the dial, read that Rakaia, say, was the
next station, so that he would be safe in sleeping
for another half-hour.
The topometer was worked from a sprocket
wheel on the axle. The model Jobling had in his
office was very neat and compact, and Jobling was
most serious about its capabilities.
He made it to suit the requirements of the Christ-
church-Dunedin express run, and was constantly
interviewing the railway authorities, urging them
to have it 'installed on a trial trip.
I often amused myself, when I visited Jobling’s
office, by turning the wheel and seeing the dif-
ferent names appear on the dial. At every 10
miles a bell rang. Supposing I started it from
Christchurch ; after-1 had turned it one mile, the
words “ Addington—don’t stop ! ” sprang into the
space marked “ Next station.” The instrument
always gave the name of the next station when
the train was one mile from the last one.
Eventually Jobling obtained permission to
instal a machine in the mail-car on the express,
and his delight was positively childish. His cal-
culations proved correct, and the machine was of
great service to the mail sorters.
On the return trip the instrument revolved in a
reverse direction, and showed considerable judg-
ment in giving names to the stations.
Jobling was triumphant.
I said, “ Put not your trust in machinery.”
In reply he showed me a letter from the rail-
ways ordering two dozen topometers for use on
the mail trains between Christchurch and Dun-
edin. Despite my scepticism, I began to think
that, with care, this thing might prove a gold-
mine to Jobling. That was in September.
Travellers became interested in the topometer,
and relied on it to apprise them of the names of
stations, paying no attention to the porters’
unintelligible cries; and the name and fame of
Jobling were a by-word in the land.
It was the Christmas traffic that brought woe
to Jobling.
Long trains pulled out of Christchurch and
Dunedin, and there were some carriages which
were not fitted with Jobling’s topometer. But it
was arranged that every second car should contain
one.
I left Christchurch by the 11 p.m. train on
Christmas Eve for Dunedin. I forgot to notice if
a topometer was attached to my carriage till we
were nearing Rakaia. On looking round I saw
there was one, and it was informing travellers
brazenly that the next station was Timaru.
Timaru was 64 miles distant, due south.
After we left Rakaia I watched the dial. It
said, “ Norman by—don’t stop.”
The next station was Chertsey, and we did
stop. I began to wonder what the other machines
were doing.
I found the one in the car ahead of mine just
running us into Dunedin. It was so palpably
wrong no one noticed it, and when it reached
Dunedin and started from Christchurch again, no
one was surprised.
Further ahead a topometer recorded the fact
that the next station was Oamaru —it bore the
legend “ Running North.”
We were running south. It turned out that this
car had been shunted on a Y switch, and had been
turned in the process. Presumably, the porters
were too busy to adjust the instrument.
As I stood watching this meter an American
approached.
“ Pretty cute affairs,” he remarked.
I agreed.
“ But Christmas don’t seem to agree too well
with them,” he said.
“Seems not,” I replied.
“I guess not. According to these machines,
this is the longest train I ever was on. The front
car is near Dunedin, and the last one is just leaving
Addington.”
I went next into a second smoker. When I opened
the door a babel of sounds reached my ears. A
stout German was just completing the mutilation
of Jobling’s topometer.
“I vos asleep,” he explained to me, “ und I
voke und look to see vat time my station come.
I vant to go to Ashburton, and that dam clock
say ‘Rakaia nex’ station.’ So I vant to sleep
again, und now ve are at Vinchester —t’irty miles
past Ashburton.”
Matters were becoming serious.
At Timaru I saw a young fellow and his girl
pacing the station platform, arm in arm, most
affectionately. Later on at Studholme Junction
I saw the same man rushing wildly along the plat-
form, evidently greatly perturbed.
“ Anything wrong ? ” I asked.
“Look here,” he said, “is this a blessed night-
mare or not? ”
“ 1 hope not,” I replied.
“ This is Palmerston, ain’t it ?”
“ No. Palmerston’s 60 miles ahead.”
“ Thank God ! ” he breathed.
Our engine whistled.
“Get on here and tell me,” I suggested.
He came.
“It was this way. You see my girl and myself
are going to Maheno to spend Christmas with her
people. After we left Timaru we both went
to sleep, and when I awoke that thigammy-bob on
the wall said the next station was Palmerston.
It knocked me sideways to think we had passed
our station. But I didn’t waken her. Glad I
didn’t. I’ll go and tell her now.”
He went; but he returned presently.
“ She’s gone,” he gasped.
“What?”
“Got off here, thinking it was Palmerston, I
suppose. Well, she has friends at Studholme,
and I’ll wire her from Waitaki. I wonder if I’m
mad, or what ? ”
I did my best to reassure him, telling him there
would be a train in the morning.
Then I started back to my car. As I passed a
bird-cage compartment, someone rapped on the
window. 1 looked in and saw Jobling !
“You?” I cried. “I thought-.you were at
Waimate. Where are you bound for ?”
“ Christchurch.”
“Hell !” I ejaculated, forgetting myself.
“Possibly, later. At present, Christchurch.”
I should explain that at Studholme the north
and south through trains meet, and the train from
the Waimate branch meets them both, and returns
to Waimate.
“ You’re on the wrong train,” I explained.
He pointed triumphantly to his topometer. It
read :
NEXT STATION,
Hook.
Don’t stop.
Jobling had, by a strange chance, got into the
carriage which had been reversed in shunting.
In his hurry he had taken the wrong train. Any
doubt he may have had was dispelled on looking
at his invention, which showed the legend,
“Running North.”
Jobling was angry when I explained the state
of chaos his instruments were causing.
“ Train hands dam’ fools. Soon set them right.”
He moved a lever and set the machine running
south. Then he visited each car and adjusted
them all.
The second smoker, before mentioned, he took
last. His face, when he saw the battered instru-
ment, was a study.
“Who did that?”
The fat German, who had stayed on the train
and was now pugilistically drunk, claimed the
honor.
Words followed, then higher words, finally
blows, from which Jobling emerged triumphant
but bleeding.
Despite the fact that he was being whirled far-
ther and farther from home and Christmas, he
was soon his old jaunty self again.
“After all, I’m glad I took the wrong train,”
he said. “Exceedingly glad.”
“ You always were optimistic,” I replied.
But even his optimism could not prevail against
the carelessness or stupidity of a certain mail
clerk.
It was in the winter, when the trains reach
their destinations in the dark. This man had
become accustomed to rely on the topometer.
Jobling says it was the man’s fault; the man
says it was the fault of the machine. I am
inclined to agree with the man.
At any rate, after Palmerston, no station save
those at which the train stopped got a mail on
this particular occasion.
It was discovered at the inquiry that the Sea-
cliff mail was.thrown into the Waitati Stream, and
that the Waitati bags had been heaved over the
Puketeraki cliffs. Other bags had flown off at
equally absurd places.
As the topometer, when examined at Dunedin,
showed that the train was then at Hornby Junc-
tion, near Christchurch, the clerk was not dis-
missed, though he was warned to see in future
that the mailbags were thrown at something
solid.
Jobling says the instrument was tampered with.
That is what I expected Jobling to say.
WILL LAWSON.
